==PAGE:1==
 i
The Super Cool 
Analysis Tool
Development of an analysis 
tool for theory inclusion in 
behavioural design projects 
Beatrice Angel 
Nanna Kristiansen
35 ECTS Master Thesis – DTU July 2017
Supervisor: Philip Cash - Associate Professor DTU 
==PAGE:2==
ii
English title:  The Super Cool Analysis Tool - Development of an analysis tool for theory inclusion in 
behavioural design projects
Danish title:  Det Super Seje Analyseværktøj - Udvikling af et analyseværktøj til at integrere teorier i 
adfærdsmæssige designprojekter
Project: Master Thesis in M.Sc. Design & Innovation
Hand-in date: 2nd of July 2017
ECTS points: 35
Institution: Technical University of Denmark 
Project description: This thesis combines literature and current industry practice to develop a support tool 
for behavioural design projects. Literature acknowledge the need for figuring out how to connect a behavioural 
problem with the most suitable behavioural theories, as well as the need for understanding and applying 
behavioural theories in the correct context. Current practice in industry acknowledge the need to build a 
theoretical back-catalogue to increase behavioural design capabilities and take advantage of theory inclusion in 
behavioural design projects. This leads to the development of an analytical behavioural design support tool that 
is able to meet the above-mentioned needs by providing a five step framework for problem analysis and theory 
inclusion.
Preface
Beatrice Angel
Student number: s123678
Stud.MSc.Eng. Design & Innovation
Technical University of Denmark
Nanna Kristiansen
Student number: s123420
Stud.MSc.Eng. Design & Innovation
Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:3==
 iii
Many of the biggest challenges today are caused by human behaviour. This realisation is 
supported by the emerging field of Behavioural Design, in which designers, through applying 
a combination of behavioural theories and behavioural design approaches, aim to design 
solutions to cope with these challenges. Despite the fact that theory driven design leads 
to more successful behaviour change interventions, a gap exists between the realisation of 
the importance of integrative thinking of behavioural theories and the explicit visualisation 
of this knowledge in practical application methods. An investigation of current practice in 
five design consultancies revealed two different needs due to this gap: 1. Designers with 
limited experience of behavioural design need easy ways of achieving a theoretical back-
catalogue; and 2. Designers with comprehensive experience of behavioural design need 
ways of challenging their current tunnel vision in regard to a multifaceted combination of 
behavioural theories. Hence, by bridging findings from theory and practice, through different 
design activities, a support tool is developed: the Super Cool Analysis Tool. The Super Cool 
Analysis Tool is a gamified analysis support tool for the early stages of a behavioural design 
project that through five steps helps designers and other professions in a behavioural design 
project team in making a thorough problem behaviour analysis by:
• providing a structured overview of behavioural theories and their relations with 
respect to considering all perspectives of behavioural design problems.
• enabling better validation of the analysis and ensuring translatable documentation 
along the analysis steps.
The Super Cool Analysis Tool is developed from a design point of view where it is desired 
to transition from a comprehensive problem understanding to a solution and thus the tool 
contributes with: A detailed visualisation of problem behaviour influences, a theoretical framework 
for increasing integrative thinking of behavioural theories and a forced theory reflection, and an 
overview of the theories’ interaction. This thesis opens up for further research on exploring 
and studying the importance and significance of applying integrative thinking of behavioural 
theories, as well as establishing evidence of the effects that different problem perspectives 
have on behaviour. Additionally, for designers this thesis opens up for the realisation that a 
more thorough problem analysis combined with an open approach and integrative thinking 
of behavioural theories will increase the probability of developing successful solutions in 
behavioural design projects.
Abstract
==PAGE:4==
iv
A student successfully completing a Master Thesis shall:
1. be able to apply current engineering background to investigate, understand and 
reflect upon behavioural design literature concerning tools, methods, and theories 
within this field.
2. be able to formulate clear research aims and objectives, based on an identified 
relevant scientific problem in the context of behavioural design.
3. be able to identify, retrieve, and apply leading international research within the 
context of behavioural design to make a systematic literature review for framing 
and guiding the project.
4. be able to deconstruct scientific problems within the field of behavioural design 
through explanation, analysis, and discussion.
5. be able to select and apply relevant design tools and methods to support the aim 
of the project and its process.
6. be able to apply current engineering design background to ideate, conceptualise, 
and develop a support tool for behavioural design.
7. be able to formulate and visually set up a guide showing how to classify chosen 
behavioural design theories.
8. be able to critically assess knowledge in the professional field – through a continuous 
and dynamic collaborative process both with internal and external partners.
9. be able to plan, structure, and manage a master project with the use of milestones, 
to handle complexity and unforeseen events that may require new solution models.
10. be able to communicate the project clearly in writing, visually, and orally, as well as 
in an easy and understandable manner with proper documentation and references.
11. be able to adjust the staging of the project based on assessment of magnitude of 
the task, project complexity, and the desired result.

==PAGE:6==
vi
Behavioural design
“A specific type of design where designers seek to develop products and/or systems that influence 
human behaviour through interventions” (Nielsen & Aakerlund 2016, p.vi; Hartlev & Durazo 
2015, p.11).
Behavioural design method
Designed to support behavioural design activities through goal-oriented rationalisation and 
simplification of analysing and targeting behaviours.
Behavioural design situation
A behavioural design situation includes a problem related to a type of behaviour of individual(s), 
behavioural theories describing and analysing this behaviour and design approaches to 
propose an effective solution to the problem.
Behavioural science
A combination of behavioural economics, cognitive neuroscience and proven experiments, 
conducted by behavioural scientists around the world (Briefcase 2017).
Behavioural theories
An overall term for the theoretical constitution of behavioural design, which is specifically 
composed of models of behaviour and theory of change (Prager 2012).
Cognitive psychology
A branch of psychology that studies mental processes, i.e. how humans process information, 
which includes terms such as attention, memory, problem solving, and thinking (Gerrig & 
Zimbardo 2002).
Designer
A professional with design background whose professionalism combines socio-technical 
perspectives with technical product and/or service development skills and possesses the 
capabilities of proceeding successfully through all phases of a design process.
Design approach
An overall term for the practical constitution of design, specifically composed of design 
frameworks, design processes, design methods, and design tools.
Design framework
A coherent set of concepts and relationships posited about some phenomena (Bates & 
Zhong 2014) which supports and guides the designer in a given direction.
Design method
A goal-oriented rationalisation or simplification of engineering work in the form of a 
standardised work description for monitoring the design process and solving design sub-tasks. 
Design methods create a staging for creative activities or structured search, classification, 
discussion, evaluation, and decision-making (Andreasen et al. 2015). 
 
Nomenclature
==PAGE:7==
 vii
Design process
A composition of different design phases which refers to both the actual activities undertaken 
by the designer and the models used to describe these activities (Andreasen et al. 2015).
Design tool
A guide on how to apply models in certain contexts in order to complete development tasks 
(Andreasen et al. 2015). Design tools tend to be specific and practice-oriented (Niedderer 
et al. 2014).
Efficient behavioural design
Design interventions that changes the problem behaviour to a specific desired target 
behaviour as intended by the designer.
Engineering design company
A type of company that develops engineering solutions in regard to different kinds of product 
and/or service design. Among other things these types of companies are found in the fields 
of manufacturing or management.
Game
“A game is a system in which players engage in an artificial conflict, defined by rules, that results 
in a quantifiable outcome.” (Salen & Zimmerman 2014, p. 11)
Intervention
A behavioural design solution (in the form such as a product, an artefact, a system or a 
campaign) that addresses a specific behaviour either to enhance, reduce or remove it.
Model of behaviour
Focus on describing existing behaviour by explaining the determinant factors (e.g. intentions, 
attitudes, values) of underlying behaviour (Prager 2012). Models of behaviour tend to be 
linear (Darnton 2008b), as they describe behaviour when it occurs.
Support tool
A tool with the aim of positively influencing and helping a person with a certain task in a 
given context.
Theoretical back-catalogue
A theoretical foundation of behavioural theories obtained through years of experience.
Theory inclusion
Direct application of behavioural theories explicitly resulting in decisions, analysis or other 
obtained insights used in behavioural design activities. 
Theory of change
Focus on describing how to change one behaviour to another more desired behaviour (Prager 
2012) and thus theories of change support interventions for changing current behaviours or 
encouraging the adoption of new behaviours. Theories of change tend to be circular, as they

==PAGE:13==
 1
Chapter 1
Introduction
==PAGE:14==
2
In today’s society many of the major challenges around the world (e.g. financial crises, 
sustainability issues, immigration issues, effects of mass production and irresponsible consumer 
behaviour) could to a large extent be solved if humans would start changing their behaviours 
(Branson et al. 2012). The realisation that behaviour affects humans in all parts of the world 
is supported by the emerging field of Behavioural Design, in which designers aim to design 
solutions for solving these challenges. They do so with the knowledge that social and collective 
problems are affected by both the aspect of individual behaviour and attitudes as well as 
the aspect of social and collective behaviour and norms (Tromp 2013). This implies that an 
integrative thinking of both aspects is needed to understand behaviour properly. 
The two aspects of individual behaviour and social behaviour are derived from numerous 
behavioural theories constituting the theoretical foundation of behavioural design. Yet, while 
behavioural theories are the link to understanding behaviour, design is the link to changing 
behaviour. Due to the fact that theory driven design leads to more successful behaviour change 
interventions (Glanz & Bishop 2010; Michie et al. 2008; Henkemans et al. 2015), designers 
must be able to do both. However, existing behavioural design literature and approaches 
reveal a gap between the realised importance of integrative thinking of behavioural theories, 
and the explicit visualisation of this knowledge in practical approaches for application. The 
gap is subject to critique, as it prevents designers from expanding their behavioural design 
capabilities and from understanding the behavioural theories’ relations in depth, which would 
enable them in making better and more validated choices of theory inclusion.Therefore, as 
this thesis aims to impact the quality of behavioural design solutions, it sets out to develop a 
behavioural design support tool to address this gap and make it easier for designers to apply 
all the valuable perspectives offered by behavioural theories.
1.1 Aim of the thesis
1.2 Research aim & objectives
Based on the aim of the thesis, the following research aim and objectives are: 
Developing a support tool for designers aimed at providing a structured overview of behavioural 
theories and their relations, with respect to considering all perspectives of behavioural design 
problems and tailoring this overview to fit with existing behavioural design approaches.
Research objectives:
1. Obtain and map a structural understanding of the different core behavioural elements in 
theory and in design industry. 
2. Identify path dependencies and other interconnections from behavioural problem to 
behavioural design approach with respect to integrative thinking of behavioural theory.
3. Based on the two previous answers above, develop a tool for supporting designers in 
structuring their understanding of behavioural problems and in selecting appropriate 
behavioural theories.
4. Validate and evaluate the support tool.
==PAGE:15==
 3
1.3 Structure of the thesis
This thesis consists of eight chapters, of which chapter 1 sets out to introduce the importance 
and the aim of the thesis, as well as the research aim and objectives.
Chapter 2 investigates current behavioural design literature and outlines the theoretical 
framework and the research framework which forms the foundation of the thesis.
Chapter 3 presents the methodology of the thesis and the chosen approach for the study.
Chapter 4 presents the study of investigating current industry practice, describing both the 
method and the results of the study, which involves an analysis of current behavioural design 
practice based on five Danish design consultancies. From this, a determination of the core 
focus of the support tool is presented.
Chapter 5 presents the development of the support tool, which outlines the design process 
and methods applied, as well as describes the conceptualisation and iterations leading to the 
final tool concept: The Super Cool Analysis Tool.
Chapter 6 describes the Super Cool Analysis Tool in detail and three likely use cases 
associated with application of the tool.
Chapter 7 discusses the main contributions, implications and limitations of the Super Cool 
Analysis Tool and its development process, of which all parts are compared and contrasted 
with findings from both the Literature Review (chapter 2) and the Investigation of Current 
Practice (chapter 4).
Finally, this leads to chapter 8, which concludes the thesis and presents the needs and 
possibilities of further work.
On the following page (4) figure 1.1 visualises the structure and contents of the thesis.
1.3.1 Reading guide
The thesis is meant to be read sequentially from beginning (chapter 1) to end (chapter 8), 
yet associated appendices (chapter 11) are attached to the thesis, optionally offering the 
reader the possibility of going into depth and detail with the provided information, methods 
or figures. Moreover, chapters 9 and 10 includes a list of References and the Assignment A & 
B as part of the official thesis formalities of the Technical University of Denmark.

==PAGE:17==
 5
Chapter 2
Literature Review
==PAGE:18==
6
This chapter aims to explore current behavioural design literature and through this exploration 
investigate the core elements of behavioural design to identify and explore possible gaps.
To understand behavioural design, section 2.2 provides a description of behavioural design, its 
importance, core elements, and context of occurrence. 
Section 2.3 reviews several core elements of behavioural design. Current problem procedures 
in literature are identified and discussed in section 2.3.1, followed by section 2.3.2 that explores 
different psychological and sociological models of behaviour, developed to unravel the 
complexity of behaviour and identify factors that affect, influence and create human behaviour. 
Next, section 2.3.3 presents an exploration of the theories concerned with behaviour change, 
developed to support interventions designed for changing behaviour. Finally, section 2.3.4 
presents a review of existing behavioral design approaches.
Consequently, the research framework of the thesis is presented in section 2.4, along with its 
associated issues and a clarification of the concrete research aim and research objectives. This 
is followed by section 2.5 that presents staging considerations of the upcoming support tool 
development. 
2.1 Introduction to the Literature Review 
2.2 Behavioural design
Behavioural design is a relatively new field (that comes from behavioural science which was 
formed in the 1970s), with a theoretical foundation of combining theories from the fields 
of psychology, social neuroscience, cognitive science and economics (Datta & Mullainathan 
2012; Tantia 2017). Each of these fields are considerably wide and comprehensive with a large 
number of relating theories, thus rather complex themselves. As an example, psychologists 
have been investigating behaviour in a scientific way since the 1930s and these investigations 
have resulted in different psychological theories and large amounts of research (Nodder 
2013; Zachrisson & Boks 2012). Today the theories and research from these different fields 
are helpful for many different design disciplines, such as Design for X (e.g. Meerkamm & Koch 
2005), Participatory Design (e.g. Beyer & Holtzblatt 1998), and Behavioural Design (e.g. Cash et 
al. 2017). In behavioural design designers can be seen as the link between behavioural science 
and humans. As a consequence, designers have for the last decades tried to apply theoretical 
thoughts from behavioural science to practical product and/or system development (Tromp 
et al. 2011), in order to create solutions that effectively influence human behaviour in a way 
that improves society (Harford 2013). 
There are several elements important for designers to consider when performing and 
implementing behavioural design (Zachrisson & Boks 2012) in their current design practice. 
In this thesis five different core elements will briefly be elaborated on in the following sections 
(2.2.1-2.2.6) to give an understanding of what a desired Behavioural Design Situation (BDS) 
consists of.
==PAGE:19==
 7
The first core element to be considered in behavioural design is problem identification, 
definition, and understanding (Datta & Mullainathan 2012; Tantia 2017). Generally, at the start 
of every design process, the designer is faced with a well- or ill-defined problem which has 
to be turned into a well-defined solution (Cross 2008). Design problems usually originate 
from some form of a problem statement, normally called a design brief (Cross 2008). 
Design briefs can vary in content, depth, and details, and as a result, the nature of design 
problems means that analysing and understanding the problem is an influential part of the 
design process (Cross 2011). However, design problems can be multifaceted and e.g. include 
technical, infrastructural, contextual, and behavioural components. Crucial to understanding 
a design problem is therefore understanding the wider picture and all its components. Yet, 
as behavioural design primarily focuses on the behaviour aspect of a design problem, solely 
behavioural aspects of problem understanding is considered in this thesis. 
2.2.1 Understanding the problem
As behavioural design is concerned with influencing the behaviour of humans, another core 
element of behavioural design is understanding behaviour and why it occurs (Zachrisson & 
Boks 2012; Tantia 2017). Understanding the psychological processes and mechanisms (e.g. 
intentions) relating to a specific behaviour is crucial, as it forms the foundation for designing 
effective interventions (Darnton 2008b). Behavioural design’s theoretical foundation comes 
from multiple fields highlighted by Datta & Mullainathan (2012), yet in psychology two major 
categories exist: Individual factors and social factors. This is quite distinguishing for behavioural 
design, as it now more than ever deals with social issues or collective concerns, and due to the 
fact that individuals play an important role in realising and counteracting most social problems 
(Tromp 2013; Branson et al. 2012). Consequently, essential to understanding behaviour is 
understanding how the psychological processes of individual and social behaviour interact 
with each other.
From a design perspective, this understanding should be achieved through a practical approach 
of integrating different models of behaviour (Fogg 2003; Michie et al. 2008) in order to 
generate initial hypotheses of why certain behaviours occur (Tantia 2017). Many researchers 
have pointed out the importance of matching psychological processes with behaviour (Fogg 
& Hreha 2010) and for this purpose different descriptive social and/or cognitive psychological 
models have been developed. Effective integration of these models in the behavioural design 
process will enable better interventions (Darnton 2008a; Orji et al. 2013). Yet, to effectively 
integrate these models into the design, an open approach to model selection should be 
applied (i.e. understanding the broad range and then selecting) (Cash et al. 2017). In addition, 
effective integration should consist of a combination of understanding both key components 
of individual and social behaviour.
2.2.2 Understanding behaviour
==PAGE:20==
8
2.2.3 Understanding behaviour change
To develop effective behavioural design solutions (Michie et al. 2008), it is also necessary 
for the designer to understand how to actually change behaviour, once the basic behaviour 
is understood. One approach is to understand the way products can (or humans can be) 
influenced. Through the definition made by Tromp et al. (2011) influence is determined as 
four different types: Coercive, persuasive, seductive, and decisive. These four types can be used 
by the designer as a strategy for shaping the design and thus as a way of influencing humans to 
change behaviour. Another approach for understanding behaviour change, closely related to 
the above mentioned influence strategies, is the field of Persuasive Technology, also referred to 
as Persuasive Design. Persuasive technology is defined as technologies that aim to change the 
behaviour and attitude of humans (Fogg 2009a) without coercion or deception (Henkemans 
et al. 2015) as they are not considered to be positive persuasive elements (Wendel 2013). 
Thus, persuasive technology implies that the targeted humans are voluntarily participating 
in the behaviour change. This is a key element within persuasive technology in regards to 
design activities, as it limits the designer’s solution space to seduction or persuasion as possible 
design strategies. Hence, following the definition by Tromp et al., behavioural design can be 
seen as a broader field than persuasive technology, since humans can be both implicitly and 
explicitly influenced. Still, understanding how to influence humans is not the only aspect 
related to explaining how to change behaviour. There are several different prescriptive theories 
of change developed with the sole aim of explaining, while enabling, behaviour change. These 
theories relate to different aspects of changing behaviour, such as understanding the principles 
of behaviour, the strategy of change, the control of the persuasion, and the persuasion route. 
Together these form the key components of behaviour change and combined they prescribe 
different ways for the designer of applying and enabling behaviour change. 
2.2.4 Understanding design
Although the understanding of the different aspects of behaviour and behaviour change is 
crucial to behavioural design, so are the actual design activities. Humans have always designed 
things and therefore the world is full of tools, machines, artefacts, and many other things 
aimed at supporting design (Cross 2008). Several design approaches have been developed 
for different purposes within the design process, all of which aim to translate knowledge, 
creativity, or skills into visible and well-argued design decisions. In regards to behavioural 
design, the behavioural design approaches are vital for integrating theoretical knowledge into 
the actual design decisions. As the translation of theories into design activities (i.e. design 
approaches) should enable efficient behavioural design, the vitality of an integrative approach 
of theory and design practice is highlighted.
2.2.5 Solution
The last core element to briefly be elaborated on is naturally the last element of any design 
process: The solution. A solution can rarely stand alone and this is also distinguishing for 
==PAGE:21==
 9
2.2.6 Global assumptions of behavioural design 
Apart from the five core elements mentioned above there are other elements, such as context 
and ethics that are applicable and important to behavioural design. The context in which the 
behaviour occurs has a significant role in regards to how a human will experience an influence 
in comparison to the way that the designer anticipated (Tromp et al. 2011). For instance, 
context related factors could be time, money, and skills, all of which are external factors 
affecting behaviour (Ajzen 1991) that make behavioural design a complex task. However, the 
behavioural design core elements mentioned so far each requires an analysis and a consistent 
awareness of the context (Zachrisson & Boks 2012) to enable project success, which is why 
context is not considered as a core element itself. 
Moreover, ethics also contributes to making behavioural design a highly complex task. As briefly 
mentioned in section 2.2.3, persuasive technology has a lot of similarities with behavioural 
design and in terms of ethics it too offers valuable insights, as“the creators of a persuasive 
technology should never seek to persuade a person or persons of something they themselves 
would not consent to be persuaded to do” (Berdichevsky & Neuenschwander 1999, p.58). 
This statement is not only applicable for persuasive technology but for behavioural design 
as well, due to the fact that predicting certain influences (which is the main aim of most 
behavioural design projects) is just as difficult as predicting unintended outcomes. Hence, 
the use of ethical evaluation and analysis is crucial for the designer in trying to overcome 
these difficulties and minimise the magnitude of unintended outcomes. Together context 
and ethics can be considered as global assumptions of behavioural design as they are both 
vital to address in theory and design practice, to continuously adjust the design processes in 
accordance with possible implications and conflicts. 
As context and ethics in behavioural design are considered to be surrounding elements 
rather than core elements, they are not within the scope of this thesis and are therefore 
excluded subsequently (although acknowledged).
Based on the stated core elements of the previous sections (2.2.1-2.2.6) the desired BDS is 
in this thesis characterised as figure 2.1 (on the following page (10)). 
2.2.7 The Behavioural Design Situation
behavioural design, as the solution is viewed as an iterative process to systematically test ideas 
and prototypes in order to arrive at an intervention that enables a desired behaviour (Tantia 
2017). Despite the fact that theory driven design leads to successful interventions (Glanz & 
Bishop 2010; Michie et al. 2008; Henkemans et al. 2015), solutions are just as well affected by 
industry practice and real-world implications (further elaborated in section 4.3.4).
==PAGE:22==
10
2.3 Behavioural problems, theories and 
design approaches
What can be extracted from this is that in the desired BDS the designer needs to address 
models of behaviour, theories of change, and design approaches to effectively progress from 
problem behaviour to a solution that enables a desired behaviour. Consequently, as integrating 
and combining the five core elements is a rather complex task, the need to go into more 
depth and detail of behavioural problems, theories (models of behaviour and theories of 
change), and design approaches is emphasised. 
Figure 2.1
The Behavioural 
Design Situation
(BDS)
The following section aims to explore behavioural design in regards to behavioural problems, 
models of behaviour, theories of change and design approaches concerned with designing 
behaviour change interventions.
2.3.1 Behavioural problems
This part elaborates on understanding problems in a behavioural design context and exploring 
current procedures of behavioural problem identification. Generally, problem understanding is 
crucial for design, as the first step towards finding a solution is by identifying the root of the 
problem (Datta & Mullainathan 2012; Cross 2008; Andreasen et al. 2015). Hence, starting by 
asking why and who leads to a deeper understanding of the problem that identifies what, when, 
where and how (Prager 2012). Doing so will create a deeper understanding of the problem 
and its causes, which contributes to the foundation of finding a solution (Cross 2008). Yet, 
it is important to keep in mind that problem understanding evolves during the process of 
reaching the solution, and thus a continuous development of the understanding is necessary. 
Additionally, problem identification can be divided into two different aspects: goals and barriers, 
where goals describe what the problem is (e.g. “I want to..”) and barriers describe why the 
problem is present (e.g. “but..”) (SkillsYouNeed 2017). In regards to behavioural design, these 
barriers can take shape of psychological, social, and physical challenges (Ross & Ward 1995; 
Community Tool Box 2016b; Gigliotti & Jason 2012), and identifying them is crucial in any 
Problem
Behaviour
Models of 
Behaviour
Design
Approaches
Solution
Theories
of Change
==PAGE:23==
 11
behavioural design process, as they are the challenges that the designer has to overcome 
and solve with the designed solution. However, goals and barriers are not the only important 
aspects of problem identification, as the perspective through which a problem is viewed is 
just as essential for the designer to acknowledge and consider. 
2.3.1.1 Problem perspectives
Based on the description in section 2.2.1 design problems can consist of many different 
components such as technology, infrastructure, context and behaviour. These can be 
considered as different perspectives, through which a given problem can be analysed. In 
behavioural design literature there is an apparent distinction between individual concerns 
versus collective concerns (Tromp et al. 2011; Wendel 2014) and a distinction between 
problem behaviour caused by individual factors and behaviour caused by social factors 
(Darnton 2008b; Prager 2012). Hence, in this thesis the categorisation of behavioural problem 
perspectives is considered as: Individual, social, and time (Park et al. 2001). While being aware 
of other possible problem perspectives, only these three are considered to be relevant and 
thus elaborated on in this thesis, as mainly the individual and social perspectives are subject 
to in-depth exposition in behavioural design literature. Although the time perspective is not 
mentioned explicitly as an important aspect of problem understanding in behavioural design 
literature, it is reasonable to take the it into account, as it too has significant impact on a 
behavioural problem understanding. 
Individual perspective of problem behaviour
Individual behaviour is generally viewed through a psychological lens (Prager 2012) of personal 
problems and a physical lens of impersonal problems (SkillsYouNeed 2017; Schotte 1985):
• Personal problems relate to internal personal factors, such as health and psyche 
(figure 2.2).
• Impersonal problems relate to external personal factors, such as lack of money and 
time (figure 2.3).
Figure 2.2 (left) 
Personal problems 
Figure 2.3 (right) 
Impersonal problems
This way of distinguishing problems enables the designer to analyse behaviour in regards to 
terms such as intentions, attitudes and values, all of which are influenced by both internal and 
external personal factors. For instance, to encourage an individual to go see the dentist, it 
would be relevant to look at the current barriers, e.g. is it due to a lack of money (external 
physical personal factors) or due to fear (internal psychological personal factors)? These two 
lenses (psychological and physical) of individual problem behaviour adds two valuable layers 
to problem understanding in behavioural design. It offers a wide range of possibilities for 
==PAGE:24==
12
Social perspective of problem behaviour
Social behaviour is frequently viewed through the sociologic, anthropologic, economic or 
political lenses (Prager 2012) all of which refer to the perspective of social challenges, where 
problems can be distinguished as interpersonal problems and/or societal problems (Schotte 
1985; Park et al. 2001):
• Interpersonal problems relate to internal social factors, such as arguing with a friend 
or disagreeing with a study group, which can be seen as in-group behaviour (Tajfel 
& Turner 1979) (figure 2.4).
• Societal problems relate to external social factors, such as crime rates, littering, and 
pollution, which can be seen as out-group behaviour (Tajfel & Turner 1979) (figure 
2.5).
Figure 2.4 (left)
Interpersonal 
problems
Figure 2.5 (right)
Societal problems
This way of distinguishing problems enables the designer in analysing behaviour with respect to 
terms such as social norms, social impact as well as group behaviour. Humans are biologically 
and psychologically equipped to live in groups (Hossain & Ali 2014) and always basically try 
to copy each other’s behaviours in order to conserve energy and survive. Thus, to lower 
graffiti incidents it would for instance be relevant to investigate whether the problem is due 
to internal societal factors (such as gang conflicts) or due to external societal factors (such as 
already evident graffiti, i.e. negative existing choice-architecture). Problem behaviours caused 
by social behaviour can rarely stand alone, as society does not exist independently without 
individuals (Hossain & Ali 2014). Even though designers can distinguish between behaviour 
caused by individual factors and social factors, the two concepts always interact and cannot 
be excluded from one another. This elaboration of social problem behaviour adds another 
two valuable layers to problem understanding of behavioural design. 
the designer to extract the exact individual factors causing the problem behaviour, and thus 
enabling better possibilities of facilitating effective behaviour change solutions.
Time perspective of problem behaviour
Finally, in achieving a comprehensive problem understanding it is valuable to consider the time 
perspective of behaviour as well. The time perspective of behaviour is in this thesis viewed 
through the lenses story & background and frequency of change. 
• The story refers to the things prior to when the behaviour occurs, i.e. the bus was 
late, spilled coffee or a broken phone. The background refers to the mood, place in 
life, environment or childhood experiences of the individual.
==PAGE:25==
 13

Based on the clarifications in the previous section (2.3.1.1), behavioural design problem 
understanding and identification can be viewed as the following figure 2.6:

• The frequency of change refers to the span or frequency of the problem behaviour, 
i.e. is it a seldom behaviour or a frequent behaviour? (Fogg & Hreha 2010).
Although not mentioned explicitly in behavioural design literature, the time perspective has 
a significant impact on the obtained problem understanding, as it recognises behaviour as 
being agent-based (i.e. individuals have different stories and backgrounds). An individual may 
consider the story lens as causing the behaviour in the immediate situation, or may draw 
parallels to earlier situations (the background), as affecting the behaviour in the immediate 
situation. Additionally, the frequency of the behaviour (one time vs. frequent) affects the 
solution, as it has impact on e.g. the salience of the solution. Consequently, it is crucial to 
consider both the story, background, and frequency change as the top layers of a behavioural 
problem understanding.
• A comprehensive and useful problem analysis in behavioural design requires 
the designer to apply an integrative thinking of the individual perspective, social 
perspective together with the time perspective of problem behaviour. Hence, 
to do so one must focus on the correlation and interaction between individual 
problem layers (personal and impersonal), social problem layers (interpersonal 
and societal), and the time layers (story & background and frequency of change). 
Individually these six layers can be seen as means for identifying different factors 
that cause behavioural problems, yet combined they form a deeper understanding 
of the problem in total.
• This distinction of problem understanding may appear to be relevant to general 
design problems as well and not just behavioural problems. Yet, as behaviour is 
==PAGE:26==
14
complex and agent-based, understanding behavioural problems requires a greater 
and wider problem characterisation specifically regarding individual, social, and time 
factors. In contrast, in general design (e.g. redesign of a fountain) the problem is 
still complex, however it can often be measured and predicted and thus requires 
greater problem characterisation of other factors, such as technical or infrastructural 
factors.
This division of problems layers is valuable when trying to understand and identify a behavioural 
problem, yet a deeper theoretical understanding of behaviour and its determinants is equally 
valuable, and thus this is the next core element to explore in the BDS.
2.3.2 Models of behaviour
Numerous models of behaviour within the field of social and cognitive psychology have been 
developed to unravel the complexity of behaviour and identify factors that affect, influence, 
and create human behaviour (Zachrisson & Boks 2012). In this thesis 13 different descriptive 
models of behaviour are found relevant and will thus be explored and discussed in order 
to create a better understanding of this core element in the BDS. These are the main 
models of behaviour identified in behavioural design literature (through extensive systematic 
investigations done by authors such as Michie et al. (2008), Darnton (2008b) and Glanz & 
Bishop (2010)). The models of behaviour are divided into two categories:
Models of behaviour
Social factors of 
behaviour
Individual factors of 
behaviour
8
5
Figure 2.7
Categories 
of models of 
behaviour
This figure (2.7) identifies individual and social factors both describing behaviour and 
consequently, this section will identify what elements behaviour is shaped of and why humans 
behave as they do, i.e. the individual and social aspect of behavioural problem understanding 
(section 2.3.1.1).
2.3.2.1 Individual factors of behaviour
To understand the individual factors influencing and creating human behaviour eight different 
descriptive individual models of behaviour shaped by cognitive psychology will be explained 
in this section.
 
Bandura’s (1977) Theory of Self-efficacy explains that cognitive processes play a 
prominent role in the acquisition and retention of new behaviour patterns. The theory states 
that behaviour is determined by an individual’s efficacy expectations, defined as “the conviction 
that one can successfully execute the behaviour required to produce the outcomes” (Bandura 
==PAGE:27==
 15
1977, p.193). Thus expectations of personal efficacy determine whether a behaviour will 
be initiated, how much effort will be expended for this and how long it will be sustained 
for (Bandura 1977; Darnton 2008b). Furthermore, the theory states that besides efficacy 
expectations, outcome expectations also have an impact on behaviour. Individuals can believe 
that a particular course of action will produce certain outcomes, but if they hold serious 
doubts about whether they can perform the necessary activities or not, such information 
does not influence their behaviour. 
 
Similarly, Rosenstock’s (1974) Health Belief Model states that health related 
behaviour can be explained and predicted by focusing on the attitudes and beliefs of 
individuals (Behavioral Research Unit BRU 2002). This means that an individual’s belief, 
perceived benefits of action, and barriers to action, together with self-efficacy (Bandura 1977) 
explain engagement in health-promoting behaviours (Janz & Becker 1984; Rosenstock 1974). 
Even though it is a recognised framework for explaining and understanding health related 
decision making (Janz & Becker 1984) the Health Belief Model has also been used to analyse 
other types of behaviour, where explaining or predicting patterns of behaviours was essential 
(Morris et al. 2012). Both the Theory of Self-efficacy and the Health Belief Model are valuable 
for the designer in understanding the psychological processes of an individual’s personal 
issues to predict behaviour. Yet, as they are psychological models, they do not consider other 
factors such as environmental or economic factors. Neither do they incorporate the influence 
of social norms (Behavioral Research Unit BRU 2002). 
 
In the same way, Ajzen’s (1991) Theory of Planned Behaviour (TPB) can be 
applied in predicting behaviours of an individual. It is based upon his original Theory of 
Reasoned Actions, but without the original model’s limitations in dealing with behaviours over 
which human have incomplete volitional control (Ajzen 1991). Ajzen has adopted Bandura’s 
(1977) term of self-efficacy for the term perceived behavioural control (Darnton 2008b), which 
describes the perceived control an individual has over the opportunities and resources 
needed for an intended behaviour. It is much alike the term of self-efficacy, as perceived 
behavioural control too is the result of a deliberate calculation of how much effort should 
be expended for a specific behaviour (Darnton 2008b). However, whereas Bandura’s Theory 
of Self-efficacy uses expectations as the source for predicting behaviours, the TPB states 
that attitudes towards a behaviour together with subjective norms and perceived behavioural 
control all contribute to predicting intentions of different kinds (Ajzen 1991). And more so, 
these intentions, together with the perception of behavioural control, account for considerable 
variance in the actually performed behaviour. This combination of psychological processes has 
made the TPB very useful in predicting intentional (rational) behaviours (Piscicelli et al. 2016), 
such as health related behaviours (Armitage & Conner 1999).
 
In Triandis’ (1977) Theory of Interpersonal Behaviour (TIB) behaviour is not 
viewed as a solely deliberate process, due to the fact that habits are valued more influential 
than personal beliefs (Darnton 2008b). The TIB states that there are two routes which 
influences behaviour: intentions and habits. Intentions are the deliberate part (conscious 
thinking) of decision-making processes, whereas habits are the non-deliberate part 
==PAGE:28==
16
(nonconscious thinking) – both influenced by previous behaviours and experiences (Darnton 
2008b). Whereas the TPB (Ajzen 1991) states that intentions are created by attitude, social 
factors and perceived behavioural control, the TIB states that intentions are created by 
attitude, social factors, and affect, together with habits. Thus, the TIB includes the affective and 
habitual dimensions missing in the TPB (Piscicelli et al. 2016). The TPB relates variance of the 
performed behaviour to perceived behavioural control, while the TIB determines facilitating 
conditions as causing the variance. Facilitating conditions differs from perceived behavioural 
control as it describes the actual control (e.g. material circumstances) (Darnton 2008b), 
which is the factor that must allow for intentions to become real (Prager 2012). Despite their 
differences both the TPB and the TIB are valuable for the designer when trying to predict 
individuals’ behaviours. Yet, whereas the TPB aims to explain behaviouras caused by intentional 
decision-making, the TIB aims to explain behaviour caused by habitual decision-making. 
 
Contrary to the previous predictive models of behaviour, the Dual Process Theory 
focuses on understanding the decision-making processes of individuals. There are many 
variations of this theory, but one way of presenting it is Kahneman’s (2003) System Thinking. 
It describes how human minds have two different ways of processing information: System 1 
(nonconscious processing) and system 2 (conscious processing). The operations of system 
1 are fast, automatic, without effort, and governed by habits, which make them difficult to 
control. On the other hand, the operations of system 2 are slow, effortful, and controlled 
reactions using reflection and logic (Kahneman 2003). For a designer this means that, system 
1 behaviours are difficult to change as it relates to habits and routines, whereas system 
2 behaviours can change with persuasion or education, as they relate to intentions and 
rationality. 
 
In addition to the Dual Process Theory, Tversky & Kahneman (1974) have developed 
the Theory of Heuristics and Biases. This theory determines three different heuristics 
(representativeness, availability of instances, and adjustment from an anchor), all of which leads 
to different biases used for improving judgement and decisions of uncertainty. Since Tversky & 
Kahneman introduced the topic in 1974, several other cognitive and/or social biases have been 
developed, all of which provides suggestions for explaining behaviour in different contexts. 
These biases are highly applicable in behavioural design, as they add a level of reason to 
otherwise inexplicable behaviours, e.g. the bias of reactive devaluation: “the very act of offering 
a particular proposal or concession may diminish its apparent value or attractiveness in the eyes of 
the recipient” (Ross & Ward 1995, p. 270). In combination, the Dual Process Theory and the 
Theory of Heuristics and Biases offer disruptive ways of thinking about behaviour in relation 
to early behavioural economics, which assumes humans to be rational-thinking individuals 
(Investopedia 2017; Rosen 2015). However, the inclusion of non-conscious decision-making 
is currently more relevant than ever in behavioural design, since human behaviour is often 
nonconscious and controlled by habits and routines (Tromp 2013).
 
Duhigg’s (2014) theory How Habits Work adds another level to understanding 
behaviour with respect to non-conscious decision-making processes, as it is an attempt to 
==PAGE:29==
 17
visualise a basic way of diagnosing and shaping habits. The diagnosis consists of identifying 
the three parts that every habit loop contains: A cue, a routine, and a reward. By identifying 
these key elements in any habit, an understanding of the habit in question becomes available 
in terms of isolating the cue triggering a certain behaviour. As a result, the theory gives an 
understanding to the designer of how habits operate in order to gain the power over it and 
change behaviour. Even though the theory can be used to understand individuals’ behaviour 
in regards to habits, it has similarities with behavioural design approaches as it is concrete and 
includes a step-by-step guide on how to change a specific habit.
 
The last of the eight descriptive models of behaviour, the Self-determination Theory 
describes different types of motivation that influence behaviour (Deci & Ryan 2000). The 
theory distinguishes between intrinsic motivation and extrinsic motivation. Intrinsic motivation 
is propelled by an individual’s interest and enjoyment in the activity or task itself (Gagné & 
Deci 2005). Extrinsic motivation concerns activities that are not interesting and therefore an 
individual must be motivated externally, e.g. through strong coercion (Gagné & Deci 2005). 
Central to this theory is the distinction between autonomous motivation (non-conscious) 
and controlled motivation (conscious) (Gagné & Deci 2005). The theory offers a lot of value 
for the designer, as it provides insights and understanding of personal issues, i.e. how to 
extrinsically influence and motivate an individual towards a desired behaviour.
These eight individual models of behaviour emphasise behaviour from a cognitive point of 
view. When trying to understand the behaviour of an individual, factors of affective (e.g. 
emotions), normative (e.g. morals), and habitual (e.g. routines) dimensions are important 
to consider. For the designer this means that each of these descriptive models of behaviour 
(alone or combined) can be applicable when dealing with behavioural problems relating 
to habits or routines (nonconscious thinking), intentions (conscious thinking), attitudes and/or 
personal issues (self-efficacy, motivation) (see table 2.1 on the following page (18)).

2.3.2.2 Social factors of behaviour
In order to understand the social factors influencing an individual’s behaviour, as well as 
understanding the impact society has on individual decision-making processes, five different 
descriptive social models of behaviour shaped by social psychology will be explained in this 
section. 
 
Bandura’s (1986) Social Cognitive Theory explains human behaviour in three 
dynamic and continually interacting terms: cognitive and personal influences, environmental 
As different social or environmental issues often affect individual behaviour as well, it is also 
essential for the designer to investigate and understand social factors of behaviour.
==PAGE:31==
 19
influences and behavioural influences (Bandura 2001). A core premise of the theory is that 
an individual’s behaviour does not only come from personal experiences, but can also come 
from observing the actions of others and their resulting behavioural consequences (Glanz & 
Bishop 2010). Essential is the key element of reciprocal determinism, meaning that an individual 
can be a mean for change but also a responder to change (Bandura 1991; Glanz & Bishop 
2010). The theory distinguishes between three modes of human agency, through which given 
effects are produced: personal, proxy, and collective (Bandura 2001). Personal describes the 
cognitive, motivational and affective processes of an individual. Proxy describes the social 
conditions over which humans do not have direct control. Collective describes the collective 
processes, i.e. shared beliefs. This theory is important for a designer as it suggest that the 
environment plays a role in adopting healthy behaviours (Glanz & Bishop 2010). Additionally, 
it deepens the understanding of how different patterns of behaviour are created through 
environmental interaction (i.e. observations and agency). 
 
As an extension to the Social Cognitive Theory, Bandura (1991) developed the 
Social Cognitive Theory of Self-regulation. It states that human behaviour is extensively 
motivated and regulated by on-going self-influence and that most human behaviour happens 
due to some level of forethought, which happens through different levels of psychological 
sub functions (Bandura 1991): Self-monitoring of the individual’s behaviour and its effects, self-
guidance of the individual’s behaviour in relation to personal standards (and environment), and 
corrective self-reactions (Bandura 1991). However, what is important to notice is that, while 
these self-regulating systems are set by individuals, they can be based on the observations of 
others (Darnton 2008a). Thus, it is crucial to understand that the operations of these self-
regulations are affected by social interaction and not solely individually determined.
 
The core construct in the Social Cognitive Theory is central to Bronfenbrenner’s 
(1986) Social Ecological Model (Glanz & Bishop 2010), where the entire ecological system 
needs to be considered when trying to understand human behaviours. The model states that 
behaviour is affected by five different levels of influence that interact across levels: individual, 
interpersonal, organisational, community, and policy (Bronfenbrenner 1986). Similar to the Social 
Cognitive Theory, the Social Ecological Model emphasises that behaviour both shape and are 
shaped by the social environment (Glanz & Bishop 2010). Due to difficulties in articulation, 
application, and evaluation the model is one of the most often mentioned theoretical models 
not yet applied in research and practice (Glanz & Bishop 2010). Nonetheless, to understand 
social behavioural factors and their corresponding levels of relations, the description of the 
relationship between an individual and the individual’s social context is important for the 
designer to acknowledge in behavioural design. As this model of behaviour takes the entire 
ecological system into account, it relates to both social and environmental interaction.
 
Tromp et al.’s (2011) Socially Responsible Design Theory explains why socially 
desirable behaviour does not always align with personally desirable behaviour. Individuals 
often act in accordance with personal concerns, and thus in conflict with the collective concerns 
(Tromp 2013). The problem with acting on the basis of collective concerns often lies within 
the fact that what the individual gains or loses depends on the choices of other individuals 
==PAGE:32==
20
(i.e. society) and therefore a certain way of acting can become less attractive (Lilley 2007). 
This relates to the term of proxy agency in the Social Cognitive Theory (Bandura 1991). 
Additionally, the Socially Responsible Design Theory stresses the importance of design in 
regards to behavioural change, as it is the designer’s task to address the individual’s concerns 
through an intervention and thus elicit the desired social implication (Tromp et al. 2011). 
In other words, it is required that the designer understands the impact of the collective 
concerns as well as the more beneficial personal concerns in order to design for a desired 
social behaviour.
 
Another model of behaviour that offers valuable insights into the social factors of 
behaviour is Tajfel’s & Turner’s (1979) Social Identity Theory. It states that individuals, when 
interacting within a certain group context, acquire different social roles, or “levels of self”, 
which influences their behaviour. The theory distinguishes between interpersonal behaviour 
and intergroup behaviour. Interpersonal behaviour means two or more individuals acting 
solely on the grounds of individual concerns and characteristics, e.g. wife and husband. 
Intergroup behaviour being two or more individuals that behave solely on the grounds of 
social concerns and characteristics, e.g. soldiers in an army group (Tajfel & Turner 1979). 
Moreover, it distinguishes between being part of the in-group (us) and the out-group (them). 
What Tajfel and Turner realise is that individuals strive to achieve or maintain a positive social 
identity, and thus be part of the in-group (Tajfel & Turner 1979; Darnton 2008a). Whereas 
Socially Responsible Design Theory (Tromp et al. 2011) offers insights as to why individuals 
act on behalf of personal concerns, the Social Identity Theory is valuable as it offers insights 
as to why individuals act on behalf of (in-)group concerns and thus not on behalf of personal 
concerns. 
These five models of behaviour describe behaviour from a social point of view and try 
to distinguish the social factors that influence behaviour from the cognitive processes that 
influence behaviour. They emphasise the importance of distinguishing between personal 
influences and social influences, as these can be the cause of certain undesirable collective 
patterns of behaviour. For the designer this means that these five descriptive models of 
behaviour (alone or combined) can be applicable when trying to understand problems relating 
to social impact (social context having an impact on individual behaviour) and environmental 
interaction (environmental context having an impact on individual behaviour) (see table 2.2 
on the following page (21)).

2.3.2.3 Concluding remarks on models of behaviour 
Based on the exploration and discussion of the 13 selected descriptive models of behaviour 
in the previous sections (2.3.2.1 and 2.3.2.2), the following remarks can be concluded:
• Models of behaviour are essential as they identify what elements behaviour is 
shaped of and why certain behaviours occurs. Yet, an integrative thinking of both 
categories (individual behaviour and social behaviour) is essential, in order for the 
designer to fully understanding and map all important aspects of the behaviour in 
question, as individual and social behaviour are intertwined and rarely stands alone 
(Tromp et al.  2011).
• Both categories of models of behaviours are excellent in describing what elements 
behaviour consist of and the mechanics and processes that shapes it. However, 
as there are multiple models of behaviour in a complex and wide field, all of 
which can be analysed for individual behavioural design projects, an unintended 
complication of the theory inclusion occurs. Additionally, the models themselves 
show no practicality in regards to how to select the proper models of behaviour for 
inclusion, which consequently results in designers lacking ways of easily-obtaining a 
way of understanding how to apply them in the proper context. 
While these 13 models of behaviour are valuable in describing and understanding behaviour 
in itself, it is essential for the designer to investigate and understand behaviour change as well, 
and thus theories of change is the next core element to explore in the BDS.
==PAGE:34==
22
2.3.3 Theories of change
Whereas the previous section (2.3.2) presented 13 models of behaviour describing the 
individual and social factors affecting behaviour, this section describes eight prescriptive theories 
of change developed to support interventions designed for changing behaviour (Darnton 
2008b). As theories of change can relate to different aspects, such as time and control, the 
rationale for this selection of eight theories is to get a broad representation of several different 
types of theories of change.
2.3.3.1 Understanding the factors of behaviour change
In order to understand the underlying factors of behaviour change eight different theories of 
change and their relation to the aspects of how, when, and where to change behaviour will be 
explored in this section. 
 
In regards to realising how to change behaviour Cialdini’s (1984) Theory of Influence 
can be applied. The theory consists of six principles of persuasion: reciprocity (humans tend 
to return a favour), commitment and consistency (when humans commit, they do not back 
out), social proof (humans trust in things trusted by humans we trust); authority (humans tend 
to obey authorities), liking (humans are easily persuaded by human they like), and scarcity 
(relating to demand) (Cialdini 2007). The Theory of Influence is applicable in behavioural 
design, as the six principles offers suggestions on how to influence humans. The principles can 
be combined or they can stand alone, but most importantly they are open to some level of 
interpretation in regards to enabling a behaviour change.
 
Another theory that offers insights with respect to enabling a behaviour change is 
Fogg’s (2009b) Behaviour Model. It states that three factors must occur at the same time: 
motivation (the individual has to be sufficiently motivated), ability (the individual has to have 
the ability to perform the behaviour), and triggers (the individual must be triggered to perform 
the behaviour). Fogg (2009b) values the trigger as being the most important factor, as the 
designer must create the right trigger at the right time, otherwise it will be difficult to achieve 
the desired behaviour. Successful triggers have three characteristics: They are noticed, they are 
associated with the target behaviour, and they happen when the person is motivated and able to 
perform the behaviour. The Behaviour Model differs from Cialdini’s (1984) Theory of Influence, 
as it states the factors that must be influenced to create a specific behaviour (i.e. Cialdini’s 
six principles are applicable within each factor). However, both theories relate to the aspect 
of persuasion principles and together they provide a good understanding of how to enable a 
behaviour change.
 
In regards to realising when to enable a of change behaviour the AIDS Risk 
Reduction Model (ARRM) (Catania et al. 1990) can be applied for predicting and explaining 
behaviour change efforts of individuals, and was specifically made for the health domain of 
HIV and AIDS patients (thus the name). It consists of a three stage model that determines the 
stages of behaviour efforts: Recognition and labelling, making a commitment, and taking action 
(Behavioral Research Unit BRU 2002). For each stage several factors has been determined, 
which are hypothesised influences that aim at making an individual successfully complete a 
==PAGE:35==
 23
stage. While being developed for the aim of predicting specific health behaviours, the ARRM 
is a great source of inspiration in regards to realising the change stages an individual can be at.
 
A theory of change that offers further elaboration in regards to understanding the 
stages of change is the Transtheoretical Model, also known as the Stages of Change Model 
(Prochaska & Velicer 1997; Darnton 2008b). In comparison with the ARRM (Catania et al. 
1990) The Transtheoretical Model describes the process of intentional behaviour change 
through five stages of change: Precontemplation (no recognition of need for or interest in 
change), contemplation (thinking about changing), preparation for action (planning for changing), 
action (adopting new habits), and maintenance (on-going practice of new behaviour) 
(Prochaska et al. 1992; Glanz & Bishop 2010). It differs from the ARRM as it also considers 
the stages before commitment and preparation of change and after taking action. The model 
assumes that, when changing behaviour, an individual will rarely go through the stages as a 
linear progression but instead relapse (Prochaska et al. 1992). Additionally it adopts Bandura’s 
(1977) term of self-efficacy as it considers the individual’s own perception of progress with 
change (Darnton 2008b). Simultaneously it only deals with conscious thinking (system 2 in 
Dual Process Theory), which at the same time is the theory’s limitation. The use of both the 
ARRM and the Transtheoretical Model is limited, as they do not take the social context in 
which change occurs into account (Behavioral Research Unit BRU 2002). Both theories are, 
however, valuable in behavioural design as they deepen the understanding of how individuals 
perceive change and when to time influences in order to design successful interventions.
 
When realising where to change behaviour, Lilley’s (2009) Loughborough Model can 
be applied. It is a theory of change developed for designing sustainable interventions (Lilley 
2009), which states that the power of decision-making is determined by an axis of influence 
that ranges from the user agentive to the product agentive (Wilson et al. 2010). The theory 
distinguishes between three ways of staging a change: Guiding a change (enabling control of 
the change to the user, e.g. eco-feedback), steering a change (controlling the user’s interaction 
through affordances and constraints, e.g. behaviour steering), and ensuring change (e.g. forcing 
action) (Lilley 2009; Wilson et al. 2010). Additionally, Lilley points out that the influence of 
these interventions should range from being passive (informative), to assertive (persuasive), to 
aggressive (coercive) (Lilley 2009; Wilson et al. 2010). This relates well to Tromp et al.’s (2011) 
salience versus force dimensions of influence, where four types of influence is introduced: 
persuasive, coercive, decisive, and seductive (section 2.2.3). What Tromp et al. (2011) notice 
about these four types is that it is hardly ever possible for a designer to assign a product to 
a certain category of influence, while having the user perceive it in the same way. However 
despite the fact that it is difficult to predict a specific type of influence, these seven types of 
influence (four by Tromp et al., three by Lilley) create an understanding of the areas where a 
change of behaviour can be enabled and how the intervention should be perceived.
 
By expanding the Loughborough Model Tang (2010) created the Design Behaviour 
Intervention Model (Bhamra et al. 2011), which combines Triandis’ (1977) TIB with Lilley’s 
(2009) Loughborough Model. It states that change can happen in three different stages: 
Declarative, knowledge compilation, and procedural (Wilson et al. 2010; Bhamra et al. 2011). 
In the declarative stage an individual relates to intentions, where the individual is aware and 
==PAGE:36==
24
considerate of his/hers actions and more so in control of the influence, thus relating to the 
stage of guiding a change. In the knowledge compilation stage an individual relates to habits, 
where the individual forms and performs habits and therefore the individual is in the middle 
of complete control and no control of the influence, thus relating to the stage of maintaining a 
change. In the last stage, the procedural stage, the product/system is in complete control of the 
influence, and therefore the force of changing behaviour of an individual is strong, thus relating 
the stage to forcing the change. While the Design Behaviour Intervention Model is aimed at 
problems relating to sustainability (Tang 2010), it can be applied for problems with other 
focuses as well. The model is valuable for the designer as it links different cognitive processes 
to behaviour change stages, which enables a greater link between models of behaviour and 
theories of change, i.e. integrative thinking in the BDS.
 
The Elaboration Likelihood Model by Petty & Cacioppo (1986) also contributes 
to the where aspect of changing behaviour as it provides a framework for explaining how 
attitudes are shaped, formed, and reinforced by persuasive communications. The idea is that 
behaviour is guided by attitude, which can be influenced by persuasion. Thus, when an individual 
is presented with information, levels of “elaboration” occur, which means the effort the 
individual makes to accept/reject the message. Similar to Kahneman’s (2003) System Thinking 
the model describes two types of processing routes: central and peripheral processing routes 
(Darnton 2008b). The central process, is when the individual through conscious consideration 
(system 2), takes a central route to decision-making, which can lead to permanent change 
in attitude (Petty & Cacioppo 1986). The peripheral process, is when the individual only 
temporary changes attitude, as persuasion is on a superficial level and thus this process is 
less conscious (system 1) (Darnton 2008b). An individual’s level of motivation and ability 
determines the balance between the two routes, yet it can also be influenced by the context 
and the message (Darnton 2008b) and, furthermore, it can be influenced by the placement of 
the intervention. The model is related to principles of behaviour, as it determines clear ways 
of changing behaviour through persuasive communications.
 
Even though behaviour change can take place through both the central and the 
peripheral route, Kaptein et al. (2015) argues that persuasion is primarily effective through 
peripheral processing. Thus, a theory merely concerned with the peripheral route is Thaler’s 
and Sunstein’s (2009) Nudging, which is defined as an aspect of choice architecture that 
alter humans behaviour in a predictable way, without limiting human’s choice of freedom 
and without using economic incentives (Thaler & Sunstein 2009; Branson et al. 2012). Similar 
to the Elaboration Likelihood Model, Nudging also relates to Kahneman’s (2003) System 
Thinking, as Nudging only intends to change the behaviour of humans through influencing the 
subconscious part of the human decision-making processes (i.e. automatic behaviour, system 
1) (Kahneman 2003; Hartlev & Durazo 2015). Nudging is particularly well-suited to complex 
decisions and with a lot of different alternatives to choose from (Arvai 2016). However, 
when trying to solve some of the biggest problems that society faces, nudging is not enough 
(Burmester 2013). This is due to the fact that nudging is likely to only lead to temporary and 
superficial behaviour change (Prager 2012). However this is very valuable in regards to public 
interventions and thus a theory that is highly applicable in this context.
==PAGE:37==
 25
From a comparison of these eight theories of change it is possible to distinguish between 
theories that prescribe how to enable a change, when to enable a change, and where to enable 
a change. Additionally, this means for the designer that these theories of change (alone or 
combined) can be applicable in understanding behaviour principles, change strategies, control 
of persuasion, and/or direction of persuasion (see table 2.3). This distinction is valuable for a 
designer, as it will create a better and wider foundation and include the relevant aspects of 
theories of change in the BDS.

Simultaneous application of theories of change together with models of behaviour enables the 
designer in creating the best possible theoretical foundation for the BDS, due to the fact that 
understanding what behaviour is and why it occurs, leads to realising how, when, and where to 
change it. Ultimately, this realisation in the BDS is essential to develop successful interventions.
