2.3.3.2 Concluding remarks on theories of change
Based on the exploration and discussion of the eight selected prescriptive theories of change 
in the previous section (2.3.3.1), the following remarks can be concluded:
• Understanding the different aspects of theories of change is crucial for the designer 
in order to enable an actual and successful change of behaviour, as they offer 
value for the designer in regards to behavioural mapping and allow for better 
possibilities of predicting behaviour. However, although the selected theories of 
change are prescriptive, they show little explicit guidance on how to exactly design 
for such a desired change (little direct relation to design activities). Furthermore, 
since behaviour happens through both conscious and nonconscious processing 
(Kahneman 2003; Petty & Cacioppo 1986), predicting the behaviour of an individual 
is a complex task, and thus theory inclusion in general (i.e. inclusion of both models 
of behaviour and theories of change) should not be underestimated.
• Combining the theories of change with the understanding derived from different 
models of behaviour, will create a more comprehensive foundation of understanding 
the complexity of behaviour and thus possibly lowering the difficulties of designing 
for behaviour change. Yet, the exploration of behavioural literature in this thesis 
have shown that few researchers in behavioural design have dealt with exploring 
and determining the importance and impact that an enablement of said integrative 
thinking will have on the behavioural design field. This leads to the realisation that 
current research to some extent underestimates, or at least does not visually 
communicate, the importance of integrative thinking of behavioural theories 
(models of behaviour and theories of change) to designers wishing to include 
them in the BDS. 
While this is a conclusion from an exploration of different behavioural theories, further 
measures must be taken to examine whether an exploration of the practical behavioural 
design approaches leads to the same conclusion.
2.3.4 Behavioural design approaches
Whereas behavioural theories are helpful for providing a basic understanding of behaviour 
and behaviour change, they offer little guidance on how to do it in practice (Michie et al. 
2008), as they are naturally theory focused. How to practically enable behavioural design is 
instead fulfilled by different behavioural design approaches that have been developed for the 
sole purpose of aiding the BDS. In this thesis, 15 different behavioural design approaches, 
found to be of relevance, will be described and discussed. The rationale of this selection is to 
include different categories of design approaches as well as having different types within each 
category. The four main categories are presented in figure 2.8 (on the following page (27)):
==PAGE:39==
 27
The aim of this division is to identify the strengths and shortcomings of the different types of 
design approaches, i.e. distinguishing how the different approaches vary in aiding the designer 
in the BDS. Moreover, the link between the different behavioural design approaches and their 
theoretical foundation will be addressed to obtain greater understanding of the elements of 
the BDS.
2.3.4.1 Framework
 
The Functional Triad made by Fogg (2003) is a framework for thinking about the 
roles that interactive technologies can play from the perspective of the individual. Persuasive 
technology is seen as an interactive product (Fogg 2003) designed to change attitudes, 
behaviours, or both, and can apply three different roles for making a desired outcome easier 
to achieve: A tool, a social actor, and a medium. Furthermore, the framework defines seven 
types of persuasive technology means: Reduction, tunneling, tailoring, suggestions, self-monitoring, 
surveillance, and conditioning. Using the Functional Triad as a design approach applies to the 
category of theories of change, however a weakness of this framework is that it does not 
explain how the principles can be transferred into actual design activities (Oinas-Kukkonen & 
Harjumaa 2008).
 
This is instead explained by the Systematic Framework made by Oinas-Kukkonen 
& Harjumaa (2008), which is a systematic framework built from a design perspective for 
designing and evaluating persuasive systems (based on the work of Fogg, i.e. the Functional 
Triad). It states that a system’s persuasiveness is mostly about system qualities and thus 
consists of 28 different design principles divided into four groups of system features:  Primary 
task support, dialogue support, system credibility support, and social support. It is primarily applied 
in system design and persuasive technology (i.e. the work of Fogg), however, the design 
principles offer great inspiration in more generic behavioural design cases as well.
 
Evil by Design made by Nodder (2013) is a framework of different approaches 
for using evil seductive persuasion in design. It claims that designs applying the underlying 
behavioural forces that underpin the seven sins (pride, sloth, gluttony, anger, envy, lust, and 
greed) are harder to discern (Nodder 2013). For each sin several design techniques are 
presented to be used for inspiration within behavioural design. The Evil by Design framework 
can be applied as a generic behavioural design approach, as it relates to both social models 
of behaviour (e.g. the work of Dan Lockton (Nodder 2013)) and to individual models of 
behaviour (“you shall not commit a sin”).
 
The MINDSPACE Way made by Dolan et al. (2012) states that behavioural problems 
can be tackled from two different perspectives: Changing minds and changing contexts. The 
framework can be used whenever behaviour change is being considered and aims at making 
Framework
Process
Method
Tool
Figure 2.8
Design approach 
categories 
==PAGE:40==
28
less coercive policies (e.g. softer nudges (Thaler & Sunstein 2009)) work better. Additionally, 
the framework presents nine robust behavioural effects to be considered in behavioural 
change: Messenger, incentives, shortcuts, norms, defaults, salience, priming, affect, commitment, 
and ego. For the designer the MINDSPACE way offers insights in regards to different possible 
incentives, yet it lacks integration with the wider design process (Cash et al. 2017).
 
The Social Implication Design Method made by Tromp (2013) is a framework 
aimed at supporting the designer in understanding what can be designed to induce behaviour 
change that brings about a desired social change. Because it points the designer in the direction 
of how to reason from a social problem to a design proposal (Tromp 2013), it appears more 
as a framework although it is named as a method. The framework states that the designer 
has to define the effects of the design at three levels (social, behavioural, and experiential 
level) before defining what to design (Tromp & Hekkert 2016). As the framework relates to 
both social problems and theories of change, the designer can apply it in relation to the two 
categories of social models of behaviour and theories of change. Yet, it lacks a logical and clear 
process to follow as well as guidance on how to move from goals to concrete interventions 
(Tromp & Hekkert 2016).
2.3.4.2 Process
 
The Eight-step Design Process made by Fogg (2009a) outlines a path to follow 
in designing persuasive technologies with techniques aimed at increasing the probability of 
behaviour change success. It consists of eight different steps to be carried out in sequence 
(Fogg 2009a): Choose a simple behaviour to target, choose a receptive audience, find what is 
preventing the target behaviour, choose an appropriate technology channel, find relevant examples 
of persuasive technology, imitate successful examples, test & iterate quickly, and expand on success. 
The designer can apply it as guidance on managing the different behavioural design phases, 
yet it is merely for inspiration, as it lacks clear guidelines and clear activities to take for the 
designer. The process can be applied in relation to the category of theories of change, as 
it is based on Fogg’s previous research.
 
The Process of Designing for Behavioural Change by Wendel (2014) defines 
four overall phases which a behaviour change process consists of: Understand, discover, design, 
and refine. In comparison with Fogg’s Eight-step Design Process, this process includes clear 
steps and activities suggested for the behavioural design project team in each phase, due 
to the inclusion of an integrated toolkit (Wendel 2013). The application of the Process of 
Designing for Behavioural Change should follow a linear process and can be applied for 
incorporating behavioural aspects into product development processes. Due to the toolkit, 
the process is very thorough in regards to theory, steps and guidelines (Cash et al. 2017) and 
due to the strong and multifaceted theoretical basis (Duhigg 2014; Kahneman 2003; Cialdini 
2007) (Wendel 2014) the process can be applied as a generic behavioural design approach.
 
The Behavioural Design Process made by Cash et al. (2017) integrates psychology, 
persuasive design and product development theory into a behavioural design process. It 
==PAGE:41==
 29
2.3.4.3 Method
 
The Behaviour Wizard made by Fogg & Hreha (2010) presents a method for 
matching target behaviours with solutions for achieving those behaviours. It classifies behaviour 
change targets into 15 different types. These types differ over the axes of flavour (five different 
behaviour distinctions visualised by colours) and duration (three different time aspects visualised 
by figures) (Fogg & Hreha 2010). Although the method lacks specific guidelines or design 
steps, it provides a common approach of categorising behaviour as well as allow for a deeper 
understanding of the winning patterns of behaviour change. It can be applied by the designer as 
a generic behavioural design approach, due to the fact that the obtained classifications relate 
to behaviour change as well as it can relate to both individuals and social groups.
 
The Behaviour Change Wheel made by Michie et al. (2011) is a method for 
characterising and designing behaviour change interventions and additionally linking them 
to an analysis of a targeted behaviour. It distinguishes between three aspects: Sources of 
behaviour, intervention functions, and policy categories (Michie et al. 2011). It is based upon 19 
different frameworks and aims at providing a more efficient method for choosing the kinds of 
interventions that are likely to be appropriate for a given behavioural target. However, it has yet 
to be researched whether this method leads to more efficient design of effective interventions 
(Michie et al. 2011) and it lacks integration with the wider design processes (Cash et al. 2017). 
Still, it can be applied by the designer as a generic behavioural design approach.
consists of seven steps: Behavioural problem/need, behaviour mapping, behavioural problem 
statement, fieldwork, behavioural specification, intervention development, and iterative testing 
and development (Cash et al. 2017). The Behavioural Design Process aims at closing a gap in 
current process support by means of clear integration with existing product development 
processes (Cash et al. 2017). While it explicitly details the development of the behaviour 
change intervention itself, it lacks the integrative aspects that the Process of Designing for 
Behavioural Change obtains from the toolkit (i.e. hands-on suggestions). It can be applied in 
relation to the category of theories of change yet refers to other parts of the BDS as well. 
2.3.4.4 Tool
 
The Developing an Intervention toolkit made by Community Tool Box (2016a) 
provides support for developing core components of a community intervention and adapting 
them to fit the context. It consists of a list of 13 steps to guide the designer through a complete 
behavioural design process, from problem identification to intervention implementation, monitoring 
and evaluation (Community Tool Box 2016a). It is aimed at solving community related problems 
and thus relates to social models of behaviour. 
 
The Design with Intent tool made by Lockton et al. (2010) is an innovation tool 
developed for helping humans use products and systems more efficiently. It is a suggestion 
tool, aimed at addressing relevant design techniques by providing examples of how similar 
behavioural problems have been tackled elsewhere (Lockton et al. 2010). These examples are 
divided into six different lenses, for which different techniques to inspire design solutions are 
==PAGE:42==
30
provided. The tool has two ‘modes’, inspiration and prescription, through which the designer or 
behavioural design project team can make use of the tool. While being an inspirational tool, 
it in itself is not supportive for ensuring an effective creation of successful interventions, yet it 
can be applied by the designer as a behavioural design approach relating to social models of 
behaviour and theories of change.
 
The Brains, Behaviour and Design toolkit made by Cervantes et al. (2011) is a 
tool developed to help designers in understanding and influencing decision-making processes. 
The toolkit is designed to be both informative and actionable, and thus enable an integration 
of the latest research in human behaviour and decision-making into practice (Cervantes et 
al. 2011). The toolkit consists of reference cards, concept ecosystem poster, irrational situations 
guides, strategy cards, and loss/gain worksheets. It is a tool that combines the understanding 
otherwise obtained through the previously mentioned behavioural theories and can be 
applied as a generic behavioural design approach.
 
Behaviour Change Strategy Cards made by Pfarr (2013) is a set of 23 cards 
developed to help designers think through strategies of nudging humans in a positive way 
when facing a behavioural design problem. The cards are divided into five thematic sections: 
Make it personal, tip the scales, craft the journey, set up the options, and keep it simple (Pfarr 
2013). The cards are an ideal tool as a supplement to an ideation session in a behavioural 
design project, which is also their limitation as they only cover a small part of the wider 
design process. As the cards cover individual and socials aspects of models of behaviour, the 
designer can apply them as a generic behavioural design approach.
 
The Persuasive by Design Model made by Hermsen et al. (2014) is a toolkit that 
describes a set of color-coded layers with accompanying questions aimed at addressing all 
aspects of a target behaviour and designing interventions for changing this behaviour. The 
layers consist of a behaviour layer, a bias and other threats layer, and a social influence layer. 
Furthermore, two additional layers of methods for changing behaviour and strategies to target 
automatic behaviour exist. What is especially valuable in this toolkit is that the tool reflects 
current behaviour thinking, by implicitly drawing parallels to several model of behaviours and 
theories of change, and in this way tries to enable the designer in making more evidence-based 
interventions (Hermsen et al. 2014). The toolkit is however limited by its complexity, which 
occurs because of the non-explicitly explained inclusion of the many models of behaviour 
and theories of change, which have made the tool difficult to apply for designers (Hermsen 
et al. 2015). 
The 15 behavioural design approaches that have been elaborated in the preceding sections 
(2.3.4.1-2.3.4.4) enables a practical transfer of behaviour and behaviour change understanding 
into design activities. This transfer is crucial in regards to increasing the likelihood of successful 
behavioural design, as design processes that are theory based are more effective than those 
that are not (Glanz & Bishop 2010; Michie et al. 2008; Henkemans et al. 2015). From the 
elaborations and discussion in the preceding sections, an attempt of linking the different 
behavioural design approaches with their theoretical foundation has been made (see table 
2.4 on the following page (31)).
==PAGE:43==
 31
The Functional Triad
(Fogg 2003)

Table 2.4
Overview of the 
15 behavioural 
design approaches
Two different ways of distinguishing such a link have been used in this thesis: generic behavioural 
design approaches (orange) and theory specific behavioural design approaches (red). What this 
attempt suggests is that all the selected behavioural design approaches are in fact associated 
with theory, regardless the specific or generic behavioural theory connection. Consequently, 
this further underlines the cruciality of enabling integrative thinking for theory inclusion in 
the BDS, as the behavioural design approaches, especially the generic ones, also require an 
understanding of the behavioural theories’ relations and applicability. 
• Although design approaches can be divided into the categories of frameworks, 
processes, methods, and tools, all four categories offer insights and different levels of 
detail relevant to all kinds of design projects. Thus, behavioural design approaches 
are not mutually exclusive, i.e. the choice of one type of behavioural design approach 
does not hinder choosing another one as well. Rather it enables better integration 
of all aspects of behavioral theories into the design activities. 
• From this exploration of 15 different behavioural design approaches it is shown 
that, while they offer practical ways of transferring behaviour and behaviour change 
understanding into design activities, they do so through an implicitly visualised 
integrative thinking. Several of the design approaches is derived from an inclusion 
of multiple behavioural theories, yet they offer little explicit, visual or practical 
guidance of the exact relations to the theories, i.e. the designers lack ways of 
understanding the explicit transfer of behavioural insights. And while it is possible 
to link specific behavioural design approaches to their associated specific theories, 
it is only feasible through an extensive review.
2.3.4.5 Concluding remarks on behavioural design approaches
Based on the exploration and discussion of the 15 selected behavioural design approaches in 
the previous sections (2.3.4.1-2.3.4.4), the following remarks can be concluded:

Figure 2.9
Research 
Framework
Unfortunately, what can be derived from an extensive review of literature as the desired BDS 
is currently far from reality, due to a complex situation affected by three main issues.
2.4.1 Identification of issues
This research framework forms the theoretical basis for the research of the thesis and 
presents the divergent and convergent steps the designer should go through in the BDS to 
develop a successful intervention. The designer can use this framework in different ways, since 
the combination of problems, models of behaviour, theories of change and design approaches 
can differ from project to project. Yet, the designer should obtain a holistic understanding of 
all relevant problem layers (section 2.3.1.1) and combine it with an integrative thinking of the 
behavioural theories (models of behaviour (section 2.3.2) and theories of change (section 
2.3.3)). Furthermore, to integrate theories continuously throughout the BDS, the integrative 
thinking of the behavioural theories should enable application of relevant behavioural design 
approaches.
==PAGE:45==
 33
2.4.1.1 Issue between problem and behavioural theories
• For designers there is an issue in figuring out how to connect a behavioural problem 
with the most suitable behavioural theories – a connection that should be based 
on a comprehensive and fully-understood problem identification. 
The synergy between behavioural problem understanding and behavioural theories is 
important because choosing theories based on the problem is essential, as it contributes to 
the foundation of achieving an effective solution (Fogg 2009b; Glanz & Bishop 2010; Darnton, 
2008b), i.e. generating the correct problem hypotheses (section 2.2.2). While this synergy is, 
to some extent, reflected in behavioural design literature (e.g. Darnton 2008b; Niedderer 
et al. 2016), few researchers analyse behaviour in regards to how it is affected on different 
problem layers, despite the fact that these layers logically do affect problem contexts in 
their own manner. From this, an issue arises for designers, due to a lack of visualisation 
and communication of how certain behavioural theories can contribute to certain problem 
contexts. This makes designers unable to reflect upon and independently determine which 
behavioural theories to apply in a given problem context. A consequence from this is that 
the designers have no opportunity of ascribing supremacy to the different behavioural theories 
(Bhattacharyya et al. 2006), which would offer a higher level of support in the selection 
process. Another consequence, which is the most outstanding, is the risk that an inefficient 
problem analysis can lead to an inefficient process (choosing an unsuitable theory), which 
can eventually lead to unsuccessful interventions. Solving this issue would therefore have an 
impact on the number of effective designed interventions, and additionally have an impact 
on the possibility of designers increasing their own behavioural design analytical capabilities.
2.4.1.2 Issue between integrative thinking among models of behaviour & theories 
of change
• Once specific models of behaviour have been chosen to describe a behavioural 
problem, designers face an issue of connecting it to the proper theories of change. 
Despite the fact that few researchers have dealt with the topic of integrative thinking among 
models of behaviour and theories of change (e.g. Prager 2012; Hermsen et al. 2014), research 
shows little indication of an explanatory practical approach for this integrative thinking. From 
this two issues arises. First of all, incorrect application occurs, as research lacks practical means 
for describing and explaining what each category of behavioural theories has to offer in the BDS 
(e.g. using theories of change to explain behaviour (Michie et al. 2008)). From this, designers 
are unable to make qualified decisions in regards to the proper combination of behavioural 
theories. Second of all, complicated application occurs, as behavioural design literature need to 
communicate why this integrative thinking is crucial in order to design successful interventions 
(understanding why it is important together with how to do it). Without this communication 
designers are not able to validate their selection processes and thus applying different 
theories in combination becomes unnecessarily complicated. While some behavioural design 
approaches (e.g. Hermsen et al. 2014 or Michie et al. 2011) do aim to provide the designers 
==PAGE:46==
34
2.4.1.3 Issue between behavioural theories and design approaches
• For designers there is an issue in connecting specific theories of change to the 
proper behavioural design approaches.
Currently research show a vague indication of which behavioural design approaches to use 
in extension of choosing a specific behavioural theory (Niedderer et al. 2016) in order to 
obtain the most efficient behavioural design solution (Zachrisson & Boks 2012; Bhattacharyya 
et al. 2006). From extensively reviewing relevant behavioural design approaches an indication 
of generic and theory-specific behavioural design approaches can be seen. Yet, without 
this review, this connection may not seem clear for designers, due to a lack of adequate 
knowledge within the psychology field and more so due to a lack of awareness, common 
language, resources, and ability to obtain this knowledge (Hermsen et al. 2014; Niedderer et 
al. 2016). The literature within the field of behavioural theories is comprehensive and has an 
unmanageable range (Zachrisson & Boks 2012), hence designers could use a more practical 
and systematic approach for making the task of incorporating knowledge obtained from 
behavioural theories into selection of design approaches manageable (Fogg & Hreha 2010). 
Yet, in order to solve this issue designers must ultimately possess the ability to understand 
and apply models of behaviour and theories of change in the correct context, and thus 
solving this third issue should come second to the two previous identified issues (section 
2.4.1.1 and 2.4.1.2). However, as literature show, this third issue has to some extent already 
been researched and dealt with (Niedderer et al. 2016; Michie et al. 2008; Zachrisson & Boks 
2012), thus it should be straightforward to solve. Further, the two previously identified issues 
are reckoned to have a bigger impact for the designer in the BDS and are thus granted the 
focus of this thesis.
with practical approaches for combining all valuable aspects of behavioural theories (i.e. 
apply integrative thinking), they do so through an extensive simplification of the behavioural 
theories. This have implicated the designers’ level of behavioural theory abstraction, as it is a 
result of a trade-off between: 1. A simplification of the behavioural theories (which is in line 
with the observation that, among others, psychological theories are viewed as “impenetrable” 
by designers (Hermsen et al. 2015)), and 2. The level of detail regarding the reasons behind 
the combination of behavioural theories that is communicated to the designers. Thus, current 
behavioural design approaches that do apply integrative thinking, do it only with respect 
to explaining how and completely leaves out the why. Consequently, current behavioural 
design literature lack ways of visualising the links and relations between the two categories 
of behavioural theories, as well as explaining why the links and relations are important to 
understand. For designers this currently leads to an unintentionally complicated and inefficient 
task of including behavioural theories in the BDS.
==PAGE:47==
 35
2.4.2 Research aim & objectives
Given the research framework and associated issues, the aim for this thesis is to develop a 
behavioural design support tool that connects behavioural problems to behavioural theories 
and thus enables a practical translation of knowledge in the BDS. In this way, the designer can 
obtain a holistic overview of the behavioural theories necessary for developing a successful 
behavioural design intervention. As a result, the research aim and objectives can be formulated 
as following: 
2.4.2.2 Research objectives
1. Obtain and map a structural understanding of the different core behavioural 
elements in theory and in design industry. 
2. Identify path dependencies and other interconnections from behavioural problem 
to behavioural design approach with respect to integrative thinking of behavioural 
theory.
3. Based on the two previous answers above, develop a tool for supporting designers 
in structuring their understanding of behavioural problems and in selecting 
appropriate behavioural theories.
4. Validate and evaluate the support tool.
This section aims at elaborating on general design to determine relevant contextual factors 
that influences design projects and thus have to be considered in the upcoming support 
tool development. To apply the findings from the literature review and develop a useful and 
successful support tool, certain relevant design knowledge about the designer is needed, 
which is the rationale behind including an elaboration of general design. Thus, it is necessary 
to take different relevant factors of general design into account when developing a support 
tool for behavioural design, as it deals with designers and their ways of working, their way of 
staging projects and their way of rationalising, conceptualising and thinking.
2.5 Staging considerations of the support 
tool development
2.4.2.1 Research aim 
Developing a support tool for designers aimed at providing a structured overview of behavioural 
theories and their relations, with respect to considering all perspectives of behavioural design 
problems and tailoring this overview to fit with existing behavioural design approaches.
2.5.1 General design processes
The first aspect of general design to acknowledge is design processes. By the definition of 
Hubka and Eder (1996) design processes are “an explanation of the actions of design in a 
socio-technical and industrial context” (Andreasen et al. 2015, p.65). However, the term design 
==PAGE:48==
36
The Double Diamond Model (Design Council 2007) presents the overall phases of a design 
project and is distinguished by its diverging and converging phases, in which different activities 
take place to support the aims discover, define, develop, and deliver. The Frame Creation 
Model (Dorst et al. 2016) presents nine different steps, which together form the analysis and 
creation central to creative design practice. Whereas the Double Diamond Model diverges 
and converges, the Frame Creation Model zooms in and out on different aspects of the 
creation of frames. Within each step of the Frame Creation Model a tool for reaching the aim 
is proposed, which makes it more prescriptive opposed to the Double Diamond Model that 
merely describes the overall phases.
Consequently, general design processes are valuable to consider in the development of the 
support tool, as behavioural design can be applied as both a complete design process or 
merely as part(s) of a general design process. Thus it is essential to acknowledge the former 
and latter parts of general design processes.
Discover
Define
Develop
Deliver
Problem
Problem
Definition
Solution
archaeology
paradox
content
field
themes
frames
futures
transformation
integration
Figure 2.10 (left)
Double Diamond 
Model
Figure 2.11 (right)
Frame Creation 
Model
2.5.2 Staging design
Whereas design processes describe and prescribe how designers work, design staging 
describes “the act of establishing and fitting a team’s space to a project, to best support the 
design activity” (Andreasen et al. 2015, p.72). In design staging it is essential to empower 
team activity and support innovation (Andreasen et al. 2015), yet it is often underestimated 
and overlooked, despite being an important aspect in creating the best possible conditions 
for design success (Andreasen et al. 2015). One aspect of staging could be the physical 
environment or work space encouraging creativity and creative mindsets. Design staging 
processes often refers to both the actual activities performed by the designer as well as the 
models describing and guiding these activities (Andreasen et al. 2015). This is supported by 
Cross (2008), who states that there are two ways of distinguishing between design processes: 
Descriptive models (describing the sequences of activities that occur in design) and prescriptive 
models (prescribing patterns of activities). Design processes often describe the sequence of 
activities or order of steps (Cross 2008) and thus when mapped they present a proposed 
flow, which can be seen in the two following examples of design processes: The Double 
Diamond Model (Design Council 2007) and the Frame Creation Model (Dorst et al. 
2016) (see figure 2.10 and 2.11).
==PAGE:49==
 37
Regarding the development of the support tool it is vital to consider design staging, as the 
tool is developed for designers and thus should be applicable in any design context. Since 
the support tool should enhance the possibility of creating successful behaviour change 
interventions, it is important that it does not hinder design staging, but rather enhances it to 
create the best possible conditions for design successes. 
2.5.3 Design mindset
Finally, design mindsets are defined as, “the proper understanding of a method’s use in 
accordance with the designer’s reality (interpretation of task, situation, execution, validation, etc.), 
and the method’s background and proper use” (Andreasen et al. 2015, p.57). A design mindset 
means understanding the proper context and phenomena related to method application 
(Andreasen 2003). In other words, a design mindset provide the designer with an approach 
for framing a design project, i.e. following a certain path within a given design field. Design for 
X (e.g. Meerkamm & Koch 2005), Behavioural Design (e.g. Cash et al. 2017) or Participatory 
Design (e.g. Beyer & Holtzblatt 1998) are all examples of design fields that can determine the 
approach for design projects and thus function as design mindsets. Consequently, the support 
tool will in a natural way address the behavioural design mindset, yet it would be beneficial if 
it was applicable with other types of design mindsets.
Figure 2.12
Design staging 
model
(Andreasen et al. 
2015, p.75)
2.5.4 Relationship between the general design elements 
Based on the elaboration of three general design aspects (design processes, design staging, and 
design mindsets) figure 2.13 on the following page (38) exemplifies the relationship between 
these three general design elements based on the Frame Creation Model.
brings individual designers together in an effective team by setting perspectives, defining 
contributions, clarifying roles, and creating motivation. In addition, staging is more than just 
supporting the team and innovation, it is also about integrating the user (Andreasen et al. 
2015) (see figure 2.12). 
==PAGE:50==
38
Figure 2.13
Relationship 
between design 
processes, design 
staging, and design 
mindset
The frame creation model prescribes the different steps of the design process. Different 
sequences of these steps may require different design staging and thus it can be interchanged 
from sequence to sequence within a design process. The mindset on top affects the entire 
process as well as the activities within each step of the process. Therefore, all three aspects 
of general design (design processes, design staging, and design mindsets) must be considered 
individually as well as together when developing the support tool.
As stated in this literature review the designer should obtain a holistic understanding of 
all relevant problem layers and combine it with an integrative thinking of the behavioural 
theories in order to effectively progress from problem behaviour to solution in the BDS. Yet, 
issues arise regarding this problem understanding and integrative thinking of behavioural 
theories. Thus, the aim of this thesis is to develop a support tool that connects behavioural 
problems to behavioural theories and enables a practical translation of knowledge in the BDS. 
Yet,  in order to develop a successful tool that highlights the designers’ ways of working, the 
three aspects of general design (design processes, design staging, and design mindsets) must be 
considered simultaneously. 
As the current theoretical basis of this thesis is obtained through literature, a more practical 
understanding is needed and thus interviews will be conducted through an Investigation of 
Current Practice in chapter 4. Yet, foremost the chosen methodology of the thesis will be 
presented in the following chapter (3). 
2.6 Conclusion & key findings of the 
Literature Review 
==PAGE:51==
 39
Chapter 3
Methodology
==PAGE:52==
40
Due to the findings from the Literature Review (chapter 2), the chosen methodology for this 
thesis is the Design Research Methodology (DRM) by Blessing & Chakrabarti (2009). It 
has been applied as a framework for the design research, providing guidelines for systematic 
planning of research and thesis activities. Hence, the thesis has been divided into four partly 
parallel and iterative main stages (figure 3.1).
3.1. Introduction to Methodology 
Stage 1: Research Clarification
Stage 2: Descriptive Study I
Stage 3: Prescriptive Study
Stage 4: Descriptive Study II

Figure 3.1
The DRM 
framework
The thesis has reached a type 3 design research, meaning that the content in the first three 
stages (1-3) have been performed comprehensively, whereas the content in the last stage 
(stage 4) has been performed on a very initial basis. The following sections 3.2-3.5 briefly 
describe each stage of the thesis both in regards to the theoretical methodology and the 
different thesis activities.
3.2 Stage 1: Research Clarification - Literature Review
In the Research Clarification stage the aim is to find supporting evidence or indications 
of the initial assumptions or hypotheses in literature, to formulate realistic and worthwhile 
research goals (Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009). As already presented in the previous chapter 
(2), a research framework was created by reviewing literature concerning behavioural design, 
current behavioural problem procedures, behavioural theories and behavioural design 
==PAGE:53==
 41
approaches. This was done to identify and explore possible gaps in current behavioural 
design literature and as a result, associated issues and a clarification of the concrete research 
aim and research objectives were determined. In this thesis these explorations were helpful 
for getting an understanding of the BDS and thus revealing the overall research aim.
3.3 Stage 2 - Descriptive Study I: Investigation of Current 
Practice
In the Descriptive Study I an investigation of current practice was obtained through the 
application of a qualitative approach (i.e. interviewing five different Danish design consultancies). 
In this thesis, a qualitative approach was preferred as the aim of this stage was to investigate 
the nature of the phenomena (the findings and gaps identified in the literature review) in 
practice, and less focus was put on investigating to what degree the phenomena occurs 
(Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009). While this study took place, a refinement of the literature 
review was conducted continuously, as Blessing and Chakrabarti (2009) suggest that a more 
detailed and better understanding of the existing situation can be obtained by identifying and 
clarifying possible gaps in practice and combining these with the knowledge obtained through 
literature. The key findings and deliverables of this stage confirmed the understanding of 
behavioural design obtained through the literature review and provided deeper insights and 
clarified some of the key factors (Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009) and the core focus regarding 
the upcoming support tool development. 
3.4 Stage 3: Prescriptive Study - Support Tool Development
From obtaining a deeper understanding of the existing situation through the two previous 
stages (1 and 2), corrections and elaborations of the initial description of the desired BDS 
took place through a Prescriptive Study. This led to a specification of the requirements of 
the support tool (most suitable factors to be addressed), chosen for the aim of improving 
the existing situation (Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009). With these requirements in mind, as well 
as with inspiration from other existing tools or games, several different support tool designs 
were developed. These proposals for solutions were then evaluated through a comparison 
of each other and against the identified gaps and need, which led to a chosen support 
tool design. This support tool design was verified in different relevant settings (workshops) 
through support evaluation (i.e. checking that the support fulfilled the requirements). This 
involved checking for consistency and completeness, as well as checking that the smallest 
elements of the support could function at the lowest intended level, and thus that it had a 
strong potential of realising the intended impact (Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009).  As a result, 
several iterations of the support tool design occurred from the support evaluation, leading 
to a final version of the support tool: The Super Cool Analysis Tool. While the support tool 
development took place, suggestions for the development of a future (partial) evaluation 
plan was made, in order to ensure that the indicators of impact would be measurable in the 
features and activities of the final version of the Super Cool Analysis Tool.
==PAGE:54==
42
3.5 Stage 4: Descriptive Study II - Validation & Evaluation 
of the Support tool
The final stage of this thesis was the Descriptive Study II, in which, according to Blessing 
and Chakrabarti (2009), the aim is to investigate and measure the impact of the support tool. 
In order to allow for an evaluation of the intended support (both in regards to application 
and success), an empirical study should have been performed together with experimental 
validation of the intended impact, (Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009). Yet, despite the fact that the 
support tool was fully developed and realised, the project duration of this thesis did not allow 
for a full evaluation, resulting in a very initial DS-II stage (making the conducted research in 
this thesis a type 3, as stated in the introduction). However, as initial evaluation of a research 
project was needed in order to round it off properly (Blessing and Chakrabarti 2009), different 
activities in regards to indicating the three aspects of 1. The applicability, usability and usefulness 
of the support; 2. The issues, factors and links that need detailed evaluation; and 3. A suggestion for 
an evaluation plan, took place. 
In this thesis an indication of the applicability, usability and usefulness of the support tool was 
provided through commencing a final demonstration of the tool and discussing the findings. 
Doing so allowed for a further indication of the issues, factors and links that need more detailed 
evaluation, which then provided suggestions for further evaluation and application measures. 
And finally from this, a suggestion for an evaluation plan was provided through a description of 
suggested activities and measures to be performed and taken into account in a experimental 
validation setting. To underline the fact that this stage was initial, the combined findings 
resulting in a suggestion for an evaluation plan is presented in the section describing the thesis’ 
further work (chapter 8), as this perfectly rounds of the research conducted in this thesis.
==PAGE:55==
 43
Chapter 4
Investigation of 
Current Practice
==PAGE:56==
44
This chapter seeks to investigate current behavioural design practice in industry to develop a 
support tool for designers applying behavioural design. As the support tool should enable a 
translation of behavioural insights (from theory) into the current design practice (industry), it 
is necessary to investigate how behavioural design happens in practice and not just base the 
support tool development on findings from literature. Hence, five explorative interviews with 
different Danish design consultancies were conducted to investigate the practical implications 
of the research framework and gather new valuable insights. 
Section 4.2 describes the interview setup and interview guide followed by an analysis of the 
results in section 4.3. Doing so allow for a determination of the core focus of the support 
tool, i.e. determining where the tool will have the biggest impact and create most value for 
the designer, with respect to the research framework (section 2.4). 
4.1 Introduction to Investigation of Current 
Practice
4.2 Interview setup
This section describes the setup and interview guide (Turner 2010) for the interviews with 
five Danish design consultancies: KRUKOW, Decision Design, /KL.7, The Nudging Company and 
Delta – Idemolab.
All five Qualitative Semi-structured Interviews (Fylan 2005) were conducted in meeting 
rooms at the respective design consultancies, and were audio recorded together with sporadic 
note taking. After the interviews, the audio recording was put into writing and combined 
with the interview notes. In all interviews the aim was to answer the main questions 
presented in the Interview Guide in table 4.1 on the following page (45), together with a 
number of sub questions illustrated in appendix 1.
However, in each of the interviews the strategy of the interview team was to apply the 
interview format of the general interview guide approach (Turner 2010) in which the 
interview team remains in the driver’s seat, but flexibility takes precedence based on perceived 
prompts from the participants. This way of interviewing ensured a relaxed atmosphere 
enhancing the possibility of getting relevant and true answers. 
Of the interviewed design consultancies four were behavioural design consultancies (offering 
expertise or professional advice only in regards to behavioural design), while one (Delta – 
Idemolab) was an engineering design consultancy (offering expertise or professional advice in 
multiple fields such as technology, sustainability or behavioural design).

4.3.1 Result 1: Problem analysis & theoretical back-
catalogue
By using an Affinity Diagram (Beyer & Holtzblatt 1998) the interview notes and audio 
recording were analysed and combined into different findings assessed to be most important 
and relevant in regards to the upcoming support tool development and in regards to the 
scope of the thesis (see appendix 2). For more detailed information about each interview see 
appendices 3-7, however the overall results derived from the five interviews were as follows. 
4.3 Analysis of the results
All of the interviewed employees (founders, designers and behavioural economist) of the 
behavioural design consultancies have a behavioural theoretical foundation (i.e. theoretical 
back-catalogue) obtained through years of experience with behavioural design projects (table 
4.2 on page 47). In contrast, the designer interviewed from the engineering design consultancy 
==PAGE:58==
46
stated that behavioural design projects are often carried out by involving a sociologist to assist 
in making a behavioural analysis. This indicates that the employee in the engineering design 
consultancy does not have the capability to do so, i.e. lacks a theoretical back-catalogue. 
A theoretical back-catalogue affects many front-end behavioural design activities, and achieving 
such a back-catalogue requires a lot of effort and time, as the founder from Decision Design 
stated, “When you present a problem and say now we need some people to do something here. 
What is behind it? What theories do we need? I do not know! I’m not a behavioural economist, how 
should I learn it in an hour? So that’s the big challenge.” In all the behavioural design consultancies 
the problem analysis consists of data collection (e.g. observing and obtaining statistical data) 
as highlighted by the founder of KRUKOW, “[..] Based on this situation, you do what we call a 
behavioural mapping. Here you collect knowledge about two things: You collect knowledge about 
the existing choice-architecture design [..] and existing interaction data [..]” However, parallel to 
data collection activities the behavioural design consultancies expressed that they are trying 
to analyse the data and make sense of it in regards to theory e.g. through behaviour mapping 
or other models, as the designer from /KL.7 said, “We also apply work models from Beyer 
& Holtzblatt “Contextual Design” [e.g. flow model] in order to identify contact point where the 
behaviour occurs.“ The importance of the problem analysis phase in a behavioural design project 
in behavioural design consultancies was further highlighted by the founder of KRUKOW, “Yet 
behavioural mapping is the first and very important step in behavioural design. [..] It’s not hard to 
come up with good ideas, especially not when you have a rock-solid behavioural mapping.” 
In contrast, the engineering design consultancy tries to map the behaviour in similar ways 
to the behavioural design consultancies, yet their approach lacks a behavioural theory 
foundation. This is among others stressed by the fact that Delta - Idemolab obtains data 
through interviews, “We try to breakdown the behaviour in a kind of flowchart in order to 
obtain an understanding of the behaviour while making observations and interviews to get a 
deeper understanding.” (designer, Delta - Idemolab). This approach is by behavioural experts 
emphasised as unsuitable in a behavioural context, as this will not provide the correct data, 
“Typically, you don’t want to go in and ask, because people often have a hard time to explain why 
they do what they do.” (founder, Decision Design). However, after behavioural mapping two of 
the behavioural design consultancies and the engineering design consultancy directly stated 
that they make hypotheses regarding the problem behaviour, “We have some insights to be 
made into hypotheses” (behavioural economist from The Nudging Company). This emphasises 
similarities and differences across the two different types of design consultancies.
Consequently, due to the fact that problem analysis is emphasised as the first important step 
in behavioural design, designers with a theoretical back-catalogue have greater probabilities 
of developing successful interventions, as their problem understanding is theoretically 
founded. Therefore, applying behavioural design in a project without a theoretical back-
catalogue, complicates the process and adds uncertainty to the BDS. Evidently, there is a need 
for a way to achieve a theoretical back-catalogue without spending months or years on it, as 
it is proved by designers with comprehensive experience of behavioural design to have great 
==PAGE:59==
 47
When mapping behaviour all behavioural design consultancies (some implicitly, some 
explicitly) relies on behavioural insights derived from theory. Although there are many 
different behavioural theories applicable for behavioural design (just in this thesis 21 of 
them are explored) the behavioural design consultancies indirectly highlight the Theory of 
Heuristics and Biases (Tversky & Kahneman 1974) as the most important one (table 4.3 on 
the following page (48)). This indicates a level of tunnel vision in regards to behavioural design 
knowledge and theory inclusion for designers with a theoretical back-catalogue.  
In section 2.3 it was found that integrative thinking of different models of behaviour and 
theories of change leads to a more holistic understanding of the problem behaviour in 
question, and hence, to more effective interventions. Relying highly on the Theory of 
Heuristics and Biases (one of many behavioural theories) implies that the understanding of a 
multifaceted combination of behavioural theories is missing in behavioural design consultancies. 
However, the understanding does to some extent exist. Two of the four behavioural design

behavioural design field is complex and requires expertise.

In contrast to behavioural design consultancies applying their personalised process, instead, a 
fairly unstructured ad-hoc process of behavioural theory inclusion is applied in the remaining 
behavioural design consultancies. Even for the design consultancies with a personalised and 
thoroughly contemplated process in place, the inclusion of behavioural theories can become 
unstructured. For instance, the founder of Decision Design emphasises that even though they 
have a database with behavioural theories, they still have to brush up on new theories, “We 
also have a database where we have read the most, but if we miss something, then we will try to 
research [..] Maybe we have to read new theories that we have not known before.” Likewise, a 
designer from /KL.7 explains that their theory inclusion process is random and unstructured: 
“[..] and sometimes it is kind of flavour of the month, if somebody just read a nice book. [..] No no 
no [answering if the theory inclusion process is structured], it is from the back-catalogue, but there 
are also some things we know work well from other projects. So there are definitely some things 
that go again.” Finally, the founder of KRUKOW states that even though they have made the 
complex less complex by developing their own design principles, it still occurs that they miss 
something and must return to theory, “Then it may occur that we gradually find out “okay, we 
really miss something here” [..] and then we always go back to the cognitive psychology and look 
“okay, out of these 450 biases - what can we have overlooked?” [..]”
This unstructured theory inclusion in behavioural design consultancies is due to differences in 
projects as well as due to the large complexity of behavioural theories and missing superiority 
or clear relevance, as stated by a designer from /KL.7, “We are rarely stuck due to a lack of 
==PAGE:61==
 49
Project staging in all the interviewed design consultancies is found to be a differentiating 
process, since project timelines, project contents and project participants’ backgrounds differ 
(i.e. behavioural economists, sociologists and designers) (see table 4.4 on the following page 
(50)). However, what all design consultancies have in common is that, in all behavioural design 
projects there is a distinctive need for communicating across different professions. 
For example, two behavioural design consultancies made it clear that they are not always 
involved in all phases of the behavioural design process, but merely part of separate phases 
(such as discover and define in the Double Diamond Model (section 2.5.1)). Moreover, not all 
employees are part of all phases in the behavioural design project. This indicates that several 
handovers, both internally and externally, occur in behavioural design and thus communication 
is an important part of the process. This seems to be a general feature of any kind of design 
project, but more complicated in behavioural design due to the field’s combination of several 
professions (e.g. sociologists, psychologists, economists and designers) and their very different 
professional languages. In The Nudging Company, a designer explains how she gets more 
involved in the beginning of the project to avoid misunderstandings, “[..] I get more involved 
in the beginning of the project and it suits me well, because for me, the design is also a lot about 
being informed of what is happening and sometimes the handovers may make it a bit unclear to 
me what I think is going on.” Communication through handovers in behavioural design projects 
is further stated by the behavioural economist from The Nudging Company, “Where much 
time is lost are by delivering to each other. We have phases where information must be moved. We 
have some insights to be made into hypotheses to be made to test. It takes insanely long time to 
deliver information. It is complex.”
4.3.3 Result 3: Staging & communication within 
behavioural design projects
theories, as there are so many theories you could include. It is more theory vs. time pressure, usually 
balancing of a lack of time compared to getting in depth.”
From making behavioural design less complex (either through combining specifically chosen 
theories into a personalised process or through an ad-hoc process), the theory selection has 
become inflexible and rigid, and to a certain degree, in all behavioural design consultancies, 
centered around the Theory of Heuristics and Biases (Tversky & Kahneman 1974). Hence, 
although the understanding of a multifaceted combination of behavioural theories does exist 
in the behavioural design consultancies, indications are that it is only used as a last resort, and 
not as the optimal point of origin for each behavioural design project.
This results in an implication of tunnel vision in regards to theory inclusion which in current 
behavioural design practice is difficult to overcome, due to huge amounts of behavioural 
theory to brush up upon and due to limited time to do so (considering all theories in depth 
is “mission impossible”). Yet, it is important to challenge this tunnel vision to encourage and 
enable more effective interventions and to realise supremacy among the behavioural theories, 
which would enable a more efficient process in all types of design consultancies.
==PAGE:62==
50
Additionally, due to different professions in the behavioural design project team, the 
employees lack ways of challenging each other’s possible theoretical blind spots, and further 
risk losing behavioural insights from one phase to another, if these handovers have not been 
thoroughly considered or planned for. Consequently, communication is an important aspect 
that impacts all phases of a behavioural design project, yet to some extent traditional project 
management tools can address it. However, as it has impact on the progress of behavioural 
design projects, it is essential to consider the consequences of both project staging and 
project communication in any support tool to be developed for behavioural design purposes.

==PAGE:63==
 51
Three behavioural design consultancies highlighted practical implications in regards to testing 
designed interventions as very challenging in behavioural design (see table 4.5), due to the 
fact that factors such as ethics, physical layout, or surroundings are difficult to foresee when 
designing in a closed environment. This is for instance expressed by the founder of KRUKOW, 
“If you are in an academic world and test things in a closed environment then you have no clue 
about how and if it would work in real life settings.”
Unforeseeable practical implications impact the BDS in many ways, as it may contribute to 
a change of the intervention and thus change the results (due to being unable to measure 
the desired behaviour change (further elaborated in section 4.3.5)). However, this practical 
implication lies after the solution element in the BDS, and is therefore not within the 
scope of the research framework. Yet, strong emphasis is given to the aspect of how to 
convert theoretical insights to practical design, as this ideally should minimise the impact 
of unforeseeable practical implications. The challenges of converting theoretical insights to 
practical work is further emphasized by the founder of KRUKOW, “Everyone wants to perform 
behavioural design and include theories but there are so few descriptions on how exactly to do so, 
and how you convert it to changes in the real world.”
When asked, all design consultancies admit taking part in different kinds of evaluation activities 
(see table 4.6 on the following page (52)). Furthermore, all design consultancies determine 
project success, through either a measurable behaviour change or through satisfied clients, 
indicating that some level of post-project evaluation occurs in all design consultancies. 
This indicates that evaluation is a part of current behavioural design practice, yet it happens 
unstructured and often implicitly. Furthermore, although several design consultancies 
evidently see the value in evaluation, they struggle in seeing the benefits of evaluating their

The five results from the previous sections (4.3.1 - 4.3.5) were not the only findings from the 
interviews, yet they have been given supremacy in this thesis, as they were the ones found 
to be most relevant in the task of investigating the practical implications of the research 
framework and gather new valuable insights. Consequently, among these five results another 
level of relevance exists. In order to visualise this relevance, a User Needs Journey (with 
inspiration from a customer decision journey (Court et al. 2009)) have been made (see figure 4.1 
on the following page (53)) illustrating a designer’s challenges, and hence needs, in a behavioural 
design project. What this User Needs Journey shows is that are five different clusters of needs 
each relating to the results mentioned above: a theoretical back-catalogue need, a challenging 
tunnel vision need, a handover need, a testing need and finally an evaluation need.

approach and those with an ad-hoc theory-including approach, could benefit from evaluation, 
as it would allow them to learn from experience and challenge their own perception of what 
a good behavioural design project is and how behavioural theories affect these projects.

Figure 4.1

Journey
Combining this user needs journey with the findings and discussion points mentioned in 
sections 4.3.1-4.3.5, the following can be concluded:
• 
Within the scope of the research framework there are limited possibilities of addressing the 
testing need, as it relates more to practical implications than theoretical implications. The need 
will therefore not be addressed as the main focus of the support tool, yet the attempt of 
translating behavioural insights into practical design opportunities should be addressed.
• 
A support tool focused on evaluating different aspects within a behavioural design 
project could be beneficial. Yet, it has not been stressed as urgent by any of the 
interviewed design consultancies and it will thus be difficult for design consultancies 
to be convinced by or see the benefits of adapting such a tool. However, as evaluation 
activities offer great benefits regarding the research framework it can be addressed in the 
support tool development, but not as the main focus.
• 
Although the handover need occurs three times in the User Needs Journey and has 
a big impact on the behavioural design process, the need is really about communication. 
This can be addressed with traditional project management tools (i.e. not theory driven 
tools) such as the IT-communication tool Asana (Asana 2008). Moreover, only one design 
==PAGE:66==
54
consultancy highlighted communication as challenging. Finally, the theoretical back-catalogue 
need has a greater impact on the design consultancies’ project successes, as a good problem 
understanding (i.e. insights) is needed before needing to communicating obtained insights. 
• 
The two major identified needs both circle around the analysis phase of a behavioural design 
project. While the need for a theoretical back-catalogue relates to designers with limited 
experience of behavioural design, the need for challenging tunnel vision relates to designers 
with comprehensive experience of behavioural design. Therefore, to create a support tool 
that will add value to all kinds of designers involved with behavioural design (both behavioural 
design consultancies and engineering design consultancies), it would be valuable to address 
both needs in one support tool that will:
• 
Provide designers with limited experience of behavioural design simple ways of 
obtaining behavioural design knowledge with respect to integrative thinking of theory 
inclusion and thus enable better possibilities of easily designing successful interventions.
• 
Challenge designers’ with comprehensive experience of behavioural design tunnel 
vision in regards to theory inclusion, through structured ways of applying integrative 
thinking and thus opening up for new possibly valuable theory perspectives.
The aim of these five interviews was to investigate current behavioural design practice and 
from this, with respect to the research framework, determine where a support tool would 
have the biggest impact for designers. Consequently, the following key findings were derived:
• From investigating current practice it was highlighted that behavioural design 
concerns many different professions besides the designer, and hence, while 
focused on the designer, the upcoming support tool must be applicable for these 
other professions as well. As a result, every time the phrase “designer” is mentioned 
in the remaining parts of the thesis, it considers these other professions as well. 
• From the interviews five different needs stood out as being possible areas in 
which value could be added to current behavioural design practice. Yet, the needs 
concerned with front-end activities (such as problem analysis) were highlighted as 
most influential, and thus chosen as the core focus of the support tool. Additionally, 
each of the two chosen needs (theoretical back-catalogue need and challenging 
tunnel vision need) ideally addresses designers with both limited and comprehensive 
experience of behavioural design. Yet, in order to develop a support tool with the 
biggest possible reach, a combination of the two needs has been chosen. This is 
possible as both needs represent parts of the same phases and thus the features 
of the needs would complement each other well.
As the support tool focus has been formulated through a combination of two needs, valuable 
aspects of the other identified needs will be considered in the further development, without 
trying to solve all needs and thus loosing focus and limiting the usability of the support tool.

5.1 Introduction to Support Tool Development 
This chapter presents the support tool development stage of this thesis. The aim of the 
development is to create the most meaningful analysis support tool intended to have great 
impact for designers within the behavioural design field.
Section 5.2 (the first phase of the support tool development) deals with Value Proposition 
Design (Osterwalder et al. 2015) applied as means for identification of the designer’s needs 
for usability and practical application most essential to incorporate in the support tool design. 
Section 5.3 sets out the Specification Requirements that the support tool must address to add 
value for the designer. Finally, section 5.4 (the second phase of the support tool development) 
presents the ideation and conceptualisation phase followed by section 5.5 (the third phase of 
the support tool development) that presents the detailing and iterations leading to the final 
concept of the support tool.
The support tool development process (figure 5.1) has followed general design practice 
with the application of different general design methods and models (mentioned when 
applied). Throughout the entire support tool development stage, workshops have been 
performed, ensuring continuous involvement of the user (designer) to constantly develop a 
user orientated solution and obtain a common understanding of what is important.

Customer profile
According to the methodology of Value Proposition Design (Osterwalder et al. 2015), 
the first step is to create a Value Proposition Canvas (including customer jobs, pains, and 
gains, figure 5.2 and appendix 9) by describing the customer profile and value map of a given 
product/service, in this case the analysis support tool. By ranking and relating the different 
jobs, pains and gains with the initial support tool aims (found in section 4.3.6), the aspect of 
when will the tool features have effect for whom was shown. Visualising the pains showed 
where the designers are struggling, and hence where they would be willing to apply an 
analysis support tool to provide substantiated value. Although there were many findings from 
this activity, the ones highlighted were:
• The need for clear structure (steps) and validation of decisions.
• The need for more diverse behavioural theory selecting possibilities.
• The need for fast ways to obtain an overview of the behavioural theories within 
the behavioural design field.
5.2.1 Value Proposition Canvas
5.2 Value Proposition Design 
Through an investigation of current practice (chapter 4) different needs in regards to 
behavioural theories, challenges and project staging were identified followed by a determination 
of the support tool focus: An analysis tool. However, as the investigation focused on the 
practical reflections of theoretically derived findings (from the literature review, chapter 
2), identification of different design needs regarding usability and practical application was 
necessary. In order to identify such design needs, the methods applied in the first phase of 
the support tool development were Value Proposition Design (Osterwalder et al. 2015), 
as well as User Scenarios (Cross 2008). However, to challenge own thesis bias and ensure 
objectiveness and inputs, two workshops were performed to support the first phase of the 
support tool development (see appendix 8 for information and contributions). 
In the second step of the Value Proposition Canvas, the value map (figure 5.3 left on the 
following page (58) and appendix 10) was generated to determine the expected value of 
the features in the analysis support tool, in terms of fulfilling the different design needs such 
as usability, flow and group context. Matching the value map (figure 5.3 right on the following 
page (58), appendix 11) with the customer profile was quite valuable, as this was the first 
step towards an elaboration of the specific analysis support tool and its determination of 
==PAGE:70==
58
Following the above validation of the match between the problem (i.e. needs) and the 
solution (i.e. analysis support tool), the next step in the optimal scenario would be to validate 
the match of the product (i.e. analysis support tool) with the market (i.e. the designers) 
(Osterwalder et al. 2015). However, due to time limitations and unforeseeable aspects in 
the design consultancies (such as project progress), matching the analysis support tool with 
designers in real ongoing behavioural design projects was not possible. 
The method of User Scenarios (Cross 2008) was applied to validate the match between 
the analysis support tool and the designers, and to substitute market observations. Having 
identified characteristics and benefits of the analysis support tool from the Value Proposition 
Canvas, different scenarios relevant to the analysis support tool context were generated and 
tested (see figure 5.4 or appendix 12) and as such the market context was simulated.
5.2.2 User Scenarios
Doing so allowed for an investigation of the actions and needs of each scenario. Essential to 
this method was the visualisation of the advantages and disadvantages of the analysis support 
tool, as well as its feasibility and relevance (i.e. on what level does the tool address the 
identified research aim). Finally, to relate the findings from the user scenario method with the 
findings from value proposition design, a Value Proposition Statement (Osterwalder et al. 
2015, pp.82–83) was formulated to translate the meaning of the identified needs into clear 
value statements. In this way, the aspects that would create most value regarding the given 
scenarios were explicitly visualised. Thus, a combination of the current practice reflection of

requirements. Interesting findings from this activity were:
• The features must be updatable and customisable (the “fluffier” the better).
• The features must translate theoretical insights from behavioural theories into 
further behavioural design activities.
• The features must be intuitive and easy-to-use.
==PAGE:71==
 59
theoretically derived findings from section 4.3 and the more design-oriented findings from 
section 5.2.1-5.2.2 resulted in the following value proposition statement for the analysis 
support tool:
“An easy understandable step-by-step analysis support tool that helps designers with 
comprehensive experience of behavioural design challenge their tunnel vision in regards 
to theory inclusion, and helps designers with limited experience of behavioural design with 
validating their decision-making processes, by providing structured ways of doing 
problem analysis and by providing an overview of available theoretical behavioural 
perspectives”.
As the value proposition statement has a rather simple composition, four additional value 
points (found through the user scenarios) were added to enhance an extra level of detail to 
the above statement:  
1. Clear means for documentation.
2. A continuously updated overview of behavioural theories with examples.
3. Checklists (e.g. to see if the problem concerns behavioural design).
4. Continuous evaluation that provides insights of previous behavioural design projects.
As evaluation could add to the feeling of ownership of the analysis support tool among the 
designers, the evaluation part of the process was integrated in the tool focus as well.  As 
the aspect of evaluation could add more structured ways for designers of learning from 
experience, and thus minimising the risk of repeating mistakes. Moreover, if the analysis 
support tool helps the designers recognize where their practice could be optimized, then the 
motivation to use it for next behavioural design project is much higher. Yet, evaluation was 
still regarded as a secondary focus of the analysis support tool, and in the case of non-
strengthening manifestation it can be eliminated from the analysis support tool.
To visualise the impact of the analysis support tool, the following figure 5.5 shows how the 
application of the tool is expected to change current work practice of designers with both 
limited or comprehensive experience of behavioural design (see appendix 13).

5.3 Tool requirements specification
Based on the findings from the Investigation of Current Practice (chapter 4) and value 
proposition design (section 5.2) the following Requirements Specification (Cross 2008) 
was formulated (see table 5.1) to determine the course of the upcoming conceptualisation 
as well as to provide guidelines for measuring solution quality of the analysis support tool.

5.4 Ideation & conceptualisation
Having identified different tool requirements, the next logic step of ideation and 
conceptualisation enabled going from random ideation sessions to determining a basic concept 
of the analysis support tool. For this, several methods such as analogies, open brainstorm, 
morphology chart (Cross 2008) was applied. Yet in order to receive inspiration and inputs 
regarding tool layout and interface, two workshops were performed to support the second 
phase of the support tool development (see appendix 15 for information and contributions).

It can appear a bit narrow to limit the ideation and conceptualisation to relate to these five 
steps only, however as the analysis support tool has a lot of different aims, this structure was 
preferred to ensure that all value-creating steps were considered.
5.4.2 Ideation
As ideation is a mixture of creative activities aimed at generating and communicating ideas, five 
different brainstorm activities took place to generate ideas in modes with an open solution 
space as well as a closed solution space. Although different ways of brainstorming were 
applied, each approach and its results (i.e. ideas) will not be explained in detail, merely 
presented to understand their connection and the combination of activities. 
The five different activities were: devising Analogies (about e.g. games, plays and ways to 
create overview) (Cross 2008), an Open Brainstorm (Cross 2008), a Guided Brainstorm 
(within the five content step), a Clustering of Ideas and as a prolonging another Guided 
Brainstorm (within each cluster to brainstorm for “missing” ideas). This combination of 
brainstorming activities was made to include unstructured ways of generating valuable ideas 
as well to fully explore the solution space parallel to the content steps determined to provide 
structure for the ideation activities. As an example Analogies and the Open Brainstorm was 
conducted to ensure immediate openness in regards to feature possibilities and to retrieve 
inspiration from other existing solutions to the same challenges. It worked well, as many ideas 
were generated, yet the approach also made it difficult to pinpoint specific tool functions and 
values. Therefore structured ways of brainstorming were needed as well. As an example, the 
Clustering of Ideas was applied as a brainstorming activity, due to the fact that the Guided 
Brainstorm began to show different correlations of idea clusters, hereunder maps, cards, 
5.4.1 Definition of analysis support tool steps
In order to create a strong concept for the analysis support tool and provide more structure 
for the ideation and conceptualisation, five overall tool content steps were clarified. They 
were based on the findings from the Literature Review (chapter 2), Investigation of Current 
Practice (chapter 4) and the design needs identification (section 5.2). In this way strong 
correlation between literature, current practice and design needs was ensured and translated 
into relatable ideation clusters. The five content steps of the analysis support tool were: 
==PAGE:74==
62
checklists, digital ideas, and games. Doing so allowed for another order of ideas and visualised 
different blind-spots in the idea generation. This was a closed approach for brainstorming, yet 
it allowed for more detail and therefore worked very well in this context.
All in all, this combination of brainstorming activities resulted in numerous specific and 
unspecific ideas applicable in the further activities in conceptualisation. 
5.4.3 Conceptualisation
The aim of this part of the support tool development was to transform the generated ideas 
into different concepts that would provide value for the designer in the analysis phase of a 
BDS. Therefore, based on the overall analysis support tool content steps (problem analysis, 
behaviour analysis, validation, translation of theoretical insights, and evaluation) and the clustering 
of ideas (maps, cards, checklists, digital ideas, and games) a Morphology Chart (Cross 2008) 
was conducted (figure 5.6). 
This resulted in 13 different concepts (most simple, most complex, designer with limited 
experience of behavioural design, designer with comprehensive experience of behavioural design, 
all game, all digital, all checklist, all maps, all cards, Beatrice’ favourite, Nanna’s favourite, fastest, 
and slowest). Especially combining ideas into the concepts for the designers with limited or 
comprehensive experience of behavioural design was valuable, as it provided a clear picture 
of what elements the concept should contain to satisfy both. For instance, the designer with 
comprehensive experience of behavioural design needed more detailed elements in the 
concept such as maps, whereas the designer with limited experience of behavioural design 
needed more checklists and guides. By comparing the 13 concepts, similarities and differences 
were highlighted and since some of the concepts had more in common than others, different 
combinations were made, reducing 13 concepts to eight concepts. From the similarities and 
differences, these eight concepts were placed within three categories: Output specific, content 
specific and context specific (application) (see table 5.3 on the following page (63)).
