Concept 6:  Maximum Documentation consists of problem behaviour mapping and ranking, followed 
by a spider-web to be filled out to ensure documentation of progress and decisions made and if 
relevant, can be returned to.
Concept 7: Maximum Validation consists of problem behaviour mapping followed by three checklists 
and a decision tree in order to ensure validation before moving on to each step.
Concept 8: Maximum Reflection aims at involving the designer through behaviour ranking, association 
cards etc. to obtain more knowledge and insights regarding behavioural theories after usage of the 
analysis support tool.
As the next step of the conceptualisation phase was to reduce eight concepts to one 
basic concept of the analysis support tool, a Weighted Objective Method (Cross 2008) 
combined with an Evaluation Matrix (Dalhousie University 2012) was used in order to 
evaluate the pros and cons of the eight concepts’ regarding the following sub-categories: 
Staging, problem analysis, behaviour analysis, and evaluation (appendix 16).
The criteria within each sub-category were formulated based on the specification 
requirements (table 5.1 and appendix 14) and the value proposition (figure 5.3 and 
appendices 9-11). At first, each criterion was weighted 1-3 with three being most important 
and one least important. Afterwards the eight concepts were given a score from 1-5 how 
much they fulfilled each criterion, with a five points score as the best and a one point score 
as the worst. Although the scoring was done subjectively, the scoring of the concepts took 
place over two different rounds, to secure that the results were as objective and dynamic 
as possible. In practice this meant that in the first round the concept was scored on its own 
(appendix 16, 1 of 2), while in the second round all concepts were scored in relation to each 
other (appendix 16, 2 of 2). Scoring the concepts in relation to each other ensured that the 
outcome and total score of the results would be in the same, regardless of the point system.
Applying this combination of methods provided structured ways of realising each individual 
concept’s strengths and weaknesses and from the method itself two concepts stood out: 
Maximum Documentation with 145 points (round 1) and 150 points (round 2) and Maximum 
Validation with 160 points (round 1) and 153 points (round 2). By breaking down each of 
the concepts sub-scores, it was possible to combine the two concepts into the strongest 
possible basic concept. Yet, despite the fact that the method stresses the choice of the highest 
scoring concept (Cross 2008), incorporations from other concepts were transferred into 
the basic concept as well. This was done both due to the fact that other concepts scored 
higher in certain sub-categories and due to the fact that a modification of ideas would secure 
a stronger link between the different concept steps. Hence, the basic concept was mainly 
based on ideas from the concepts Maximum Documentation and Maximum Validation, yet 
incorporation and modification of ideas from the other concepts has been made as well.
Evaluation of the concepts

The basic concept of the Super Cool Analysis Tool is a concept based on behavioural 
design literature (chapter 2), an investigation of current practice in industry (chapter 4) and 
Value Propostition Design (section 5.2). Yet, to make the Super Cool Analysis Tool ready for 
implementation in current behavioural design practice, more iterations and improvements in 
regards to flow and usability were needed.

5.5 Detailing & development
Throughout the third, and final, phase of the support tool development, nine workshops 
(see appendix 17 for information and contributions) were performed, all contributing to five 
overall iterations to ensure maximum usability and application in an industry context. 
Therefore, the basic concept has changed significantly to the final concept of the Super 
Cool Analysis Tool. In the basic concept the Super Cool Analysis Tool consisted of five steps 
described in table 5.4, including five different elements (Problem Analysis Template, Theory 
Cards, Theory Overview Wheel, a Validation Spider-web and Evaluation Template). The final 
concept of the Super Cool Analysis Tool only consists of four steps as the evaluation step has 
been eliminated to focus on the unique value-adding elements. In addition, the four remaining 
steps have been combined into only two elements: a Theory Card-set and a Theory Overview 
Wheel, to make it simpler, user friendly, and gamified. Despite this combination of steps, the 
Super Cool Analysis Tool still addresses all four steps (problem analysis, behaviour analysis, 
validation and translation of theoretical insights). In the following sections, the iterations are 
visualised and the key takeaways are highlighted.
• 
Step 1+4: The behavioural mapping element was eliminated and instead the problem 
analysis was combined into the Theory Cards by including problem layers and factors in 
the card tasks. Further, a “Force yourself” task was included on the backside of each Theory 
Card to provide guidance of how to apply the behavioural theory in a specific context.
• 
Step 2: Previously the Theory Overview Wheel only showed the behavioural theories’ 
relations to the three categories described in the literature review in section 2.3.2 and 
2.3.3 (named as individual models of behaviour, social models of behaviour and theories 
of change): Individual behaviour, social behaviour, and behaviour change. Now it is included 
to show the relations of the different behavioural theories in regards to different topics 
derived from the 10 keywords in tables 2.1, 2.2 and 2.3 describing the core of each of the 
elaborated behavioural theories. This visualisation of the behavioural theories enables a 
common language (applicable between different professions) and communicates complex

social impact, self-efficacy, habits, and principles of behaviour) was made, as it was assessed to 
enable better means for usability.

5.6 Conclusion & key findings of the 
Support Tool Development
After ideation, conceptualisation and several workshops, the final concept, the Super Cool 
Analysis Tool was developed with the aim of making designers with limited experience 
of behavioural design build a theoretical back-catalogue, while at the same time challenging 
designers’ with comprehensive experience of behavioural design tunnel vision in regards to 
theory inclusion. Hence, the Super Cool Analysis tool is found to be meaningful and have 
great impact for designers within the behavioural design field with respect to the research 
framework. The key findings of this chapter are therefore:
• In the beginning of the second support tool development phase, five different tool 
content steps (problem analysis, behaviour analysis, validation, translation of theoretical 
insights, and evaluation) were determined to be vital in the final concept of the 
Super Cool Analysis Tool. However, through iterations the steps were combined 
dynamically to create better flow and coherence of the tool activities. Yet, despite 
the combination of the steps, all value-adding parts of the initial step activities are still 
part of the final tool features, just not as separate tasks.
• Through the investigation of current practice (chapter 4), evaluation was highlighted 
as being a possible value-adding aspect to the Super Cool Analysis Tool to the 
benefit of all kinds of designers. Yet, through the iterations it was clarified that the 
evaluation step did not provide any unique value to the concept, as the core of the 
concept rather were the Theory Cards, the Theory Overview Wheel and the flow in 
between them. Therefore, evaluation was eliminated from the further development 
of the Super Cool Analysis Tool to achieve greater focus and proof of concept of 
the truly unique tool elements. With that being said, evaluation is still acknowledged 
as being potentially valuable when having applied the Super Cool Analysis Tool, yet 
evaluation could be achieved from other tools and methods separated from the tool.
Other key findings are of course the features and elements of the final concept of the Super 
Cool Analysis tool to be described and elaborated in detail in chapter 6.
The final concept of the Super Cool Analysis Tool is described in details in the following chapter 6, yet 
the key takeaways of the final iteration were:
• 
Step 1+4: The aim of this iteration was to ensure better adaption to an industry context 
achieved through incorporating more gamification elements (e.g. time pressure, output in 
all tasks and staging considerations). Next, the Guide Book was updated and modified to 
ensure greater usability, among other things, by introducing a table of behavioural design 
approaches applicable after playing the game. Finally, the Theory Cards were updated and 
modified sufficiently as well to make the content more correct and more understandable.
Final concept of the Super Cool Analysis Tool

6.1 Introduction to the Super Cool Analysis Tool 
This chapter presents the Super Cool Analysis Tool which is a gamified analysis tool primarily 
developed for designers in behavioural design projects, yet applicable for other professions 
involved in behavioural design projects as well.
Section 6.2 provides an overall description of the Super Cool Analysis Tool, as well as visualises 
its content. Section 6.3-6.7 presents each of the steps in detail regarding purpose, tool activities, 
preparation, procedure, outcome, and considerations/limitations for each step. Finally, section 6.8 
reflects on accessibility and ownership of the Super Cool Analysis Tool followed by section 
6.9 exemplifying the different use cases in which the tool can be applied. All tool materials 
can be found in appendices 18-23 which are strongly recommended to be looked at when 
going through section 6.3-6.7.

6.2 Overall support tool description
The Super Cool Analysis Tool is a front-end gamified analysis support tool for the early stages 
of a behavioural design project (such as define and discover in the Double Diamond Model 
(Design Council 2007)). The Super Cool Analysis Tool helps designers in making a thorough 
problem behaviour analysis, while at the same time enabling better validation of the analysis and 
ensuring translatable documentation along the analysis steps. Based on easy-understandable 
behavioural theory examples and an overview of the theories’ relations, structured ways of 
doing problem analysis and challenging current behaviour analysis routines are obtained. In 
practice this is obtained by a 60-90 minutes game consisting of the five main steps visualised 
in the table 6.1. The Super Cool Analysis Tool is distinguished as a gamified tool, as it consists 
of several game elements: Clear purpose (where achieving this purpose means winning and 
ending the game), means, players (that actively participate and make decisions), limiting context 
(rules), conflicts, and quantifiable outcome (Salen & Zimmerman 2004) (further elaborated in 
section 6.3-6.7). 
Table 6.1
Steps of the Super 
Cool Analysis Tool
The intended use of the Super Cool Analysis Tool recommends going through all steps (0 to 
4), yet to keep from restraining the usage, each of the steps can be operated on a stand-alone 
==PAGE:85==
 73
basis and provides great value in itself as they are fitted with a sub-aim of single usage. 
The following figure 6.1 visualises the overall flow and contents of the gamified Super Cool 
Analysis Tool described in details in the subsequent sections 6.3-6.7. To ease the reading the 
tool will be labelled as a “game”, and the users of the tool will be labelled as “player(s)” in the 
subsequent sections 6.3-6.7.

The Super Cool Analysis Tool is developed from a design point of view where it is desired to 
transition from a comprehensive problem understanding to a solution, and hence the primary 
intended users are designers (ranging from designers with limited experience of behavioural 
design to designers with comprehensive experience of behavioural design). Despite this

6.3 Step 0: Preparation & instructions
A quick guide to start the game: 
Although we recommend you (or at least one from your group) to read this Guide Book 
before playing the game, the following quick guide explains how to start the game quickly: 
• 
Place the Theory Overview Wheel so that all players can see it, and give each 
player their own set of Theory Cards (at all times all players must discuss the same 
Theory Card)
• 
Based on the chosen game-mode (page 6), you (as a group) should work your way 
thorugh the Theory Cards, meaning: 
1. 
Read the brief description and the relating bias
2. 
Read and understand the applied problem case
3. 
Complete the “force yourself” task
4. 
Complete the three remember-tasks on the back of the Theory Card
5. 
Go through the decision-tree and determine further steps of the game
6. 
Finally, again depending on your chosen game-mode, reach the “End Card” and 
finish the game successfully! 
• 
In order to make sure that you progress successfully through the game, keep the 
following two time frames in mind: 
1. 
If you struggle for more than five minutes in trying to formulate a hypothesis 
in relation to the theory – move on to the ne[t Theory Card stated on the 
backside of the current Theory Card.
2. 
Flip the hour-glass everytime you pick a new Theory Card – try not to spend 
more than 10 minutes on each card (you can always come back to it!)

1
How to understand the game
What is the Super Cool Analysis Tool? The Super Cool 
Analysis Tool is a game aimed at helping you make better problem analysis and 
behaviour hypotheses applicable in your design projects concerning Behavioural 
Design (or Design for Behaviour Change). The aim of the game is supported 
by the belief that in order to develop the most successful solutions, you have to 
achieve a deep level of problem understanding first. This is a crucial aspect to keep 
in mind in regards to the aim of the game, as you otherwise may misunderstand 
the game and therefore not achieve the output you hoped for. 
Who should apply this game? The game can be played by any 
kind of designer, yet it is the perfect game for 
• 
Experienced behavioural designers who wish broaden their horizon 
with new behavioural theories and 
• 
Designers with no or limited experience with behavioural design who 
wish to explore the (many!) benefits behavioural analysis have to offer 
different kinds of design work or to obtain relevant insights into 
human behaviour.
Although the game’s imagined user is a designer, the tool’s language and tasks could 
complement users of other professions as well (such as behavioural economist, 
sociologists, psycohologist). No matter the profession, the game can be played by 
either individuals or groups, however we recommend you to be maximum four 
people playing the game (otherwise split the team into two groups). This is due to 
the fact that being more than four people in one group will require more time to 
reach the same level of output, and thus the duration will be more than 90 minutes.
When should the game be played? As the game helps 
you  make better problem analysis and behaviour hypotheses, the game should 
2
Why should I play this game? The Super Cool Analysis Tool 
will help you by providing an overview of different possibly relevant behavioural 
theories and the insights each of them could offer you, as well as providing better 
possibilities of translating these insights into your design work (by helping you 
formulate and rank different problem hypotheses that could apply to your project).  
be played in the early-phase of your design 
work – more specificly in between the 
discovery and the definition of the problem 
presented in your design project (as you 
can see in the Double Diamond figure (Design 
Council 2007)).

NB! Although a facilitator is not stated as a necessity, using one when playing would 
probably ensure better results and more structure of the game. Therefore, if you wish to use 
a facilitator, (s)he should consider the following things before playing: 
• 
Have I read and understood the game? (it will save you time while playing!) 
• 
How big is the team who should play the game? Do we need to split the team into smaller 
groups?
• 
Am I open-minded in regards to the output of the tool? 
• 
How many times should we play the game? 
• 
How do we ensure the needed documentation? Who is responsible for transferring the 
results from the game into the project we are working on? 
How to prepare and play the game
5
Preparation for the game: 
1. 
Fold out the Theory Overview Wheel and place it for all to see.
2. 
Fold out the disc-bag and together with the Begin Card, Problem 
Documentation Card and End Card put it next to the Theory Overview 
Wheel.
3. 
Give each player their own set of Theory Cards.
4. 
Look through the game-modes and determine how you wish to enter 
the game. 
Playing the game: While the game-modes determines how you 
enter the game, the following steps describes how the game is played out: 
1. 
Flip the (10 minutes) hour-glass everytime you pick a new Theory Card. 
2. 
Read the brief description and the relating bias on the chosen Theory 
Card.
3. 
Read and understand the applied problem case
4. 
Complete the “force yourself” task in relation to your project problem.
5. 
Complete the three °remember±-tasNs on the back of the Theory Card:
6. 
Write your hypothesis on a post-it.
7. 
Write the theory-letter on your post-it (to backtrack your decisions).
8. 
Cover the theory letters with the discs (to realise progress).
9. 
Go through the decision-tree on the back of the Theory Card and 
determine the further steps of the game.
10. You should be able to move through one Theory Card in ma[imum 10 
minutes. If it takes you longer, try to wrap it up and move on. You can 
always come back to it later! 
11. Once enough cards have been considered (the game will tell you when that 
is), reach the End Card and finish the game successfully!
NB! Each of the Theory Cards has a task, which “forces” you/your group to formulate a 
hypothesis that should describe how a given theory applies to your problem (if you prefer 
two hypotheses, you can of course write both of them). An example of a hypothesis could be 
“people throw cigarette butts at the train platforms, due to a convenience need and due to time 
pressure”. For the Theory Cards it is important that you attempt to formulate a hypothesis, 
yet if you struggle for more than five minutes in trying to make sense of a specific theory 
in relation to your problem – move on to the ne[t Theory Card (found on the back of 
the card)  – it is not a nessecity to formulate a hypothesis from all theories, it is merely the 
reflection over each theory’s relevance that is important.

NB! When choosing game-mode 2 the Begin Card prompts you to determine whether 
your project concerns the behaviour of an individual or of a group. This can be a tricky question 
to answer, as there is the possibility that your problem relates to both kinds of behaviours. So,

How to end the game
7
Ending the game: Based on the game you have now formulated 
several hypotheses relevant to the behavioural problem you are trying 
to adress. Based on these hypotheses, the End Card will guide you 
through the task of ending the game.
This task will help you/your group to reflect upon the results from the 
game and ultimately help you transfer the insights you have obtained from 
playing the game into your further design work.  
Once you agree that you have the best hypotheses you wish to apply 
in your further activities, remember to: 
• 
Take the Theory Cards (from which your chosen hypotheses 
have been formulated). 
• 
Consider the key takeaways each individual Theory Card has 
to offer in your furhter design process.
• 
To provide even more inspiration for the further design 
process, the table on the backpage of this Guide Book 
shows different design approaces that could possibly benefit 
you and your project! 
How to update the game
Why would I want to update the game? Although the 
game has been developed to fit different companies, projects and problems, the 
possibility of updating the game has been included in the case that:
• 
You are super-experienced at behavioural design and would like to apply 
the game, yet some of the theories that you find to be very valuable in your 
projects are not included in the Theory Cards.
• 
You aren’t super-experienced at behavioural design, yet you would still like 
to learn and expand your behavioural theory back-catalogue – and to do so 
fully, you need the possibility of adding other theories as well.
Luckily, we have taken these scenarios into account and made it super simple 
for you to customise the Super Cool Analysis Tool to fit your context
How do I add new theory cards? Adding Theory Cards to the 
game is actually very simple and intuitive, all you have to do is: 
• 
)ind the empty template Theory Cards and fill in the information from 
the given theory you wish to add. To do so, you have to realise whether 
the theory is describing individual behaviour, social behaviour or 
behaviour change. You also have to reflect upon which of the eight 
topics the theory refers to. 
• 
Give the new theory a letter at the top of the Theory Card (a letter that 
has not been used for the other Theory Cards). If this is the first theory 
card you are adding, the letter would be V.
• 
All the Theory Cards are linked to each other, meaning that they are all a 
part of a big chain. When adding a new Theory Card you must therefore 
find the right place in the chain. To do so, there are only two simple 
rules to follow: 
1. 
The same card colour (individual, social or change) must not be next to 
each other in the chain – i.e. the Theory Cards before and after must be 
different than the one you are adding. 
2. 
The Theory Card can only have one topic in common which the Theory 
Cards next to it, i.e. the Theory Cards before and after should be 
somewhat describing another behaviour context. 
• 
Once you have found the place in the chain, be sure to remember to 
update the card-linNing bo[es on the Theory Card before and the 
Theory Card after - so the chain will not be broken when you play the 
game! 
• 
Finally, all you have to do is add the Theory Card to the Theory Overview 
Wheel. Make sure to place it in the correct circle – i.e. in the correct topics 
and in the correct category of behavioural theories. 
• 
Once this is done, you have customised the game and are good to 
go again! 
8
How to understand the Theory Cards
1. Symbol and colour illustrating the Theory 
Card category. 
2. Letter illustrating the Theory Card “no.” and its 
placement on the Theory Overview Wheel. 
Name of the behavioural theory. 
Brief theoretical description of the theory.
A reference, if interest in the specific theory has 
been awoken and further information is wanted.
A behaviour bias, relevant to either the theory 
on the card or the problem case on the card. Biases 
are easy-to-understand phrases developed to describe 
a certain type of behaviour - they are therefore great 
to consider in regards to your problem as well.  
An example of a case, to show how the theory 
could be valuable in a specific context. Do not let 
this example limit the application of the theory – it 
is merely meant as a clearer way to understand the 
theory. Even though your problem in no way relates 
to the problem example on the card, the theory 
can still be valuable! 
Together with each problem case is an illustration 
relating to either the way the theory works or showing 
the figure originally formulated together with the theory. 
Social Identity Theory
%pplied pVSFleQ case
Why are people eating more healthy things at home 
than they are at work? 
For a problem like this, environmental context 
is valuable to consider, yet explaining it from the 
perspective of Social Identity Theory adds 
another layer of knowledge. The roles which the 
employees take part in influences the behaviours. 
Perhaps the wife is super active and will only eat 
salad, which leads to the husband eating healthier 
at home, and perhaps “relaxing” a bit on the 
health-front at work (or opposite). Or maybe, the 
majority of the employees are eating healthy, 
and in order to not be “outside” of the 
group, the husband eats healthy at work – but 
not at home, where his wife loves pizza and pasta.
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can your problem be explained by either ingroup behaviour 
or out-group behaviour?
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Social Identity Theory offers insights as to why individuals 
strive to achieve or maintain a positive social identity. It states that individuals act on behalf 
of (in-)group concerns (being part of the group) and not on behalf of personal concerns. 
Individuals, when interacting within a certain group context, acquire different social roles, or 
“levels of self”, which influences their behaviour. The theory distinguishes between interpersonal 
behaviour (two or more individuals acting solely on the grounds of individual concerns and 
characteristics, e.g. wife and husband) and intergroup behaviour (two or more individuals that 
behave solely on the grounds of social concerns and characteristics, e.g. soldiers in an army 
group).
Reference: 
Tajfel, H. & Turner, J., 1979. An Integrative Theory of Intergroup Conflicts. In The Social Psychology of Intergroup Relations. 
Brooks Cole Publishing, pp. 33–47.

The purpose of step 0 is, as the name indicates, to prepare and to get instructed for the game. 
The tasks in this step are prior to the actual game, to indicate that some level of preparation 
is needed before playing the game. Therefore, it is always recommended to go through step 0 
when playing the game for the first time, despite the possibility of applying all steps separately. 
First, the player(s) must read the Guide Book (see appendix 18) to get an understanding 
of the game as it describes the game in detail and provides the player(s) with guidelines and 
6.3.1 Purpose
Figure 6.2
Game contents of 
step 0: Guide Book
intended use, the Super Cool Analysis Tool can also be applied by other professions in a 
behavioural design project team, due to the tool’s application of non-profession dependent/
specific language. Further, the Super Cool Analysis Tool facilitates both individual usage as well 
as group usage. The tool is based on the belief that theory driven behavioural design results 
in the most successful interventions. Hence, the purpose of the Super Cool Analysis Tool is 
to build a theoretical back-catalogue for designers with limited experience of behavioural 
design and to challenge tunnel vision of theory inclusion of designers with comprehensive 
experience of behavioural design. Doing so will create the best foundation for successful 
development of behavioural design interventions for both kinds of designers. While this is the 
overall purpose of the Super Cool Analysis Tool, the actual purpose of the game itself is to, 
in maximum 90 minutes, create three specific, realistic and feasible hypotheses (describing a 
problem behaviour of a specific behavioural design project) that are transferrable and useful 
in the following behavioural design activities.
The Guide Book
This Guide Book will help you through the game, visualise 
game elements and give instructions for the usage both before, 
during and after the game
1-4 players
60-90 min.
==PAGE:87==
 75
instructions of the game. Thus, here the player(s) can get familiar with the purpose of the 
game and means provided to obtain this purpose as well as the rules structuring and limiting 
the activities of the game. The Guide Book focuses on usability, and is consistent with clear 
instructions (e.g. “quick guide for starting the game” on page 1 in the Guide Book) and any 
information necessary/nice to know about the game (e.g. “explanatory guide for the theory card 
elements” on page 9-10 in the Guide Book). Both elements are necessary to communicate 
as the game has to stand alone and be well communicated for others to apply. The Guide 
Book ensures this guidance through easy-understandable content explanations, such as step 
by step elements and a clear flow to measure progress in the creation of hypotheses. For 
instance, the hypothesis that theory driven behavioural design results in the most successful 
interventions (section 2.2.5) is in the Guide Book rewritten as “in order to develop the best 
solutions, you have to achieve a deep level of problem understanding”. Due to the fact that it 
was found that behavioural design differs a lot within and across design consultancies (section 
4.3.3), flexibility of the game is ensured by introducing a section, which sets up rules of how 
to update and customise the game (page 8 in the Guide Book). Likewise, the flexibility of 
the game is ensured by having three different game-modes (see page 6 in the Guide Book): 
Game-mode 1 deals with a vaguely-specified problem, whereas game-mode 2 deals with a 
well-specified problem. Furthermore, an inspiration game-mode (3) is introduced to extend 
the usage sphere. However, game-mode 3 has no intended structure or flow, and hence 
the following description of the game steps in this thesis are based on game-mode 1 and 
2 (despite the fact that the descriptions could somewhat be applicable for game-mode 3). 
Together the understanding of the game and its elements obtained from the Guide Book, 
combined with the choice of game-mode, form the foundation of proceeding to step 1. 
This step 0 is carried out by the Guide Book. Even though it can seem extensive to read 
the Guide Book, it is a necessity to ensure the intended game flow, successful experience, and 
outcome. However, a quick guide on page 1 in the Guide Book makes it possible to start the 
game immediately and use the Guide Book on an ad hoc basis during the game. 
6.3.2 Preparation
6.3.3 Step by step procedure & outcome
Due to the fact that step 0 is a preparation step for the game no preparations are needed 
other than determining whether the design project concerns behaviour or not. Obviously, 
this is an important aspect to clarify before playing the game, as the game otherwise will be 
irrelevant to play.
1. Determine whether to read the full instructions before playing the game, or skip 
the full instructions and start the game from the “quick guide for starting the game” 
instructions on page 1. If the full instructions have been skipped, the following 
procedure steps (2-6) are skipped as well (yet, visited if necessary).
2. Read page 2 to get an understanding of the game and its aim, while determining 
the number of players and the duration of the game.

The purpose of step 1 is to start a systematic reflection of the problem to be analysed in the 
behavioural design project and to lead this reflection into the game. First, the player(s) must 
discuss the preliminary problem perception to define the root causes and the target group 
of the problem behaviour. This is done by discussing how the seven different problem layers 
(section 2.3.1, appendix 19) affect the problem behaviour. This preliminary problem analysis is 
introduced as a starting task to get the players to systemise their observations (made prior 
to the game, elaborated in section 6.4.2). This will start a discussion of the perceived target

6.3.4 Considerations & recommendations
First, it is recommended to consider whether a facilitator would be beneficial to the game or 
not. If so, the player who takes on the facilitator role, should read the Guide Book thoroughly 
prior to playing the game, as well as plan the setup of the game. A well-prepared facilitator will 
ensure better flow and structure of the game to the benefit of the output. On page 4 of the 
Guide Book a facilitator check-list of elements to be considered before playing is visualised.
Begin Card
front
*iVsX XLiRgs fiVsX Since you have chosen to play this game, we imagine that you 
are faced with a problem concerning some type of behaviour – or at least you think so!
The following task will get you started in the structuring and analysis of the problem behaviour 
that you have identified. This task will ease the usability of the game and is therefore 
valuable to the process, but if you are short of time or already have a rather thorough 
understanding of your problem, you can skip this task and go straight to the back of this card. 
If not, start the game by identifying how the following seven problem layers affect 
the problem individually (based on you initial perception of the problem). If there are any 
problem layers in which you cannot identify your problem, don’t worry – the game should 
guide you through them anyway. As a group fill out the problem layers you are able to identify 
on post-its and place them on the front of the “Problem Documentation card”. 
Once this is done, you can go to the back of the Begin Card and start the game!  
8Le seZeR pVSFleQ layeVs aVe
Personal problems relate to internal personal factors such 
as health and psyche.
Impersonal problems relate to external personal factors 
such as lack of money and time.
Interpersonal problems relate to internal social factors 
such as arguing with a friend or disagreeing with a study group, 
which can be seen as in-group behaviour.
Societal problems relate to external social factors such as 
crime rates, littering, and pollution, which can be seen as out-group 
behaviour.
Immediate story refers to the things prior to when the 
behaviour occurs, i.e. the bus was late, spilled coffee, or a broken 
phone.
Longterm background refers to the mood, place in life, 
environment, or childhood experiences of the individual.
Frequency of change refers to the span or frequency of 
the problem behaviour, i.e. is it an seldom behaviour or a frequent 
behaviour?
==PAGE:89==
 77
groups (individual vs. social behaviour), as well as a discussion of whether a limited or a deep 
perception of the problem’s root causes (number of considered layers) is the case. Doing 
so will both enable the player(s) in understanding the aim of the game, as well as ease the 
task of formulating more focused hypotheses in the game activities to follow (elaborated in 
section 5.2.2). Additionally, this task can help the player(s) realise gaps in both the obtained 
observations and in the current way of analysing a problem behaviour (section 4.3.6). Once 
the discussion of the preliminary problem perception has been made, the player(s) must 
discuss and choose the path leading them into the game – i.e. choosing the starting point of 
step 2. This task is introduced to enable some degree of freedom in terms of customisation, 
to ensure that different companies can engage in the game in the way perceived to be most 
useful for them. The path links to the discussion of the problem perception, as the path 
choices refer to the findings from the discussion (vaguely-specified problem versus well-
specified problem, individual behaviour versus group behaviour).  
Step 1 is carried out by the Begin Card (see appendix 19), while the documentation of 
the step is supported by the Problem Documentation Card (see appendix 21), 
post-its and pens to ensure that the player(s) will be able to reflect upon the insights 
retrieved from playing the game. The Problem Documentation Card enables a comparison 
of the problem perception before and after playing the game (due to the fact that the 
Problem Documentation Card is also applied at the end of the game). Finally, the role of 
Problem Documentation Card supports a sense of productivity of the player(s), as it provides 
indicators to measure progress.
6.4.2 Preparation
In order to make the best initial problem analysis, the player(s) need(s) to have an initial 
understanding of the behavioural problem in question. Hence, to minimise guessing and 
pave the way for a focused process, it is strongly recommended to gather observations and 
statistical data of the problem behaviour (as current practice elaborated in section 4.3.1). 
However, as design projects often struggle with project duration, goal indicators and project 
costs (Ulrich & Eppinger 2012) the initial problem analysis can based on assumptions. However, 
this can affect the output of the game and is therefore a trade-off to discuss before playing 
the game. If possible, behavioural mapping could, parallel to the data collection activities, 
be beneficial to progress, as this will enable an even more thorough understanding of the 
problem. Yet, behavioural mapping is no easy task, and since the game guides the player(s) 
through structured ways of viewing and relating behaviour, behavioural mapping is not a 
required preparation, but a beneficial element.
6.4.3 Step by step procedure & outcome
1. Discuss and, once agreement is reached among the players, state the overall 
behavioural problem that the behavioural design project deals with. 
==PAGE:90==
78
2. Use the Begin Card (front) for reading and understand each of the seven problem 
layers. 
3. Discuss and identify how the seven problem layers are visualised in the given 
behavioural problem. Keep in mind that not all problem layers will seem relevant 
to the given problem behaviour. 
4. Once a layer has been identified, fill out a post-it describing the problem layer in the 
given problem behaviour context and place it on the Problem Documentation 
Card (front). 
5. When all layers have been discussed, go to the back of the Begin Card and go 
through the decision path, while focusing on answering the questions based on the 
previous discussions. 
6. Depending on the chosen path, the next step will be:
a. If the Begin Card states to start with either a yellow or a green Theory Card, 
pick such a card from the deck of Theory Cards. Keep in mind that all player(s) 
must go through the same card at once.
b. If the Begin Card states to assess the Theory Overview Wheel go to the Theory 
Overview Wheel and choose the theory found to have most relevance, based 
on the discussion that has just taken place. The choice of the first Theory 
Card should be based on the categories of theories (only the green individual 
behaviour cards or yellow social behaviour cards can be chosen as a starting 
point), as well as on the eight different topics.
The outcome of step 1 is an initial problem analysis and a chosen Theory Card for starting 
the problem behaviour analysis.
6.4.4 Considerations, limitations & alternatives
First, both the Begin Card and the Problem Documentation Card refers to the seven problem 
layers identified and described in section 2.3.1, yet the definition in the literature review 
refers only to six different layers. This is due to the fact that the layer “Story & Background” is 
divided into two separate layers in the game, as it offers a greater level of detail. The variance 
in the number of problem layers is looked upon as ignorable, with no implication for the 
level of detail in the literature review. Next, as stated on both the Begin Card and in the 
procedure steps, it is only possible to start the game with either a green or a yellow card. 
This is due to the fact that the output of the game was found to be at a higher level if the 
player(s) tried to understand the behaviour, before trying to figure out how to change it. 
This is a limitation of game-mode 2 (section 6.3.1) as it enables freedom in the game setting, 
which is then constrained by eliminating one of the theory categories (behaviour change) 
for the selection of a Theory Card. An alternative could be to open game-mode 2 up for 
choosing behaviour change theories as a first Theory Card, especially since the problem 
understanding in this game-mode should be quite substantial at this step.
==PAGE:91==
 79
6.5 Step 2: Problem behaviour analysis
Figure 6.4
Game contents of 
step 2: Theory Cards 
(front pages)
The purpose of step 2 is to, by using the Theory Cards as means (see appendix 22), 
create a thorough problem behaviour analysis by discussing different behavioural theories 
and reflecting upon their application in regards to the given problem behaviour context. This 
will allow the player(s) to create focused and valuable hypotheses (as already practiced in 
industry, section 4.3.1), each one explaining the problem behaviour in different contexts and 
ways. Although, some designers are not aware that they formulate hypotheses in general 
design projects, it is a task that is highly valuable, as formulating hypotheses to a given problem 
provides an explanation of the problem (based on theory) that directly reflects the devised 
solution (Boundless 2016; Koskinen et al. 2011). Thus, the game enables an already occurring 
activity of general design. First, the player(s) must understand a chosen behavioural theory and 
thereafter discuss it in relation to its appropriateness and relevance regarding the behavioural 
problem at hand. Here a game element of conflict can occur because if the specific Theory 
Card is not relevant for the player(s)’ problem behaviour, the Theory Card is an obstacle 
preventing the player(s) from easily achieving the goal of the game. Next, the purpose of 
the discussion is to create a hypothesis describing the problem behaviour in a very specific 
behavioural context (e.g. in regards to the aspects of intrinsic and extrinsic motivation, Deci & 
Ryan 2000). In order to ensure a high level of theoretical presence in the game, 21 behavioural 
theories in the categories individual behaviour (section 2.3.2.1), social behaviour (section 2.3.2.2) 
and behaviour change (section 2.3.3.1) are presented via three different colours of Theory 
Cards (green, yellow and purple). The different elements of the Theory Cards have been 
introduced to ease the understanding (e.g. a brief theoretical description) and translation 
of the theory into practice (e.g. an applied problem case), while at the same time giving 
the player(s) an in-depth understanding of the specific theory. Finally, the formulation of 
6.5.1 Purpose
Elaboration Likelihood Model
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How do I make sure that people do not smoke on 
planes?
The central route is efficient to use, once you 
have a person’s attention. Therefore it could be 
valuable to state that smoking is forbidden as a 
part of the security procedure in the beginning of 
the flight (i.e. when people are already reasoning 
with your logic). However, if the person is not 
paying attention to what you are saying, the 
peripheral route should be used. A way to do 
so, could be through small distractions leading to 
the same conclusions (e.g. through small pocket-
books stressing the dangers of smoking on planes 
or through strategically placed icons in the most 
obvious places).
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can the behaviour in your own problem case be changed by addressing 
either the central processing route or the peripheral processing route?
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Elaboration Likelihood Model presents two different 
strategies of influencing the user through different processing routes.  The theory states that 
persuasion can happen via two types of processing routes: The central route (much cognitive 
processing, i.e. rational thinking) and the peripheral route (little cognitive processing, i.e. 
automatic responses). The idea in this theory is that behaviour is guided by attitude, which 
can be influenced by persuasion (e.g. through motivation and ability or context and message). 
Therefore the theory is valuable when considering how to enable a certain behaviour change, 
either through communication of a message or through the placement of an intervention.
Reference: 
Petty, R.E. & Cacioppo, J.T., 1986. The Elaboration Likelihood Model of Persuasion. Advances in Experimental Social 
Psychology, 19, pp.123–205.
G
        A bias relating to the theory on this card is the Framing Effect bias:  
             Drawing different conclusions from the same information, depending on how that 
information is presented

Why are people eating more healthy things at home 
than they are at work? 
For a problem like this, environmental context 
is valuable to consider, yet explaining it from the 
perspective of Social Identity Theory adds 
another layer of knowledge. The roles which the 
employees take part in influences the behaviours. 
Perhaps the wife is super active and will only eat 
salad, which leads to the husband eating healthier 
at home, and perhaps “relaxing” a bit on the 
health-front at work (or opposite). Or maybe, the 
majority of the employees are eating healthy, 
and in order to not be “outside” of the 
group, the husband eats healthy at work – but 
not at home, where his wife loves pizza and pasta.
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can your problem be explained by either ingroup behaviour 
or out-group behaviour?
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Social Identity Theory offers insights as to why individuals 
strive to achieve or maintain a positive social identity. It states that individuals act on behalf 
of (in-)group concerns (being part of the group) and not on behalf of personal concerns. 
Individuals, when interacting within a certain group context, acquire different social roles, or 
“levels of self”, which influences their behaviour. The theory distinguishes between interpersonal 
behaviour (two or more individuals acting solely on the grounds of individual concerns and 
characteristics, e.g. wife and husband) and intergroup behaviour (two or more individuals that 
behave solely on the grounds of social concerns and characteristics, e.g. soldiers in an army 
group).
Reference: 
Tajfel, H. & Turner, J., 1979. An Integrative Theory of Intergroup Conflicts. In The Social Psychology of Intergroup Relations. 
Brooks Cole Publishing, pp. 33–47.
J
        A bias relating to the theory on this card is the Ingroup bias: 
       The tendency for people to give preferential treatment to others they perceive to be 
members of their own groups

Self-determination Theory
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How can I get people to recycle bottles?
Sustainability is a topic known to cause 
discussions and different opinions. People that 
are intrinsically motivated to recycle 
bottles, believe that it is for a good cause and see 
enjoyment in knowing that they do something 
good for mother earth. Maybe they have reached 
this intrinsic motivation by watching a movie or 
reading a book about plastic in the oceans. People 
that are not intrinsically motivated, i.e. see no 
enjoyment in taking the gross and sticky bottles 
to specific plastic sorting cans need extrinsic 
motivation instead. This could be in the form of 
a psysical bait, such as an economic prize.
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Self-determination Theory is valuable when dealing with 
problems relating to motivation. The theory offers insights into two kinds of motivation: 
Intrinsic motivation (an individual’s interest and enjoyment in the activity or task itself) and 
extrinsic motivation (activities that are not interesting and therefore an individual must be 
motivated externally). Motivation is a part of many other theories describing behaviour, yet 
what this theory states is that people need to witness their own competences (learn skills), 
connection (experience a sense of belonging), and autonomy (feel in control of own behaviours 
and goals) in order to change behaviour.
Reference: 
Deci, E.L. & Ryan, R.M., 2000. Self-Determination Theory and the Facilitation of Intrinsic Motivation, Social Development, and 
Well-Being. American Psychologist, 55(1), pp.68–78.

     When people view others as having (situational) extrinsic motivations, but (dispositional) 
intrinsic motivations for oneself

6.5.4 Limitations & alternatives
First, the main limitation in step 2 is the chain of Theory Cards which might lead to a Theory 
Card not (quite) relevant for the given behavioural design project. Thus, the player(s) may have 
to go through several Theory Cards before reaching a relevant. Yet, while this can seem as a 
6.5.2 Preparation
6.5.3 Step by step procedure & outcome
1. Flip the 10 minute hourglass. 
2. Start by reading the brief description of the theory in order to understand its 
content.
3. Read the behaviour bias stated in the green box, which is a phrase developed to 
describe a certain behaviour principle that can be relevant in the player(s) problem 
context.  
4. Read the applied problem case in order to understand the behavioural theory in a 
clearer way and see how it can be valuable for the specific problem context.
5. As a group, perform the task “Force yourself to formulate a hypothesis” in order 
to translate the theoretical knowledge from the theory into a relatable hypothesis 
applicable to the specific behavioural problem context. An example of such a task is: 
”How can your own problem be explained by the terms attitude, perceived behavioural 
control or subjective norms?”
6. If in doubt about any Theory Card element during the game, the player(s) can go 
to page 9-10 in the Guide Book. 
The outcome of step 2 is the first part of the behavioural problem analysis of the player(s)’ 
specific context, resulting in formulating hypotheses. 
No preparation is needed for this step. 
hypotheses is to be repeated until a semi-predetermined number of Theory Cards have 
been considered (each Theory Card guides the player(s) to the next card in an implicitly 
predetermined chain (see appendix 23)). The semi-predetermined number of Theory Cards 
to go through follows the definition: “a thorough behavioural analysis should consist of all three 
categories of behavioural theories to create a foundation of understanding the complexity of 
behaviour and to ensure integrative thinking” (section 2.3.3.2). Yet, the number of Theory Cards 
considered within each category is determined by the player(s) themselves, thus making the 
number of considered Theory Cards semi-predetermined. Furthermore, the predetermined 
chain ensures that the player(s) are constantly guided to a Theory Card that considers other 
topics relating to behavioural theory (the eight topics presented on the Theory Overview 
Wheel, section 6.6.1) than the ones just covered. The chain does so, as it only allows one 
similar topic between the linked Theory Cards (see appendix 23). 
This step 2 is carried out by applying the front page of the Theory Cards (one card per 
player) meanwhile the track of the time for each card is kept with the 10 min hourglass.

limitation for the player(s), it is not viewed as such, due to the tool’s purpose of challenging 
tunnel vision regarding theory inclusion. And as such, the linking of Theory Cards and forcing 
the player(s) to consider different perspectives of behavioural theories in a specific problem 
context deals with this. In other words, the order of the chain is carefully planned to introduce 
the player(s) to a new category of behavioural theories and as a result, the player(s) will 
constantly view the problem from new behaviour perspectives. Next, another limitation from 
the chain is the risk of player(s) coincidentally starting at the same Theory Card when playing 
the game again (which is not ideal when intending to expand theoretical knowledge). An 
alternative to this could be to include the possibility of choosing another Theory Card from 
the same category, but only if the limitation turns out to be a big frustration for the player(s). 
Consequently, these limitations can be fully eliminated by choosing game-mode 3 in step 
0 (section 6.4.1) which allows entirely free use of the Theory Cards with no instructions in 
terms of what Theory Cards to consider. Moreover, another limitation deals with the number 
of formulated hypotheses. In the game it is recommended to define one hypothesis per 
Theory Card per group playing. Yet, if the group consists of four players it can turn out to be 
difficult to agree on one or get all player(s) involved in defining one hypothesis. Alternatively, 
a workaround exists regarding an unrestricted number of hypotheses per Theory Card in the 
case of hypothesis disagreement. However, more hypotheses could jeopardise the player(s)’ 
selection of the top three hypotheses later on in step 4 (section 6.7). Finally, the last limitation 
of step 2 is that the Theory Cards are quite extensive information-wise and contain a lot 
of text which make them time consuming to read. Yet, this has to be put in contrast to the 
existing alternative, which requires one to read 40+ pages of an academic journal article just 
for one behavioural theory. Thus, the Theory Cards are in fact a simplification, despite their 
extensive appearance.

6.6.2 Preparation
The only preparation needed for this step is formulated hypotheses about the problem 
behaviour on post-its.
6.6.3 Step by step procedure & outcome
1. Flip over the Theory Card so that it faces the rear side.
2. Read the three reminder-tasks in order to remember
a. to write the formulated hypothesis on a post-it, and place it on the Theory 
Overview Wheel in the box saying “hypotheses”.
The purpose of step 3 is to visually provide the player(s) with a structured overview of the 21 
behavioural theories included in the game (see appendix 24) and their relation to the three 
behavioural theory categories (individual behaviour, social behaviour, and behaviour change) 
(section 2.3.2 and 2.3.3) and to eight topics relevant to behavioural theories (attitude, thinking, 
change strategy, motivation, social impact, self-efficacy, habits, and principles of behaviour). These 
eight topics are derived from the keywords describing the behavioural theories (in table 
2.1, 2.2, and 2.3), with the intended purpose of easing the reflection of how the different 
behavioural theories relate to each other. Yet, only eight topics are described in the game, 
despite the literature review presenting 10 keywords, as this was assessed through the 
iterations to enable better usability (the simplification from the 10 keywords to the eight 
topics can be seen in appendix 25). This division of eight topics ensures a relation across 
the behavioural theory categories, and especially a relation of the intuitively closest/most 
relevant topics. First, the player(s) must explicitly visualise the theory progress (i.e. through 
putting the hypothesis post-it on the Theory Overview Wheel and cover the theory letters 
with the appropriate amount of discs). Doing so will both make it possible for the player(s) 
to measure progress, while at the same time implicitly fulfil the sub-purpose of creating an 
indication of the missing topics in the behavioural problem analysis. Once the visualisation of 
the behavioural problem analysis progress has been shown, the player(s) must discuss and 
choose the path leading them into the next task of the game. Depending on the chosen path, 
the outcome will either lead the player(s) further into the chain of the Theory Cards or it will 
lead them towards the end of the game. This path requires little discussion, as it again follows 
the definition just described in section 6.5.1.
Step 3 is carried out by applying the back of the Theory Cards together with the 
Theory Overview Wheel. The documentation of the task is supported by post-its 
and pens and the progress on the board is visualised by the bag of discs. As with the 
Problem Documentation Card, post-its (and also discs) are applied to give the player(s) a 
sense of productivity and show clear indicators to measure progress. Further, using post-its 
to document the hypotheses (and for writing the theory letter), the player(s) are able to 
backtrack the progress and simultaneously ease the comparisons of the behavioural theories.
6.6.1 Purpose
==PAGE:95==
 83
6.6.4 Considerations & limitations 
As the Theory Overview Wheel refers to eight topics each relating to behavioural theories 
in different ways, it is worth noticing that these eight topics are based on the keywords found 
in table 2.1, 2.2, and 2.3, yet the names have been simplified in the game to make them 
more understandable and to enhance simplicity. A limitation from this is the risk of simplifying 
21 rather complex behavioural theories into eight more relatable and user-friendly topics. 
Compressing 21 behavioural theories into eight topics adds to the risk of lacking depth, which 
will only be visual to player(s) with a high level of behavioural design expertise. Although 
described as a limitation, it is rather a trade-off between theoretical depth and usability, i.e. 
usage for player(s) with limited experience of behavioural design as well.
6.7 Step 4: Game evaluation & documentation
Based on the game you/your group have now formulated several hypotheses. 
What you/your group should do now, is to try and identify the top 3 of 
your hypotheses in regards to the aspects of:
• 
Are the hypotheses realistic to address# (i.e. will it be possible to change the 
behaviour?)
• 
Are the hypotheses feasible# (i.e. will it be possible to make a solution for this#) 
• 
Will it provide value to the project to solve the identified hypotheses#
Once this is done, you can go to the back of this card to end the game!

to be taken (either picking a new Theory Card or going directly to the End Card).
4. If in doubt about any Theory Card element during the game, the player(s) can go 
to page 9-10 in the Guide Book. 
The outcome of step 3 is an overview of the applied Theory Cards relations to the three 
categories of behavioural theories and the eight topics, as well as a visualised progress of the 
behavioural problem analysis (and also in the game).

6.7.2 Preparation
This task could be performed without going through the previous steps, as the only 
preparation needed for this step is an unspecified number of formulated hypotheses about 
the behavioural problem (written on post-its), which could come from other activities than 
this game. 
6.7.3 Step by step procedure & outcome
1. Rank and identify a top 3 of the strongest formulated hypotheses. This ranking 
should be based on the aspects of reality, feasibility, and created value.
2. Visualise the top 3 by placing the post-its on the End Card.
3. Go to the rear of the End Card and go through the decision path, while focusing 
on answering the questions based on the identified top 3.
6.7.1 Purpose
The purpose of step 4 is to create structured, flexible ways of evaluating the game output as 
well as enabling structured ways of documenting (and transferring) a behavioural problem 
analysis by discussing the formulated output’s (i.e. hypotheses) strengths and weaknesses. 
Doing so will allow for an even deeper level of reflection of the problem understanding, 
as it will visualise any clear gaps or lacks of knowledge. First, the player(s) must discuss and 
compare the hypotheses, based on the aspects of reality, feasibility, and created value (see 
appendix 20). The intention is to help the player(s) in validating their choices of hypotheses 
(section 5.2.2), while at the same time clearly visualising the complete amount of output 
created during the game. Once the discussion of the hypotheses has been completed, the 
player(s) must discuss and choose the path leading them either further into the game or 
towards ending the game. If the player(s) choose to end the game, they are prompted 
to repeat the problem layer task from the Begin Card, yet this time using the back of the 
Problem Documentation Card (see appendix 21). Repeating this task will create means for 
comparing the achieved behavioural insights as well as visualising whether ending the game is 
the correct choice or not. This visualisation is introduced with quite a level of freedom, as the 
player(s) themselves choose whether the problem understanding has enhanced significantly 
enough to end the game. If the player(s) have reached a quantifiable outcome (three strong 
hypotheses for their problem behaviour) the purpose of the game is achieved which means 
that the player(s) have won and the game is ended. If it indeed is the correct choice to the 
end game, the final tasks will be to gather the output (formulated hypotheses and applied 
Theory Cards) and apply them in the further behavioural design activities. If the game is not 
ended, the player(s) are sent back to look at more behavioural theories to enhance an even 
deeper problem behaviour understanding, before being asked to end the game again.
This step is carried out by using the End Card and the Problem Documentation 
Card (back) supported by post-its and pens.
==PAGE:97==
 85
6.7.4 Limitations & alternatives
Ending the game is based on a subjective opinion of the player(s) which is a limitation, as 
player(s) with limited experience of behavioural design may find it difficult to judge whether 
or not a given hypothesis is strong. However, the whole game is based on the assumption 
that the Theory Cards will guide the player(s) through different behavioural theory categories 
and topics, and support them with different perspectives on the behavioural problem to 
make them able to define strong hypotheses. Additionally, the task of filling out the Problem 
Documentation Card should make the player(s) able to see whether or not they have 
obtained a deeper problem understanding. Still, this limitation contains two risks, 1. Ending the 
game while still lacking problem understanding and 2. Leaving after applying 21 Theory Cards 
without knowing whether or not the problem understanding is thorough enough. In other 
words, the game guides and structures a thoroughly behavioural problem analysis, but in the 
end the game’s output depends on the player(s) skills and confidence. However, other game-
ending scenarios might create more complexity at the expense of usability and thus this 
limitation is a trade-off between rigidity and flexible use, whereas the latter is favoured and 
viewed most beneficial, since it relies on the player(s) own ability to make good reflections. 
Consequently, this limitation is difficult to eliminate.
4. Depending on the chosen path, the next step will be: 
a. If the hypotheses are strong:
i. 
Discuss and identify how the seven problem layers are visualised in the now 
thoroughly analysed behavioural problem. Once a layer has been identified, 
fill out a post-it describing the problem layer in the given problem behaviour 
context, and place it on the back of the Problem Documentation Card. 
ii. If the front Problem Documentation Card have been filled out before 
playing the game, compare and contrast the two different levels of problem 
behaviour analysis.
iii. Depending on the outcome follow the path to either go back to assess the 
Theory Overview Wheel or to end the game.
b. If something is still missing from the hypotheses: 
i. 
Follow the path and go back to assess the Theory Overview Wheel and 
choose a new theory, relating to the least covered topic. 
5. Once the game is ended, remember to take the formulated hypotheses and their 
relating Theory Cards, and include both in the further behavioural design project 
activities.
6. To acquire a level of inspiration for the further design activities, the back of the 
Guide Book can help the player(s) to continue the process, as it describes several 
different behavioural design approaches possibly relevant to the further design 
work (found in section 2.3.4).
The outcome of step 4 is a ranking of the top three most feasible, realistic, and valuable 
hypotheses and their corresponding Theory Cards regarding the behavioural problem.
==PAGE:98==
86
6.8 Accessibility & ownership
In order to make sure that the Super Cool Analysis Tool will be applied and found valuable 
in an industry context, the two aspects of accessibility and ownership have been carefully 
considered and incorporated in the Super Cool Analysis Tool.
To ensure easy accessibility of the Super Cool Analysis Tool, it should be available to companies 
through two options in which the full game experience is guaranteed: The first option is to 
download the tool as files from a web page, print them and cut them out. However, in order 
to ensure the full game experience, this option would require a laser cutter and associated 
material (acrylic, plexiglass, wood etc.) to print the discs, as well as an A2 printer to print the 
Theory Overview Wheel. Thus, if these facilities are not available at the company, the second 
option (recommended) is to buy a fully prepared version of the Super Cool Analysis Tool for 
a small amount of money (150-200 DKK) due to production and delivery costs. 
To create ownership of the Super Cool Analysis Tool, the initial suggestion was to incorporate 
structured ways of creating evaluation into the one of the features in the tool (section 5.2.2). 
However, due to the fact that a separate evaluation step appeared to provide no extra value 
to the core of the concept (the interaction between the Theory Cards and the Theory 
Overview Wheel), the evaluation step was removed and other more focused methods for 
creating ownership were applied, such as:
• Each of the steps in the Super Cool Analysis Tool have been developed with the 
aim of fitting into a step-by-step process and at the same time being able to 
stand alone (without the necessity of previous steps being performed). Doing 
so balances flexibility and rigidity (section 6.7.4) to make it possible for different 
industry companies (several of which will be elaborated on in the following section 
6.9) to take ownership of the tool.
• The option for the companies of updating the Theory Cards, through the add-
on of blank Theory Cards, provides the possibility of incorporating behavioural 
theories (not part of the original card set) which the companies find valuable and 
want to apply in their current practice. By doing so the companies can create their 
“own version” of the Super Cool Analysis Tool, and hence add ownership in each 
individual company. To enhance the simplicity of updating the card set, different 
rules (mentioned in section 6.3.1) have been established to ensure ongoing 
coherence of the purpose of the game, despite new behavioural theories being 
added. However, no specific guidelines exist regarding linking new Theory Cards 
to the three behavioural theories categories or eight topics. In such cases the 
companies are advised to look up the description of the categories and the topics 
in the Guide Book.
• As mentioned in section 6.3.4, it is recommended that the companies make use of 
a facilitator, for instance the person responsible for the Super Cool Analysis Tool 
(i.e. taking ownership), when playing the game. While the Guide Book provides 
==PAGE:99==
 87
6.9 Imagined use cases
While the steps in the Super Cool Analysis Tool can be applied in many different ways, either 
as consecutive steps or as inspirational individual activities, two different types of application 
will likely influence the usage: Outcome driven application and output driven application. 
Outcome driven application focuses on the non-case dependent outcome (e.g. applying the 
Super Cool Analysis Tool to obtain a buy-in with customers or applying the tool to show 
customers insights in behavioural design practice). Output driven application focuses on the 
actual case-specific immediate output (e.g. applying the Super Cool Analysis Tool to figure out 
why certain behaviour occurs and to understand it). While both types can be applied by the 
same group of designers, each application will have a different meaning for the needs of the 
designer and the expected results of playing the game. Figure 6.7 (appendix 26) illustrates 
the two types of application in relation to the Double Diamond Model (Design Council 
2007), as well as visualises likely main purposes of applying either outcome-driven or output-
driven application in the given process step. 
6.9.1 Use case 1: Outcome driven application in 
design consultancies
This use case could be imagined to take place in the plan and prepare phase of the design 
process, where the behavioural design project team (in this case consisting of a designer, 
behavioural economist and a project manager) would like to show the client company 
how they would analyse a given behavioural problem (e.g. cigarette butts in Copenhagen 
Municipality). Therefore, the aim of the application of the Super Cool Analysis Tool is to 
give the behavioural design project team an opportunity to impress and create interest 
guidance for the task of being a facilitator, the role in itself is not aimed at any specific 
kind of employee (e.g. project manager). Thus, the role can be enacted by different 
employees and additionally the facilitator role can shift between behavioural design 
projects, to ensure that no insights or time is lost in the case of employee rotations.
Outcome driven
Output driven
plan and prepare
discover
define
develop
deliver
Used as a mean for 
communication, to convince 
customers of the approach for 
the project
