This use case differs from the two previous use cases, as it describes the application of the 
Super Cool Analysis Tool in an engineering design company (a type of company not considered 
nor mentioned earlier in this thesis). Despite the fact that this type of company is imagined 
to be unfamiliar with behavioural design, there is still a huge potential for applying the Super 
6.9.3 Use case 3: Output driven application in 
engineering design companies
As opposed to use case 1, this use case mainly focuses on the output of the tool (i.e. 
applying the Super Cool Analysis Tool for the immediate benefits transferable to the project 
activities). This use case could be imagined to take place in the define phase of the design 
process, where the behavioural design project team (consisting of a behavioural economist 
and a designer) wishes to clarify problem hypotheses in relation to enabling physical activities 
in office spaces. The aim of the application of the Super Cool Analysis Tool is completely 
focused on producing results for the project, and therefore the behavioural design project 
team applies game-mode 1 (section 6.3.1), as the previous project data collection showed 
no clear behavioural coherence. This use case is likely to occur in both behavioural design 
consultancies and in engineering design consultancies, yet it would affect current work practice 
differently in the two kinds of consultancies. In engineering design consultancies, current work 
practice would change a lot, due to the fact that structured ways of developing a problem 
understanding require a larger amount of focus and time than what is currently being spent 
(section 4.3.1). In behavioural design consultancies (experts in understanding behavioural 
problems) applying this tool would not change current work practices much, yet it would 
add more structure and effectiveness to an already present process, while simply requiring a 
little extra time. Nonetheless, in both types of design consultancies a further formalisation of 
documentation of the results of the gamified tool is required (e.g. through a documentation 
form). 
6.9.2 Use case 2: Output driven application in 
design consultancies
from the client company (i.e. outcome driven). Thus, in order to show structure and clarify 
the capabilities of the specific design consultancy, the behavioural design project team, in 
cooperation with the clients, will apply game-mode 2 (section 6.3.1). This use case is likely 
to occur in both behavioural design consultancies as well as in engineering design consultancies, 
as both of them relies on the loyalty of returning client companies and winning bids on 
projects. Applying the Super Cool Analysis Tool in this use case would not change current 
work practice a lot, yet it would provide a fast opportunity to impress and a structured way 
of showing what behavioural design has to offer in the specific design consultancy. As the 
application focuses on the outcome (and not the output) of the Super Cool Analysis Tool, 
the need for documentation would be limited, and further formalisation of documentation is 
not needed for this use case.
==PAGE:101==
 89
6.10 Conclusion & key findings of the Super 
Cool Analysis Tool
The Super Cool Analysis Tool is a gamified front-end analysis tool that supports designers 
in making a thorough behavioural problem analysis with respect to integrative thinking of 
behavioural theory inclusion. Moreover, the Super Cool Analysis Tool enables better validation 
of said analysis and ensures translatable documentation throughout the analysis steps. The 
Super Cool Analysis Tool is aimed at designers working with (or interested in applying) 
behavioural design, by providing structured ways of understanding and overviewing different 
categories of behavioural theories (section 2.3.2 and 2.3.3) and making them adaptable to 
different problem contexts. Further, the key findings from the presentation of the Super Cool 
Analysis Tool are the following:
• As a pre-game step, step 0 prepares the player(s) for the game and explains 
different instructions, formulated to enhance the understanding of the game’s aim 
and to ensure maximum focus on the output from the very beginning of the game.
• As the first main step, step 1 facilitates an initial problem analysis based on a 
systematic reflection of the problem to be dealt with and linked to an analysis of 
seven different problem layers (section 2.3.1). This is determined as the first step 
of the game, as it prompts an initial discussion of the behavioural problem at hand 
and leads the player(s) into an analysing mindset (which is needed in the rest of 
the game).
• As a prolongation, by discussing different behavioural theories in relation to the 
same problem behaviour context step 2 facilitates a deeper level of behavioural 
problem analysis. Furthermore, the step ensures a certain level of discussion, 
Cool Analysis Tool. This is because engineering design companies aim to develop the best 
possible products (as selling these products are their main income) and thus they deal with 
user involvement and usability, i.e. behavioural design could be seen as a valuable input in 
their design practice. For instance this use case could take place in the discovery phase, 
where a UX group in a large manufacturing company wishes to introduce the Super Cool 
Analysis Tool to a specific design team, to get a different view on a project concerning the 
development of a new bike helmet. Therefore, the aim is completely focused on producing 
results for the project, and since the design team are believed to have limited experience 
with behavioural design, they apply game-mode 1 (section 6.3.1). This use case would change 
current work practice a lot, because the UX employees would have to take ownership of 
the Super Cool Analysis Tool and appoint a facilitator each time it is applied. Additionally, due 
to the fact that engineering design companies often consist of many different departments 
(each one responsible for their own part of the process), there would be an extensive need 
for documentation of the output of the Super Cool Analysis Tool, since all decisions must be 
traceable.  
==PAGE:102==
90
due to the chain of behavioural theories, forcing the player(s) to describe and 
understand the problem in relation to very different aspects and topics. The Super 
Cool Analysis Tool focuses on theory inclusion in the analysis phase of a design 
project, due to the fact that it is developed based on the belief that theory driven 
behavioural design results in the most successful interventions.
• Step 3 starts a reflection of how the different behavioural theories relate to each 
other in regards to the aspects of what behaviour do they address (individual, 
social or change) and in regards to the topic describing the theory (section 2.3.2 
and 2.3.3). This reflection is obtained through a clearly visualised overview of the 
different behavioural theories, which at the same time provides clear indicators for 
measuring progress.
• Finally, step 4 facilitates a comparison of the initial behavioural problem 
understanding, discussed in step 1, with the achieved behavioural problem 
understanding, determined in step 4. This allows for an evaluation of the game 
output, while visualising any clear gaps or lacks of knowledge in regards to 
behavioural design. Finally, step 4 guides the player(s) in regards to transferring 
the obtained understanding into the further design process activities by, among 
other things, referring to different behavioural design approaches (section 2.4.3) 
applicable in the later design phases.
Regarding application in industry, aspects such as accessibility and ownership of the Super 
Cool Analysis Tool have been considered, as well as different possible use cases. By 
considering these aspects in the development of the Super Cool Analysis Tool, a better, 
and more continuous, application of the tool should have been ensured. However, direct 
implementation of the Super Cool Analysis Tool has not been conducted, and therefore 
certain central elements, crucial for the application of the tool, have not yet been considered 
nor described in the given context. Therefore, the next chapter (7) discusses the different 
contributions, implications and limitations of the Super Cool Analysis Tool, together with a 
discussion of the thesis in general. 
==PAGE:103==
Chapter 7
Discussion
==PAGE:104==
92
7.1 Introduction to the Discussion
This chapter aims at discussing the Super Cool Analysis Tool in relation to the research 
framework and the findings from the Literature Review (chapter 2) and in relation to the 
results from the Investigation of Current Practice (chapter 4). Section 7.2 highlights the 
contributions of the Super Cool Analysis Tool, whereas section 7.3 highlights the implications 
that the thesis will have for both design research and design practice. Finally, section 7.4 
discusses the theoretical and practical limitations of the thesis.
7.2 Contributions of the support tool
The Super Cool Analysis Tool offers structure and guidance for the front-end activities in 
behavioural design projects by providing means for making a coherent problem behaviour 
analysis through the five steps of: Preparation and Instructions, Initial Problem Analysis, Problem 
Behaviour Analysis, Problem Behaviour Validation, and finally, Game Evaluation and Documentation 
(see description in chapter 6). The core of the Super Cool Analysis Tool (the five steps) 
directly reflects the research framework of the thesis (section 2.4), as it systematically 
represent the front-end steps a designer should go through to develop successful behaviour 
change interventions (section 2.4.1.1). Thus, the Super Cool Analysis tool can be regarded 
as an analytical behavioural design support tool consisting of a series of, both separate or 
consecutive, analytical design steps. Like few existing behavioural design tools the Super Cool 
Analysis Tool exclusively focuses on the activity of understanding, analysing and reflecting 
upon a problem behaviour, based on a combination of specific theoretical insights and 
practical observations. To highlight a few, the Super Cool Analysis Tool is associated with the 
Developing an Intervention toolkit (Community Tool Box 2016a) and the Persuasive by Design 
Model (Hermsen et al. 2014). While both of these tools communicate a problem behaviour 
analysis in a somewhat similar way to the Super Cool Analysis Tool, none of them provides 
clear visualisation of the benefits of making such a problem analysis. Providing structure 
for the problem analysis is important, yet if the designer fails to see the purpose of the 
results of the analysis, no value will be gained from this activity. Further, only the Persuasive 
by Design Model (Hermsen et al. 2014) explicitly reflects current behaviour thinking, by 
implicitly drawing parallels to several models of behaviour and theories of change. However, 
the Persuasive by Design Model, limited by its complexity, offers little explanation of the 
behavioural theories most likely to supply the designer with a better and deeper behavioural 
understanding. Therefore, the Super Cool Analysis Tool is a valuable contribution to the 
existing population of behavioural design support tools, as it guides the designer through 
clearly goal-oriented structured steps of making a problem analysis. At the same time it links 
these steps to explanations from explicit behavioural theories, each of them emphasising 
the purpose of the analysis. This combination of activities gradually increases the designer’s 
behavioural understanding as well as it simplifies the complexity currently affecting theory 
inclusion in behavioural design. 
==PAGE:105==
 93
7.2.1 Comprehensive visualisation of problem 
behaviour influences
The Super Cool Analysis Tool contributes to current problem understanding practice 
(achieved in step 1) as it divides the problem behaviour analysis in relation to seven different 
problem layers: Personal, impersonal, interpersonal, societal, story, background, and frequency 
of change (section 2.3.1.2 and 6.4.4). Defining the behavioural problem is nothing new in 
the behavioural design field, as several behavioural researchers in literature state problem 
identification, definition, and understanding as the first step in behavioural design (Datta & 
Mullainathan 2012; Tania 2017), and further that identifying the root of the problem is 
essential towards finding a solution (Datta & Mullainathan 2012; Cross 2008; Andreasen 
et al. 2015). Yet, few researchers distinguish between analysing problems in relation to 
how behaviour is affected on different levels (i.e. layers), despite the fact that these layers 
logically do affect problem contexts in their own manner (section 2.3.1). However, from 
combining the results of the few researchers who actually do distinguish between some 
type of problem layers (Tromp et al. 2011; Darnton 2008b; Prager 2012; Fogg & Hreha 
2010), a correlation between viewing problems in relation to the perspectives individual, 
social, and time is shown. For instance, Fogg’s & Hreha’s (2010) Behaviour Wizard considers 
the frequency of a target behaviour and thus takes the time perspective into account, yet 
this is in relation to the target behaviour and how this behaviour can be changed, and 
not specifically in relation to understanding the given problem behaviour. Moreover, the 
Behaviour Wizard considers neither the individual perspective nor social perspective of behavioural 
problems, as it mainly focuses on the frequency of behaviours. In contrast, the Developing 
an Intervention toolkit (Community Tool Box 2016a) contains a problem behaviour 
identification step where personal factors, environmental factors and implicit time factors 
(“people’s experience and history”) are considered through seven questions. The questions are 
quite comprehensive, yet with no overview in terms of their interconnections and individual 
contributions to explaining the specific behaviour, however asking these questions can lead to 
a deep problem analysis. Therefore, despite the fact that both behavioural design approaches 
to some extent reflects the three problem behaviour perspectives (either individual, social 
or time), neither the Behaviour Wizard nor the Developing an Intervention toolkit provide 
the designer with an easily obtainable way of identifying the root of the problem from 
interconnecting different problem affecting levels. On the contrary, the Super Cool Analysis 
Tool enables a problem understanding based on existing behavioural literature and clearly 
visualises the interconnections between the problem layers. Hence, the Super Cool Analysis 
Apart from this main contribution, the following sections (7.21-7.2.3) aim at discussing 
especially how the specific steps of the Super Cool Analysis Tool (namely step 1, 2 and 3) 
contribute to new knowledge in the field of behavioural design. This is done by discussing the 
logical processing in literature to the final version of the Super Cool Analysis Tool combined 
with a sense of practicality (derived from a comparison of existing behavioural design tools 
and approaches), which features the Super Cool Analysis Tool’s uniqueness and novelty.
==PAGE:106==
94
Step 2 of the Super Cool Analysis Tool elaborates the distinction of the three categories of 
behavioural theories: Individual behaviour, social behaviour, and behaviour change (section 
2.3.2 and 2.3.3). Several researchers in literature clearly distinguish between these categories 
and stress the importance of understanding the differences of their purposes and aims 
(Prager 2012; Darnton 2008b; Morris et al. 2012). Yet, behavioural design practice reflect no 
such distinction when including behavioural theories in behavioural design projects. Instead, 
in practice an individual division of the behavioural theories in regards to topics or different 
clusters of biases is widely applied due to preferences and familiarity (section 4.3.2). The 
same division is mirrored in the Behaviour Change Wheel (Michie et al. 2011). Although the 
Behaviour Change Wheel communicates different types of “sources of behaviour” (clearly 
derived from literature) it fails at including the designer in understanding them individually, as 
well as in communicating exactly what sources that are necessary to apply in order to achieve 
a successful behaviour change. To some extent, this division (seen both in current practice and 
in the Behaviour Change Wheel) displays a disconnected application of the theories in regard 
to their purpose and aim. From this, two different consequences arise: 1. Designers with 
limited experience of behavioural design have no experience with these behavioural theories 
and are therefore neither able to make their own applicable division of the theories, nor able 
to understand the divisions currently implicitly applied in behavioural design approaches. 2. 
Designers with comprehensive experience of behavioural design who lack experience with 
a given theory, unintended adapt a tunnel vision in regards to applying only formerly applied 
behavioural theories, which results in a non-optimal application of the theories. Therefore, 
the Super Cool Analysis Tool adds a valuable behavioural design contribution, as it provides 
a theoretical framework for understanding how the three categories of behavioural theories 
interconnect and thus add valuable insights – all of which require an integrative thinking 
of the three categories. Doing so will enhance the probability of applying the behavioural 
theories accordingly (e.g. applying theories of change to change behaviour and not to explain 
behaviour (Bhattacharyya et al. 2006)). 
7.2.3 Overview and forced reflection of behavioural 
theories 
Finally, the Super Cool Analysis Tool offers a reflection of behavioural theories through a 
holistic overview of 21 chosen behavioural theories, which relates them to each other and 
to the descriptive topics: Attitude, thinking, change strategy, motivation, social impact, self-efficacy, 
habits, and principles of behaviour (section 6.6.1). Dividing behaviour in relation to simplified 
descriptive topics (as mentioned in the previous section 7.2.2) is not a novel thing to the field 
7.2.2 Theoretical framework for increasing integrative 
thinking
Tool does create a valuable contribution to the field of behavioural design, as it provides a 
new way of visualising and structuring behavioural problems.
==PAGE:107==
 95
of behavioural design. For instance, both The Behaviour Change Wheel (Michie et al. 2011) 
and The MINDSPACE Way (Dolan et al. 2012) do the same. Whereas the Behaviour Change 
Wheel describes three “sources of behaviour” (motivation, capability, and opportunity), the 
MINDSPACE Way describes nine “robust effects” on behaviour (messenger, incentives, norms, 
defaults, salience, priming, affect, commitments, and ego). Both divisions of topics are derived 
from a best-judgement of categorising relevant behavioural literature, aimed at reducing the 
complexity of understanding behaviour (Michie et al. 2011). And due to the fact that the 
reasoning behind the division of the eight topics in the Super Cool Analysis Tool is the same 
(as they are based on the design contributions from the literature review sections 2.3.2.1, 
2.3.2.2 and 2.3.3.1, see appendix 25), this is not a main contribution to the field of behavioural 
design. However, combining this division with the forced chain of reflecting upon dissimilar 
behavioural theories is the Super Cool Analysis Tool’s novel contribution to behavioural design. 
It is a valuable contribution, as it in a systematic and thoroughly contemplated way guides the 
designer through enforced reflection resulting in a deeper and better understanding of the 
behavioural theories. Doing so boosts the designer’s capabilities of understanding behavioural 
theory while at the same time reflecting upon their strengths and weaknesses in relation 
to each other. All of which eventually results in building up a theoretical back-catalogue of 
behavioural theories.
7.3 Implications for research and practice
Based on Super Cool Analysis Tool’s valuable contributions to the field of behavioural design, 
this thesis can in many ways be seen as a substantial asset for both future research and 
practice. Therefore, the following two sections (7.3.1 and 7.3.2) will elaborate on implications 
arising in regards to behavioural design research and behavioural design practice.
7.3.1 Implications for design research
As presented in this thesis, current behavioural design researchers have dealt little with 
structuring and defining existing behavioural theories, or approaches, in relation to each 
other. And those who have (Niedderer et al. 2014; Darnton 2008b; Morris et al. 2012), 
focus either on sustainable innovation or on matching specific behaviour with specific 
behavioural models. Nevertheless, current behavioural design research does mirror an 
understanding of the differences and significances of the two kinds of behavioural theories 
(models of behaviour and theories of change) (Prager 2012; Darnton 2008a; Morris et 
al. 2012). Yet, still a gap exists between on one side the realisation of the importance of 
an integrative thinking of both models of behaviour and theories of change, and on the 
other side the actual visualisation of this knowledge in behavioural design frameworks (i.e. 
design approaches). This gap is further emphasised in this thesis, as the results of the study 
(chapter 4) show that, despite the fact that integrative thinking is somewhat implicitly present 
in current behavioural design practice, an explicit and structured connection between the 
integrative thinking and practical application is missing. Therefore, an implication of this thesis 
==PAGE:108==
96
7.3.2 Implications for design practice 
is the expansion of understanding the behavioural theories’ interconnected relations. This 
acknowledgement forms a suggestion for future design research in exploring and studying 
the importance and significance of applying integrative thinking in behavioural design projects.
A second implication of this thesis is the seven problem layer distinction’s contribution to 
current research of understanding problems in a behavioural context. Here a gap exists 
in current behavioural design research, as no researchers mirror a similar visualisation of 
problem influences, and thus the distinction in this thesis have been brought together by 
fragmented pieces of research. This acknowledgement can be part of future design research 
as well, in determining the effects problem perspectives have on behaviour as well as in 
exploring and studying each effect’s significance. This is an important issue to investigate, 
since research shows that a better understanding of the factors contributing to certain types 
of behaviours leads to a deeper understanding of the problem, which undoubtedly leads to 
more successful behavioural design interventions. 
The introduction of the theoretical framework will enable designers in applying behavioural 
theories in their behavioural design projects in a more practical way, which is important 
as theory based design is more effective than non-theory based design (Glanz & Bishop 
2010; Michie et al. 2008; Henkemans et al. 2015). While several current behavioural design 
approaches (Hermsen et al. 2014; Michie et al. 2011; Wendel 2014) do implicitly include the 
same categories of behavioural theories (individual behaviour, social behaviour, and behaviour 
change), a gap exists between simplifying the inclusion of these complex theories and 
extending the designer’s level of abstraction in regards to expanding theoretical behavioural 
expertise. Hence, as a practical implication designers should use this theoretical framework 
for enabling integrative thinking of behavioural theories, as well as for obtaining an overview 
and a more comprehensive reflection of these behavioural theories in their design work. This 
applies to designers with limited experience of behavioural design whom, with the help of 
this thesis, should be capable of obtaining an understanding the similarities and differences 
of the three categories of behavioural theories, and use this to expand their capabilities and 
current level of behavioural knowledge. Likewise, it applies to designers with comprehensive 
experience of behavioural design whom, with the help of this thesis, are forced to reflect 
upon their choices, and especially their deselection, of included behavioural theories, which 
should help them eliminate their current level of tunnel vision in regards to theory inclusion. 
Another practical implication of this thesis is the output of an analysis tool applicable to all 
kinds of professions involved in behavioural design projects (or projects open to investigating 
behavioural influences). Despite the fact that the investigations of both literature and practice 
in the thesis focus on designers with comprehensive experience of behavioural design, the 
Super Cool Analysis Tool aims at a wide application context. Thus, both companies and 
consultancies with limited or comprehensive experience of behavioural design interested in 
==PAGE:109==
 97
behavioural design will be able to apply the Super Cool Analysis Tool, through the facilitation 
of a shared language and a simplified “middle ground”. As a result, The Super Cool Analysis 
Tool enables different professions in the behavioural design project teams to participate in 
activities currently regarded as non-beneficial or as without any value adding contribution.
The two implications presented above is based on the work and the findings of this thesis 
(i.e. the development and use of the Super Cool Analysis Tool). Yet, the implications should 
still have an effect on designers choosing not to apply the Super Cool Analysis Tool as current 
(and future) designers should know or acknowledge that more time should be spent in 
the problem analysis phase. And additionally, that an open approach and integrative thinking 
of behavioural theories will add to the probability of developing successful solutions in 
behavioural design projects.
7.4 Limitations
Following the nature of research projects, some limitations regarding the methods and the 
representativeness of the results of the thesis exist. The following sections outline the five 
most essential and critical limitations of the thesis’s contributions.
7.4.1 Use context of the support tool
The first limitation applies to the usage of the tool, as the Super Cool Analysis Tool is a front-
end analysis tool and not a synthesis design tool, meaning that the Super Cool Analysis Tool 
does not offer in depth support to designers in all phases of a behavioural design project, but 
only the first initial phase of behavioural design - namely problem analysis. This is a limitation, 
since the application of the Super Cool Analysis Tool requires certain knowledge about what 
output to expect from the tool, when to apply it and how to transfer this output into the further 
design activities. To eliminate or reduce this limitation tool features, such as the Guide Book, 
were incorporated specifically with the aim of addressing these three w’s (what, when and 
how). However, actually overcoming this limitation requests an application of the Guide book 
in accordance with its intended manner.
7.4.2 Limited amounts of research about behavioural 
problem influences
Discovering and investigating current ways of distinguishing behavioural problem influences 
(section 2.3.1) revealed limited sources of literature and research concerning this topic. 
Therefore, a complete visualisation of problem influencing perspectives (i.e. the problem 
layers) was distilled by fragmented pieces of literature. This limitation does not impair any 
of the overall contributions of the thesis, it merely implies that it cannot be fully ruled out 
that other problem layers can as well be valuable to consider in the process of obtaining a 
comprehensive problem understanding of behaviour. 
==PAGE:110==
98
7.4.4 Lack of validation
The most significant limitation of the thesis is the lack of validating the Super Cool Analysis 
Tool in an experimental manner. Proof of concept has been verified through several iterations 
and involvement of several relevant actors. However, there is no significant proof that the 
Super Cool Analysis Tool will enable designers with limited experience of behavioural design in 
building a theoretical back-catalogue or that the tool will enable designers with comprehensive 
experience of behavioural design in extending their theoretical back-catalogue and diversity 
of theory application. In order to prove the significance of the Super Cool Analysis Tool, 
experimental validation sessions must be conducted and their results must be analysed in 
order to conclusively determine the difference created by the tool. This limitation does not 
obstruct any of the contributions stated in the previous sections, as it is the next evident 
step of making the Super Cool Analysis Tool ready for real-world application. Moreover, the 
aspects of conducting experimental validation sessions will be elaborated in the description 
of further work for this thesis (chapter 8). 
7.4.5 Generalisability 
From limitations of the study (chapter 4) the Super Cool Analysis Tool to some extent lacks 
generalisability, due to the fact that the interviews were conducted with only Danish design 
consultancies, and thus the results seem non-representative from an international point 
of view. However, there is an underlying assumption (confirmed by including international 
literature) that behavioural design is performed in the same way across cultural borders (i.e. 
shared application of behavioural theories, hypotheses, and barriers). Therefore, this has no 
implications for the contributions of the thesis, as further studies of including international 
companies/consultancies to confirm/reject this assumption are simply required to validate 
and accommodate the tool for an international context. This is also the next logical step 
elaborated in the description of further work for this thesis (chapter 8).
7.4.3 Simplified topics relating to behavioural theories
To support usability and clarity of the theory overview, the 21 chosen behavioural theories 
were divided into eight simplified topics, all in different ways relating to and describing 
behaviour. Since the determination of the eight topics is logically based on a contrast and 
comparison of different behavioural theories, it is prone to some level of subjectivity as no clear 
rules or guidelines for relating the behavioural theories to these topics exist.  Furthermore, while 
other researchers have divided behavioural theories in regards to topics as well, the division 
applied in this thesis is not precisely the same as any of the previous divisions. Consequently, 
despite the attempt of making the eight topics as broad and applicable as possible, there is 
a risk of designers adding a behavioural theory to the Super Cool Analysis Tool that is not in 
line with any of the current eight topics. This does not harm the overall contributions of the 
Super Cool Analysis Tool, it merely emphasises the need for further research to prove the 
representativeness and applicability of this division of behavioural theories. 
==PAGE:111==
 99
7.5 Conclusion and key findings of the 
Discussion
From the research conducted in this thesis, different gaps in current behavioural design 
practice (chapter 4) and research (chapter 2) have been identified. Together, these gaps 
indicate a lack in literature of emphasising the importance of problem analysis, as well as a 
lack of utilising integrative knowledge of both problem influences and behavioural theories 
in current behavioural design approaches. The findings derived from an extensive review of 
literature (chapter 2) and from an investigation of current practice (chapter 4) conclude the 
need for a theoretically-founded analysis tool specifically developed for the aim of performing 
behavioural design. The Super Cool Analysis Tool favours an integrative thinking of combining 
insights provided from the distinction of problem layers, models of behaviour, and theories 
of change. This integrative thinking is further combined with several factors of general design 
(section 2.5) to enhance usability and application. The Super Cool Analysis Tool directly 
reflects the research framework, as it guides the designer through clearly goal-oriented 
structured steps of making a problem analysis, while at the same time linking these steps to 
explanations from explicit behavioural theories, each of which emphasises the purpose of 
the analysis. Contributions and implications affecting both design practice and research are 
derived as:  
• The Super Cool Analysis Tool contributes to a new way of visualising and 
structuring behavioural problems through three types of problem perspectives. This 
acknowledgement forms a suggestion for future design research in determining 
the effects problem perspectives have on behaviour and studying each effect’s 
significance. 
• The Super Cool Analysis Tool provides a theoretical framework for understanding 
how the three categories of behavioural theories (individual behaviour, social 
behaviour, and behaviour change) interconnect and thus add valuable insight – all 
of which require an integrative thinking of the three categories. As a result, this 
acknowledgement can be part of future design research in exploring and studying 
the importance and significance of applying integrative thinking in behavioural design 
projects. Further, design practice are likely to change as the Super Cool Analysis 
Tool will enable designers in applying behavioural theories in their behavioural 
design projects in a more practical way. This contribution should have an effect on 
designers who choose not to apply the Super Cool Analysis Tool as well, as they 
should know or acknowledge that more time should be spent in the problem 
==PAGE:112==
100
analysis phase and that integrative thinking of behavioural theories increases the 
probability of developing successful solutions.
• The Super Cool Analysis Tool reflects upon behavioural theories through a holistic 
overview of 21 chosen behavioural theories, which relates them to each other and 
to eight descriptive topics (section 6.6.1). This composite overview of behavioural 
theories should increase the designers’ capabilities of understanding behavioural 
theories and their strengths and weaknesses in relation to each other. Therefore, 
forced reflection upon theories will eventually result in building up a theoretical 
back-catalogue of behavioural theories. Furthermore, these eight topics are 
simplified excerpts from complex behavioural literature, made in order to achieve 
a sort of “middle ground”. This should enable different kinds of professions involved 
in behavioural design, or projects open to investigating behavioural influences, to 
participate in activities which they currently view as non-beneficial or as without 
any value adding contributions. 
Thus, the Super Cool Analysis Tool adds a valuable contribution to the field of behavioural 
design. However, certain limitations follow from the research conducted and concern the use 
context of the tool, limited amounts of research, simplification of topics, validation of the concept, 
and finally, generalisability. In order to prepare the Super Cool Analysis Tool for real-world 
application, several of these limitations have been addressed during the development of the 
tool, yet some limitations still require further work (elaborated in section 8.2). 
==PAGE:113==
Chapter 8
Conclusion
==PAGE:114==
102
 
The aim of the thesis was to develop a relevant support tool for designers in 
behavioural design projects. The aim of such a tool was to provide designers with a structured 
overview of behavioural theories with respect to behavioural design problems and tailor this 
overview to fit with current behavioural design approaches. Based on guidelines from the 
Design Research Methodology this was done through a three-staged thesis, consisting of a 
Literature Review, Investigation of Current Practice and Support Tool Development of a behavioural 
design support tool. The findings from the first stage acknowledged the needs for designers 
in 1. To figure out how to connect a behavioural problem with the most suitable behavioural 
theories, i.e. the theories that will result in the most successful interventions. And 2. Be able 
to understand and apply models of behaviour and theories of change in the correct context. In 
addition, the findings from the second stage acknowledged the needs for 1. Designers with 
limited experience of behavioural design being able to build a theoretical back-catalogue to 
increase their behavioural design capabilities and take advantage of theory inclusion in their 
projects. And 2. Designers with comprehensive experience of behavioural design obtaining 
ways of challenging their current tunnel vision in regards to theory inclusion. 
 
Combining these findings resulted in the development of an analytical behavioural 
design support tool: The Super Cool Analysis Tool. The tool meets the above-mentioned 
needs, by providing a five step framework for problem analysis and theory inclusion. In regards 
to practical application, the framework is realised in a game through the following steps: 
Step 0 prepares the designers for the game and provide instructions. Step 1 facilitates an 
initial problem analysis based on a systematic reflection of the current problem understanding. 
Step 2 facilitates a deeper level of problem behaviour analysis by discussing different 
behavioural theories in relation to the problem behaviour context at hand. Step 3 reflects 
on how the different behavioural theories relate to each other through a clearly visualised 
overview of the different behavioural theories. Finally, Step 4 facilitates a comparison of the 
initial problem understanding, discussed in step 1, with the achieved problem understanding 
determined in step 4. 
 
All in all, the Super Cool Analysis Tool contributes with a detailed visualisation of 
problem behaviour influences, a theoretical framework for increasing integrative thinking of 
behavioural theories as well as forced reflection and relating overview of these theories. 
Hence, the thesis opens up for further research of exploring and studying the importance 
and significance of applying integrative thinking, as well as determining different problem 
perspectives’ effects on behaviour. Additionally, the thesis realises that a more thorough 
problem analysis combined with an open approach and integrative thinking of behavioural 
theories will increase the probability of developing successful solutions in behavioural design 
projects. In conclusion, three of the four stated research objectives (section 2.4.2.2) were met, 
making this thesis a successful contribution to both behavioural design research and practice. 
However, as the fourth research objective of “Validate and evaluate the support tool” is yet 
to be met, further work is needed in order for the Super Cool Analysis Tool to become an 
experimentally validated tool for industry application. 
8.1 Conclusion
==PAGE:115==
 103
8.2 Further work
This section provides recommendations and suggestions for future work that will add further 
value and detail to the contributions made by the thesis. As described in the Methodology 
(chapter 3), according to the DRM framework this thesis is a type 3 design research, meaning 
that the final stage of validating and evaluating the support tool is initial. Therefore, the 
following sections (8.2.1 - 8.2.5) present steps and aspects to consider in further research, to 
enhance the usability of the results and to experimentally validate the Super Cool Analysis 
Tool and its impact on behavioural design practice. 
8.2.1 Pilot implementation of the support tool 
8.2.2 Experimental validation
A final demonstration of the Super Cool Analysis Tool was made with students in a workshop 
setting (appendix 27). However, to maximise the level of output, detail and feedback, 
workshops with behavioural design consultancies and general engineering companies should 
be carried out. Additionally, to validate the tool’s applicability (Maxwell 2013) and value in 
a professional setting a full pilot implementation of the Super Cool Analysis Tool should 
take place in both general engineering companies and in design consultancies, as both kinds 
of pilot implementations would provide different insights and feedback. Contrary to the 
final demonstration, a pilot implementation should take place in real project settings (and 
preferably on different projects for a continuous period of time). 
Similar to the reasons for a comprehensive pilot implementation of the Super Cool Analysis 
Tool, further work is also needed in terms of experimental validation of the tool (determining 
the overall impact as well as the individual features and their results). Thus, this section 
presents suggested activities and measures to be performed in an experimental validation 
setting aimed at validating and evaluating the essence of the Super Cool Analysis Tool. This 
essence is defined as: To build a theoretical back-catalogue, i.e. expand the understanding of 
behavioural theories and their interrelations and to use this understanding in determining whether 
or not a theory is relevant to apply. This essence of the Super Cool Analysis Tool should be 
experimentally validated through a test of a null-hypothesis and an alternative hypothesis 
(Freund & Miller 2011) formulated as the following:   
Null hypothesis
A designer not applying the Super Cool Analysis 
Tool will achieve the same behavioural design 
understanding of behavioural theories as a 
designer applying the Super Cool Analysis Tool
Alternative hypothesis
A designer applying the Super Cool Analysis Tool 
will achieve a better behavioural design under-
standing of behavioural theories than a designer 
not applying the Super Cool Analysis Tool
Figure 8.1
Hypotheses for 
experimentally 
validating the 
essence of the 
Super Cool 
Analysis Tool
==PAGE:116==
104
The most optimal way of experimentally validating the essence of the Super Cool Analysis 
Tool would be to perform a pilot study (Maxwell 2013). In this study several (25+) different 
companies (mixture of behavioural design consultancies, engineering design consultancies. and 
engineering design companies) should be supplied with the Super Cool Analysis Tool, and the 
application and results in their real life projects should be measured. However, as this is a 
rather extensive method, it is instead recommended to experimentally validate the essence 
of the Super Cool Analysis Tool through simulated experimental settings with two sample 
groups: One group applying the tool to its full extent, opposed to a group applying only parts 
of the tool. 
Regarding the data collection from these two groups, it should be obtained through pre-tool-
application measurements and post-tool-application measurements to compare the initial 
starting point with the progressed understanding. It is recommended to apply the method 
of concept maps (Davies 2011; Johnson & O’Connor 2008), which is an illustration of a 
visual display of theories and their relations (Maxwell 2013). Before asking the participants 
to make a concept map, it is recommended to introduce them to different types of concept 
maps. Thus, through concept mapping the determination of an understanding of behavioural 
theories and their interrelations could be obtained by counting the number of behavioural 
theories, relations, clusters, non-randomly connections etc. in each concept, and thereby 
confirming/rejecting the null and alternative hypothesis.
In a more practical sense, the data collection of both groups should be based on concept 
maps of each individual participant’s behavioural understanding before using the Super Cool 
Analysis Tool. After making these concept maps, both groups of participants should then 
be presented to a fictional behavioural design project, including a design brief with data 
from imagined observations, followed by the task of individually going from design brief to 
hypotheses about the problem behaviour. For this task of formulating hypotheses, one group 
of participants are provided the full Super Cool Analysis Tool, whereas the other group of 
participants are merely provided the 21 Theory Cards without colour and symbols indicating 
their interrelations and the flow. Having individually worked their way through the task, the 
participants’ should again be asked to individually create concept maps of their progressed 
behavioural understanding.
This type of experimental validation allows for two kinds of comparisons: 1. Comparing 
across the application of the Super Cool Analysis Tool (comparing each individual participant’s 
understanding before and after applying the tool), and 2. Comparing across the availability of 
tool features (comparing participants who applied the full tool with participants who only 
applied parts of it). Whereas the first comparison allows for statistical analysis that takes prior 
behavioural design knowledge into account, the latter allows for a statistical validation of 
the Super Cool Analysis Tool’s impact. However, if time is short, the data collection could be 
simplified by skipping the introductory concept mapping, and thus a comparison of the Super 
Cool Analysis Tool’s impact could be made without taking each individual participant’s prior 
knowledge into account.
==PAGE:117==
 105
8.2.3 Expand to an international context
Despite the fact that behavioural design appears to be performed identically across borders, 
only applicability in a Danish context was investigated in this thesis, and thus further 
data collection is needed to validate the applicability of the Super Cool Analysis Tool in 
an international context. To do so and to apply the approach of concurrent triangulation 
(combining qualitative data with quantitative data (Creswell 2014)), a confirmatory survey 
should be conducted. An attempt for conducting such a confirmatory survey with the aim 
of exploring the connections, similarities and possible additions needed to move towards 
a substantiated internationally applicable Super Cool Analysis Tool was made in this thesis 
(see appendix 28). Yet, due to unforeseen data collecting complications and time limitations, 
the survey was removed from the investigation of current practice to the benefit of fully 
developing and realising features and aspects of the Super Cool Analysis Tool. However, the 
value adding perspective of a survey study is still taken into account through a detailed 
recommendation of conducting such a survey.
The aim of the survey should be to obtain data collection and to confirm and validate 
the insights discovered in the interviews (chapter 4) regarding theory inclusion, identified 
challenges and project staging in an international context. Further, conducting a survey 
provides the research with more representative results as well as better opportunities for 
statistically analysing results and providing stronger conclusions (Creswell 2014). Regarding 
the setup of the survey, it is suggested to conduct it in a professional data collection program 
such as Qualtrics (Qualtrics 2017, see appendix 28). An elaborate list of companies and 
relevant persons to contact and ask to participate in the survey can be found in appendix 
29. In this list four different countries are represented: Belgium, Denmark, the Netherlands, 
and United Kingdom. Regarding the survey questions, it is suggested to categorise them with 
regard to behavioural theories, behavioural design challenges and project staging. Suggestions 
for explicit questions to ask can be found in a suggested table of survey questions (appendix 
30). In this table, 14 questions are formulated to fully cover all survey aspects to explore, 
through a mixture of open-ended questions, agree-disagree questions and question matrices 
(Thayer-Hart et al. 2010). Apart from asking questions for the three overall topics, general 
questions about language, company name and general remarks should for practical reasons 
be added as well.
Different limitations of conducting such a confirmatory survey should be considered. First, 
there is a risk that the users do not understand the questions and thus the premise of 
answering the questions correctly is limited. Thus the questions must be formulated with 
To the benefit of representativeness of the experimental validation, the number of participants 
must be significant, as different statistical tests require different numbers of samples (Freund 
& Miller 2011). Although the Super Cool Analysis Tool is aimed at designers, the diversity of 
the participants should be considered as well, due to the fact that the tool can be applied 
by other professions part of a behavioural design project team as well. Whether or not to 
include other professions in the setting depends on the hypotheses to be investigated.
==PAGE:118==
106
8.3 Final words
8.2.4 Establish evidence of problem layers
In brief terms further work is needed to establish evidence of the comprehensive problem 
understanding presented in the Super Cool Analysis Tool (consisting of seven problem layers 
(section 6.4.1)). Through pilot implementation and experimental validation it should be 
investigated if the problem understanding is complete, or if other problem layers should be 
added in order to obtain a complete visualisation and understanding of problem behaviours. 
Obviously, It is recommended to address this topic through further work of this research 
project, although the topic itself could be an entirely separated research project.
8.2.5 Web page and accessibility
Finally, practical further work exists regarding the accessibility of the Super Cool Analysis Tool. 
To make it available for application in consultancies, companies and other design teams with 
interest, a web page with the tool documents and files should be set up. From this web page 
it should be possible to download the files for free, yet it should additionally be possible to 
buy a finished tool version, hence, a small production of the Super Cool Analysis Tool should 
also be established. 
a focus on both simplicity and at the same time understandability (e.g. by including figures 
illustrating specific parts of the questions). Secondly, the major limitation about the survey 
procedure is that participants often tell what they think they do, rather than tell what 
they actually do. Again, this requires some serious consideration in the formulation of the 
questions as well as different survey methods developed for overcoming survey bias (e.g. 
System Usability Scale, (Brooke 1996)). Finally, it can be challenging to gain new relevant 
information from surveys, as the questions in the survey are very specific and/or with answer 
options. To overcome this limitation, a closing remarks field should be added to the survey 
to catch valuable remarks for the study. 
This thesis has developed a gamified analysis tool, the Super Cool Analysis Tool, that provides 
designers with structured ways of analysing behavioural problems, and with easy-applicable 
and practical ways for understanding and selecting what behavioural theories to include 
in their behavioural design projects. Thus, with respect to emphasising integrative thinking 
of theory inclusion, the Super Cool Analysis Tool eases the process of theory selection for 
designers, eventually obtaining and expanding their theoretical back-catalogue concerning the 
field of behaviour.

10.1 Assignment A: Preliminary assignment
Focus area and possible project options: 19/01-17
Formalia: 
Beatrice Angel, s123678, Stud.MSc.Eng. Design & Innovation, DTU Management Engineering
Nanna Kristiansen, s123420, Stud.MSc.Eng. Design & Innovation, DTU Management Engineering
Supervisor: 
Philip Cash, Associate Professor, DTU Management Engineering
Collaborative partner:
/KL.7 ApS
Nørregade 6
1165 København K
Denmark
Time frame and ECTS points:
January 2nd – July 2nd 2017.
35 ECTS
Scope:
Behavioural design: Development of support tool for designers based on a classification of 
behavioural design problems in regards to social theories.
NB! What was initally named social theories is in the actual thesis named as behavioural theories, 
as they were found to include perspectives of individual behaviour and behaviour change as well, 
thus the name “social theories” was misleading in this context. 
Focus:
The development of a design support tool will be based on design development and design 
research methodology, with a mapping of both theoretical and practical current practice. 
Further, it will be based on behavioural design theory.
We expect the design support tool to 
• provide a practical way for applying and tailoring theoretical determinants of 
behaviour in behavioural design situations. 
• aid the designers in gaining a holistic understanding of behavioural problems and 
related social theories
• be based on behavioural design theory and practice
Alternative end goals: 
All alternative end-goals will end up in a satisfying completed master thesis with proposals 
for further work.
1. Develop a design support tool for aiding designers in the behavioural design 
situation, through a classification of behavioural problems and social theories. 
Experimental validation (through workshops) and evaluation of the support tool, 
as well as real-life tested pilot implementation. 
==PAGE:129==
 117
2. Develop a design support tool for aiding designers in the behavioural design 
situation, through a classification of behavioural problems and social theories 
(thorough literature review as well as real-life case studies).
3. A classification of behavioural problems and social theories (merely through a 
thorough literature review and classification) and develop a research framework 
as the final delivery.
For further clarification see figure 10.1 on the following page (118) that visualises the 
preliminary Gantt chart (assignment A) of the thesis (Attrup & Olsson 2008).
Project Management:
We will from the beginning carefully estimate resources, time, and deliverables for the thesis 
in order to achieve a successful thesis progress and end results. This includes: 
• Manage our time and planning by using a Gantt chart and a shared Google calendar.
• Manage our resources through an assessment and good use of our skills, materials, 
literature and human capital (supervisor and collaborating partners).
• Manage and ensure good deliverables through on-going milestones (using the 
success criteria of the concept SMART (Attrup & Olsson 2008)) to monitor and 
manage the thesis.
Using the Gantt chart as a way of steering will help us navigate through this Master Thesis and 
inform us of possible detours of the thesis – thus, the Gantt chart will probably change from 
the beginning to the end (Attrup & Olsson 2008). The Gantt chart is used by the project 
group (us) and the supervisor to give overview, context and perspective on the thesis.

10.2 Assignment B: Postliminary assignment 
Project reflections 27/06-17
Main objective of postliminary assignment: To put the initial project plan (assignment A) 
into perspective by comparing it with the actual project plan (assignment B) and reflect on 
the difference in order to enable learning and improve project manager competences.
Scope: Development of an analysis tool for theory inclusion in behavioural design projects.
Scope: 
The project progressed as expected according to the predefined stages, yet compared to 
the initial project plan (assignment A) adjustments and changes occurred along the way, as 
knowledge and insight were obtained. First, the scope of the project changed, as the literature 
review showed that other authors (e.g. Darnton 2008b) had already studied the link between 
general behavioural problems and behavioural theories thoroughly. Additionally, investigating 
current practice revealed that another scope would have greater impact on current practice. 
Thus, the scope of the thesis changed to focus on comprehensive problem understanding 
and integrative thinking of behavioural theories. This change of scope was necessary to do, as 
the thesis must be adjusted in regards to the actual relevant situation (Attrup & Olsson 2008). 
Nonetheless, the delivery of a support tool for designers in behavioural design projects as 
well as the success criteria (learning objectives), remained the same from the initial project 
plan to the actual project plan. Additionally, the tool expectations set out in the preliminary 
assignment A were met, as the Super Cool Analysis Tool provides structured ways for obtaining 
a holistic/comprehensive problem understanding, a holistic overview of behavioural theories 
and practical ways of behavioural theory application to a specific behavioural problem. Finally, 
the Super Cool Analysis Tool combines findings from behavioural design theory and practice 
and hence, the last expectation was also met.
End of the thesis: 
The Design Research Methodology (Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009) was applied as a framework 
for the design research, providing guidelines for systematic planning of research and thesis 
activities. Hence, the thesis was divided into four partly parallel and iterative main stages:
• 
Stage 1: Research clarification – Literature Review
• 
Stage 2: Descriptive Study I - Investigation of Current Practice
• 
Stage 3: Prescriptive Study – Support Tool Development
• 
Stage 4: Descriptive Study II - Validation and Evaluation of the Support Tool
Due to priority given to the development of the Super Cool Analysis Tool, the thesis reached 
a type 3 design research (Blessing & Chakrabarti 2009), meaning that the content in the 
first three stages (1-3) was performed comprehensively, whereas the content in the last 
What changed & why did it change?
==PAGE:133==
 121
stage (stage 4) was merely on a very initial basis. Consequently, the alternative end-goal 2 
in preliminary assignment A was this thesis’ end, despite the fact that research objective no. 
4 referred to validating and evaluating the Super Cool Analysis Tool. However, all alternative 
end-goals (including no. 2) would constitute a complete and satisfying Master Thesis with 
proposals for further work and thus, the thesis can be viewed as successful. Moreover, it is 
better to formulate a very ambitious scope and then accept fulfilment to a 80% degree (in 
this case, the last stage/last research objective was subject to initial effort only) instead of 
making a lower, but realistic, scope (Attrup & Olsson 2008).  
Extended stages: 
In order to make an in-depth comparison of the initial project plan (assignment A) and the 
actual project plan (assignment B) in depth, two simplified Gantt charts representing each 
project plan can be found in figure 10.1 and 10.2 (page 118-119). As the project plans in this 
context are used as means for following up on the project, focus is on the stages rather than 
on the details. By comparing the initial Gantt chart with the actual Gantt chart clear changes 
are visualised. As Stage 1: Research Clarification – Literature Review progressed as planned 
and the milestone was reached on time, Stage 2: Descriptive Study I - Investigation of Current 
Practice was extended. This was partly because of the challenges to establish interviews, but 
mainly because of the preparation, development and distribution of a survey. Unfortunately, 
the survey preparation and data collection required more time than expected and as a result, 
Stage 3: Prescriptive Study – Support Tool Development was delayed. Additionally, stage 3 
was extended due to challenges in setting up workshop dates (especially with the design 
consultancies) but also due to the importance of focusing on the usability of the Super Cool 
Analysis Tool. However, after 13 workshops (resulting in five overall iterations), the Super 
Cool Analysis Tool was finalised and initial experimental validation ideas were generated, 
yet due to the delay in two previous stages, the focus shifted towards finalising the thesis. 
Consequently, changes occurred compared to the initial Gantt chart as several project stages 
were extended at the expense of the final stage of the project. Yet, in the end the thesis’ scope 
of developing a support tool for designers in behavioural design projects was met.
What did we learn from these changes?
Fortunately, reflection on why things went differently than expected, enables learning (Attrup 
& Olsson 2008) which leads to experience and valuable insight obtained in favour of increasing 
our project manager competences. Firstly, due to lack of knowledge of the actual problem 
ascertained in the beginning of a project, it is almost impossible to define the final scope at the 
first attempt (Attrup & Olsson 2008). Despite the fact that the defined scope will most likely 
change as a project evolves and more knowledge and insight are obtained, it is still important 
to formulate a scope, as it creates a path to follow and is very valuable when changes occur 
in a project. Next, we learned that to plan, prepare, conduct, send out, and collect a surveys 
data requires extensive amounts of time, especially in an international context with no other 
contacting details than a general info-mail. In addition, we found that delays in one stage will 
most likely result in delays in the following stages and thus postponing the whole ending 
==PAGE:134==
122
point of a project. Things take time and therefore a buffer of time for all the different stages 
should have been part of the initial project plan. The fact that project plans are often too 
optimistic is a classic project manager mistake (Attrup & Olsson 2008), yet one of the only 
ways to overcome this challenge is to learn from experience. Finally, we also learned that a 
project can still be successful in terms of achieving the scope and contributing to research 
and practice, despite a lack of meeting all research objectives and/or stages. In other words, 
further work will always occur in projects, as it is always possible to find other things to do 
or clarify. What we found is that the practical challenges of projects in terms of complexity 
(many activities simultaneously), human behaviour (get people to answer survey, participate 
in interviews, workshops etc.), and uncertainty (scoping of the project and clarifying focus) 
(Attrup & Olsson 2008) affect all types of projects. Therefore, these challenges must always 
be considered and dealt with at the time of occurrence, as they are nearly impossible to 
prepare for and prevent.
What would we do differently in the future? 
In the light of our achieved project management competences and skills, several things will 
be done differently in a similar future project. First, as the uncertainty of the project is high 
in the beginning and the actual knowledge is relatively limited, it can be appropriate not 
to set out the scope too soon before the required insight is obtained (Attrup & Olsson 
2008). According to Attrup & Olsson (2008), especially product development projects are 
characterised by a relatively long scope/goal phase compared to, for instance, construction 
projects, as the scope in product development projects must be validated by the customer 
group. Next, it should be determined beforehand if a survey should be conducted, and if 
so, carefully planned with a sufficient dedication of time. Thirdly, from the beginning of a 
project it is important to clarify and determine clear goals of the stages to allow for a better 
optimisation of time as well as realisation of when the goals are met. Managing this could be 
done by applying the Stage Gate Model (Cooper 2008), which is a blueprint for managing 
new product development processes to improve effectiveness and efficiency. As it consists of 
clear stages followed by gates, in which decisions to kill/go/redo are made, it is highly applicable 
to ensure that all objectives of a stage are sufficiently met. This will ensure a more efficient 
flow of the project stages, which in this thesis could have been likely to have decreased the 
amount of time spent in the stages, as well as decreased the amount of overlapping stages 
(the most noticeable difference between the two Gantt charts in figure 10.1 and 10.2).

Appendix 3: Interview 1 - KRUKOW (1 of 3)
Date:   
21st February 2017 from 15.45-16.45 o’clock
Place:  
Strandvejen 124, 2900 Hellerup
Language:  
Danish
Participants:
 
 
Beatrice Angel (Student - Technical University of Denmark)
 
 
Nanna Kristiansen (Student - Technical University of Denmark)
 
 
Sille Krukow (Founder and behavioural design director - KRUKOW)
Key findings
• Applies KRUKOW Design Principles, which are “self-made” principles, based on e.g. cognitive 
psychology and behaviour economy, in order to define the problem behaviour and thus forming a 
solution for behaviour change. 
• KRUKOW does not explicitly state that it applies different behavioural theories, but mentions different 
buzzwords such as triggers (Fogg 2003) and priming (Cialdini 2007). Further, KRUKOW states that 
90% of all behaviour is controlled by automatic thinking, i.e. System 1 (Kahneman 2003) and therefore 
the behavioural design consultancy only works with behaviour relating to system 1. 
• Once the target behaviour has been determined, KRUKOW maps the problem behaviour and from 
this mapping, different types of barriers (physical, psychological and social) are identified and described 
by the use of heuristics and cognitive biases (Tversky & Kahneman1974).
• Success definition for a behavioural design project in KRUKOW is defined as whether the desired 
behaviour change was achieved or not and is determined through statistical measurements.
• KRUKOW states that everyone want to make behavioural design and include theoretical knowledge, 
yet there are few descriptions on how exactly to do so and how to convert knowledge into changes 
in the real world.
Answers
1.
2.
Initial work (in cooperation with the client company):
1. Problem behaviour identification: What does this ambition mean in practice? Well the problem behaviour is when customers 
choose x product instead of y, which happens in x markets.
2. Problem behaviour definition: The problem behaviour occurs when x,y,z.
3. Selecting one problem behaviour: Which one of these problem behaviours costs you the most? Cost-benefit analysis with 
social impact included (which human consequences does the problem behaviour have?) The problem behaviour is chosen 
(now we have a specific behaviour situation that represents a measurable problem (someone does this much at this time)).
4. Discussion with client company: How big/how small should the commercial package be? 
No.
KRUKOW get hired by a client company that have very fluffy ambitions for behaviour change e.g. “secure the sale of sustain-
able products.”
 
KRUKOW's purpose with behavioural design is to create measurable behaviour changes that make a difference in a positive 
manner, based on the assumption that no human on the earth want to die of diabetes, destroy the planet on purpose etc.
 
KRUKOW only work with companies within three areas:
 
– Sustainability 
 
– Health
 
– Safety (mainly financial safety) 
 
“We are a part of the whole behavioural design process” -  Founder of KRUKOW
==PAGE:141==
 129
Appendix 3: Interview 1 - KRUKOW (2 of 3)
4.
5.
3.
KRUKOW Behavioural Design Method
1. In behavioural mapping KRUKOW collect knowledge about:
 
1.1 Existing choice-architecture design that surrounds the negative decision process, because it is here the errors 
 
should be found (eg. cues, guides, triggers). 
 
1.2 Existing interaction data (eg. counting at Hovedbanegården). Often it is needed to cross two or more kinds of data 
 
sets (eg. the weather versus when do you turn on your kettle). What is the specific behaviour? Which interactions take 
 
place? Does this match what we found? Outcome: description of what is going on and the observed behaviour.
2. Set up test parameters: What do we expect that we can change? (eg. increase sales of product X by 10% compared to y)
3. Identify and describe three types of barriers with help of visual overviews of cognitive biases (draws on experience and 
speculating):
 
3.1 Physical (e.g. bus signs or bins placed invisibly).
 
3.2 Psychological (“the expert trap” - e.g. hospital signage).
 
3.3 Social (e.g. everyone else do this so I will also do that).
4. Design process starts: Now we know which barriers we must design us out of. In the first design research phase where 
system boards are made (kind of analogies where you look at other areas that have nice solutions to a barrier). Make a visual 
collection of good solutions, but what is practically possible? Then we start designing the solution into the new context, brand.
5. Prototyping 2-3 good solutions.
6. Design of test protocols and brief the observer that must make the tests (often employees) 
“Which data do we need for the test set-up? What is this data we need? How do we get it?” - Founder of KRUKOW
7. Test (pilot-testing).
8. Data analysis: What happened? What do the results show?
9. Recommendations about what changes in the choice-architecture must be made, what effects can the client company 
expect. Now the company can make these changes in other platforms that might be relevant as well. 
 
All steps happens with ongoing client meetings to agree on which way to go.
 
“[..] Based on this situation, you do what we call a behavioural mapping. Here you collect knowledge about two things: You 
collect knowledge about the existing choice-architecture design [..] and existing interaction data [..]” - Founder of KRUKOW
 
“[..] we describe three types of barriers, and that is physical barriers, psychological barriers and social barriers [..] What we find 
here is: What barriers are we facing? Once they have been identified [..] then we go through visual views of the cognitive 
biases. So, we go through these and look at: Is it status quo bias we are up against? Is it unit bias? [..] What is at stake here? 
And, of course, here we draw on experience [..]” - Founder of KRUKOW
 
“Yet behavioural mapping is the first and very important step in behavioural design. [..] It's not hard to come up with good 
ideas, especially not when you have a rock-solid behavioural mapping.” - Founder of KRUKOW
Cognitive psychology + automatic thinking: 
1. Reflective (system 2, which can be influenced through normal informative communication, only happens 10% of the time).
2. Automatic thinking (system 1, behaviour is 90% of the time controlled by automatic/uncontrolled thinking where we just 
react on our surrounding environment and their capability to work with or against our instincts).
 
Design principles (made by Sille Krukow) is based on cognitive psychology, behaviour economy etc. that you can use when you 
make system boards and then if we realise we miss something then we go back to cognitive psychology or social to see if we 
miss something. 
 
“We have such a scheme with our design principles [..] So I have made such principles and I have drawn from all sorts of 
places [..] So these [the design principles] are the ones you use when making system boards. Then it may occur that we 
gradually find out “okay, we really miss something here” [..] and then we always go back to the cognitive psychology and look 
“okay, out of these 450 biases - what can we have overlooked?” [..]” - Founder of KRUKOW
 
“Matter out of place” formulated by Mary Douglas to change automatic behaviour: Humans will go a long way to maintain our 
own personal systems (eg. how we put our cutlery), but where you can awaken automatic behaviour and make us aware 
(reflective) is if something breaks our system and thus causes us to act and behave differently.
 
*The founder doesn’t tell directly which theories they use but mentions triggers (Fogg 2009b), automatic thinking (Kahnemann 
2003), cues (Duhigg 2014) and priming (Cialdini 2007).
“There are so many practical things when you get out in the real world, so that not all good solutions actually work. This 
contributes to that you often end up with a low-practice solution.” - Founder of KRUKOW
 
General challenge:  To familiarise themselves with new fields and professions, and train employees to think differently (espe-
cially top management).

No.

Appendix 3: Interview 1 - KRUKOW (3 of 3)
6.
7.
“For us, the only thing that determines whether we have succeeded or not is whether we have created a measurable effect.” - 
Founder of KRUKOW
A behavioural design process at KRUKOW consist of behavioural economists, behavioural designers and a project manager 
(often 1 economist, 1 designer and 1 project manager pr. project). 
1. Behavioural economists can understand and gather data, set-up protocols and logarithms.
2. Behavioural designers can shape and understand the design part. Collect information about choice-architecture (the 
tourist principle - go through what the customers go through). 
 
Throughout the process some work will be solo and other in groups.
 
“It is always the composition of a behavioural economist and behavioural designer. Then there may be more economists 
depending on how much data is to be processed. [..] Always these two roles and then a project manager [..]” - Founder of 
KRUKOW
Other
About Sille Krukow
Educated at The Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts Schools of Architecture, Design and Conservation in the field of visual 
communication design. 
Been in the advertising industry:
“I have spent around 9 years with researchers within cognitive psychology and political science to try to say okay, which 
theories do they work with and which do we as designers need” - Founder of KRUKOW
Interest area: How do we use design to make measurable changes in behaviour?
Behavioural design definition 
Many different definitions of what behavioural design is - especially theoretical descriptions, but for Sille Krukow behavioural 
design is a practical view/understanding.
Often when humans view behavioural design from a theoretical perspective they have no real life practical experience with 
behavioural design and that is fine, but not how KRUKOW design works.
Behavioural design work in general 
KRUKOW's behavioural design methods is developed to make field experiments and design solutions in real life settings.
“If you are in an academic world and test things in a closed environment then you have no clue about how and if it would work 
in real life settings” - Founder of KRUKOW
Basically we are animals so we will always try to copy each other's behaviour only to conserve energy and survive. We always 
try to fit into the social norm in the context we are in.
Complexity we have created in our world (so much choice-architecture we must interact with all the time, information flows 
etc. all things that have not been taken into account that we are automatically thinking).
Important comments in regards to the research framework
The research framework only shows the behavioural mapping part of a behavioural design process because testing is not 
included (which is the most difficult part of a behavioural design process according to Sille Krukow). 
“Yet behavioural mapping is the first and very important step in behavioural design.”
 “Everyone wants to perform behavioural design and include theories but there are so few descriptions on how exactly to do so, 
and how you convert it to changes in the real world” - Founder of KRUKOW

No.
