Appendix 4: Interview 2 - Decision Design (1 of 3)
Date:   
28th February 2017 from 10.00-11.00 o’clock
Place:  
Center Boulevard 5, 2300 København S
Language:  
Danish
Participants:
 
 
Beatrice Angel (Student - Technical University of Denmark)
 
 
Nanna Kristiansen (Student - Technical University of Denmark)
 
 
Thor Ridderhaugen (Founder of Decision Design)
Key findings
• Often Decision Design is only hired into the first part of a behavioural design process (discovery and 
problem definition), yet in some projects they contribute to development and implementation as well 
(the last part of behavioural design according to Decision Design). 
• The general project plan varies from project to project, but identical for all projects is the goal of 
applying a high level of measurements, as these measurements can be applied for determining whether 
the actual behaviour change occurs. 
• Decision Design distinguishes between different scientific levels of behavioural theories: Behavioural 
economics, anthropology, behavioural psychology, and communication. Yet, as the employees of Decision 
Design do not posses the knowledge of all these fields, different professionals with different expertises, 
i.e. anthropologists, are occasionally hired into different projects.
• Decision Design has a database with behavioural theories, which they use for investigation of relevant 
theories for specific projects. 
• Each project starts with determining a hypothesis of the problem behaviour in question, yet Decision 
Design faces a challenge of figuring out both whether the chosen hypothesis is relevant and whether 
it will achieve the desired effect. 
• Decision Design states that it is difficult to design successful interventions if you do not have the right 
theoretical understanding, yet achieving this understanding cannot be obtained easily. 
Answers
1.
No.
Private and public client companies contact Decision Design with a task, e.g. “We must develop a campaign”. Then Decision 
Design figures out what should be the purpose of the campaign, in order to figure out who to involve, e.g. if the company just 
wants to be visible, a communication bureau would be relevant to involve in the project.
 
It's very important to identify the purpose of the client company’s task in order to have the right approach to the project and 
thus more likely achieve project success.
 
“Often we are only hired into different stages of a behaviour design process, often the first part.” - Founder of Decision Design
 
Thus Decision Design can be seen as a very advisory company that has provided knowledge to a behavioural design process, 
that then have contributed to design interventions and behaviour change in the end. 
==PAGE:144==
132
Appendix 4: Interview 2 - Decision Design (2 of 3)
Answers
No.
4.
5.
2.
3.
The process up to an analysis of how we develop behavioural design is dictated by the behavioural economy statistical analyses 
(i.e., measuring). 
 
The generally plan of the project varies from project to project, but overall we would like to measure everything we can measure 
(“It is in the numbers that all magic occurs”), so if we can measure, the process would be:
 
1. Observation and analyse the surrounding environment.
 
2. Analysis of target group. 
 
3. Baseline measurement: create the data that we need in order to change the behaviour (e,g. humans eat x much 
 
and throw y out).
 
4. Development of interventions.
 
5. Measurements to see if the interventions have changed the target groups’ behaviour.
 
However in some projects you cannot measure, because the target group is too large and resources limited (or due to legal 
reasons). In these projects we instead begin to analyze the decisions about the problem (qualitative studies). E.g. whether a 
farmer has changed the bulb for the last 4 weeks cannot be measured, so instead we analyse which factors play a role into the 
farmer's world ,that can influence whether or not he has changed the bulb in the last 4 weeks. Maybe if the farmers can’t see 
the value over time in changing the bulbs, then their agriculture will not exist in the long run? Here anthropologic conclusions 
are made in order to continue the process of developing interventions (which are based on theory and experience).
Observation and analysing the surrounding environment:
Observation study where anthropologists are hired to investigate: How do humans behave? And why do they behave in this 
way? We rarely interview people, because people tend to have difficulties in explaining why they do as they do. 
Yet, interviews can guide you in some directions, so if we still need some answers after the observations, the anthropologists 
will conduct a participant-observation study to get insight in what is happened up to this point (that the decision is made)? 
 
Decision-mapping
“Typically, you don’t want to go in and ask, because people often have a hard time to explain why they do what they do." - 
Founder of Decision Design
We apply theories when we can. If the project deals with something communicative, then we look at communication theory, 
etc.
 
Sometimes we hire some anthropologists who have the whole theory apparatus to make an anthropological analysis and find 
out the motives of the problem decision and behaviour. 
 
When the project concerns behavioural economics we look at barriers and look at the financial decisions in practice.
 
“My dear big brother [the other founder of Decision Design]  is a behavioral economist so he has a huge theory apparatus. We 
also have a database where we have read the most, but if we miss something, then we will try to research [..] Maybe we have to 
read new theories that we have not known before.” - Founder of Decision Design
 
Outlines decision process model that outlines biases or heuristics and a direct way of applying theoretical hypothesis in design. 
E.g. Læger Uden Grænser sends a letter out to collect money. What decisions lies in reaching the point of actually donating 
money?
 
1. Do you open the letter or not? What can influence this decision? (first intention design – logo, the address is  
 
handwritten, written for your name etc.).
 
2. Do you read the content or not?
 
3. Do you want to donate money/was the content interesting enough?
 
4. How much money do I want to donate?
 
5. How will I donate the money?
 
Develop hypothesis: “[..] and then we try to go in every time and say, what are the biases or heuristics that might have an 
impact here? So it's very direct way to applying theoretical hypothesis in design.”- Founder of Decision Design
Theoretical challenge: “To establish hypotheses and then figuring out that they do not have the effect as you thought. That is 
challenging in itself and also figuring out why.” - Founder of Decision Design
 
Practical challenge: Collection of qualitative data.
Sometimes the behaviour change can first be measured several years after the intervention, so it is difficult to collect data and 
measure the behaviour change right here right now.
Sometimes it is difficult just to collect data of the problem behaviour, e.g. hygiene in public toilets - how do you measure how 
much pee there is?

Answers
No.

7.
“In measurable projects we measure how much behaviour change we have achieved, where 4% is poor and for example 60% 
would be very good. In non-measurable projects we look at the client’s reaction. Is the client satisfied?” - Founder of Decision 
Typically, we start with meetings to see whether we can help the client company or not. If not, can we involve our other 
company-partners in the project? 
 
Next, the founder evaluates the project and figures out how many employees do we need, which skills, how much money do we 
have? Then he hires a project team based on Decision Design’s network. The project team varies a lot from project to project:  
 
– A project running 1 year could be 2 team members .
 
– A project with a lot of money (several millions), then a project team of for instance 8.
 
“It is very different [..] It also depends on the time horizon [..] maybe we need 5 people to do the task. If we have one year for 
the same work, we can finish with only 2 people. It is very different. [..] sometimes we hire anthropologist, sociologist, etc.” - 
Founder of Decision Design
Other
Behavioural design in general
Behavioural design can be many different things, for instance to change the words in a certain context, to change physical 
arrangements of things that the target group faces, or something visual.
Behavioural economics started in the 1970s.
 
Behavioural theories
Behavioural theories takes place in many different scientific levels: Anthropology, behavioural psychology, behavioural 
economics, communication.
“When you present a problem and say now we need some people to do something here. What is behind it? What theories do we 
need? I do not know! I'm not a behavioural economist, how should I learn it in an hour? So that's the big challenge.”
 - Founder of Decision Design
It is difficult to design interventions when you don’t have a theoretical understanding.
 
“Important to have the right approach to a project”-example 
The government of United Kingdom wanted homeowners in England to insulate their attics to reduce CO2 emissions, so 
homeowners would get a energy saving (in heating their houses up) and United Kingdom as a EU state would achieve their 
targets of reducing CO2. The government offered homeowners economic incitement if they got their attic insulated. Nothing 
happened because humans are lazy. There was a barrier in the fact that the householders should first clean up the attic before 
it could be insulated. So they changed the offer so the homeowners could get their attics cleaned and donate some of their 
things from the attic for charitable purposes. Homeowners who now accepted the offer increased with 300% (3 times as many 
homeowners accepted the offer now compared to the first offer).
 
Ethics
The concept of ethics started to be mentions in relation to nudging.
We must not deprive the individual some freedom of choice when we make behavioural design.
The founder got the question “What do you think about using human as means to achieve a goal?” he answered “I use the 
environment and communication, not humans. We all have a goal as soon as we open our mouths.”

Appendix 5: Interview 3 - /KL.7 (1 of 3)
Date:   
1st March 2017 from 14.00-15.00 o’clock
Place:  
Nørregade 6, 1165 København K

Participants:
 
 
Beatrice Angel (Student - Technical University of Denmark)
 
 
Nanna Kristiansen (Student - Technical University of Denmark)
 
 
Sebastian Borum Olsen (Behavioural designer - /KL.7)
Key findings
• Projects in /KL.7 are distinguished between four different types: Behavioural strategy, behavioural 
campaign, behavioural studies and behavioural analysis. Each of these types determines different levels 
of involvement in the behavioural design process.
• All projects in /KL.7 deal with behaviour change, yet creating new behaviours or eliminating existing 
ones is relevant as well. 
• /KL.7 have a triangulation of expert areas: Data, design, and behavioural science. However, design 
methods (e.g. Double Diamond (Design Council 2005)) are viewed as a fundamental part of their 
process, and thus behavioural science and data are applied and added in each of the design phases. 
• Although the design consultancy states that behavioural theory is applied in each phase of a project, 
theory selection is composed of a non-structured selection process on the basis of each designer’s 
individual “back catalog” (i.e. the 10-30 most common biases (Kahneman, 1974) or existing theory 
knowledge).
• A tool that would secure a minimum of behavioural knowledge and thus point the designer in the 
correct direction would be beneficial, as the behavioural design field is complex and requires expertise, 
which takes time to gain. 
Answers
1.
No.
Almost all projects is about behaviour change or about creating a behaviour that does not exist. If we can eliminate the 
behaviour, it is also very fine. We only accept the project if it consist of elements of behaviour. 
 
The client company contact /KL.7 with projects, yet the projects differ so it is not always that /KL.7 are involved from start to 
end in the whole behavioural design process.  
 
“In fact, we are probably best when we do the analysis and the concepts, and when they [the concepts] are to be cast in bronze 
and such things, then we will pull back. As long as we are allowed to decide how the concepts should look. [..] Yes [answer for 
that they are included until prototype level] [..] Then we have someone externally to shape the concepts and then we will take 
over at the measurement again.” - Designer from /KL.7

Answers

4.

3.
Double diamond is always put on the behavioural design processes (if possible), but is more a model that we have in our heads 
(a kind of mental model) when we work through the process:
 
1. Analysis.
 
2. Idea generation.
 
3. Conceptualisation.
 
4. Test.
Design methods are fundamental to what we do, but we always apply behavioural science and data in all steps. This 
behavioural design process is not called a general design process, because we have a lot of expertise knowledge that we bring 
into each step - especially the first one: what has been done before?
 
Types of projects: 
 
– Behavioural strategy
 
– Behavioural campaign (often trying to communicate to system 2) e.g. Realdania as client 
 
– Behavioural studies (analysis + testing of solutions on concept level through e.g. mock-ups) 
 
– Behavioural analysis (What is the problem? Why does the behaviour occur?) e.g. Grundfos as client
Usually there is a good understanding of the problem because the client companies have tried everything else and now goes to 
/KL.7 as a last resort. Yet, we still make problem definition, which includes (objective and quantitative (digital data)):
 
– Investigate if there is data we can use?
 
– Which behaviour exist?
 
“We also apply work models from Beyer & Holtzblatt “Contextual Design” [e.g. flow model] in order to identify contact points 
where the behaviour occurs“ - Designer from /KL.7
Duhigg with How Habits Work 
Kahneman system 1 and system 2
Richard Dawkins with The Middle World model
 
Non-structured selection process of theories on the basis of the “back catalogue” (knowledge about the 10-30 most common 
biases). Some projects are better with some theories than others.
 
"We decide in the beginning with a project definition which heuristics and biases plays a role in this project" - Designer from 
/KL.7
 
“[..] and sometimes it is kind of flavour of the month, if somebody just read a nice book. [..] No no no [answering if the theory 
inclusion process is structured], it is from the back-catalogue, but there are also some things we know work well from other 
projects. So there are definitely some things that go again.” - Designer from /KL.7
 
"It's not something that can be read anywhere, but it requires a back-catalogue, a knowledge of at least 10-20, maybe 30 most 
common biases [..] but, there is a back-catalogue with the most commonly used [biases] that you quickly get into when you are 
here" - Designer from /KL.7
 
Also draws on the network's knowledge and experience.
 
E.g. uses the Behaviour Model (Fogg 2009b) to idea generation in terms of evaluating the ideas and identify where the ideas 
could be better. Also uses Pyramid model: Design & Interaction vectors made by Rune Nørager.
 
The approach can change due to practical circumstances or when you get more insights by talking to others.
 
When getting new employees there certainly are an expectation of a basic knowledge base (system 1 and 2 - dual processing 
theory, behavioral economics) and interest to learn more .
Classical project management challenges.
 
To get acquainted with a lot of professionalism and expertise - understand it, read it etc. 
 
“We are rarely stuck due to a lack of theories, as there are so many theories you could include. It is more theory vs. time 
pressure, usually balancing of a lack of time compared to getting in depth” - Designer from /KL.7

Other
Behavioural design in general
Human behaviour can almost always be measured, so measuring is a huge part of behavioural design.
Instead of watching behavioural design as a new approach to design, it is in our optic a basic approach to everything that 
humans touch/interact with. If there is no behavioural science behind, then it wont work. Behavioural design is above every-
thing related to product design, system design, service design, process design, information campaigns and so on.
 
/KL.7’s expert areas
Triangulation of expert areas:
 
– Behavioural science (engineering, economics, communication).
 
– Design (methods and processes). 
 
– Data (technology and statistik).
Experts in what shape humans.
 
Assumption: deep rationality
We do not like to talk about behavioural economics because here humans have a strange perspective as human being rational. 
Some others try to soften this by saying that humans are predictably irrational. But it is a false premise. We are irrational 
because the models that we have putted on us from the beginning, are not correct. In fact, there are lots of things that influence 
our decisions that can not be put into a model. Therefore we talk in KL/7 about deep rationality - there is a meaning that we as 
human stick to the flock for example. This is a perspective that we have in addition to behavioural economists.
 
References that Sebastian mentions
Dan Norman “The Design of Everyday Things” (from the 1980s)
Richard Dawkins “The God Delusion”
We human live in a middle world because we can’t understand things that are big or small og too long a way or too long since 
Rune Nørager “Design Psychology”
User-centered design: Ask the users 
Human-centered design: Ask the users + expert knowledge
 
Important comments in regards to the research framework
“It is difficult to understand behavioural design, as there is a lot of stuff to understand” - Designer from /KL.7
“If you are expert within a field, it requires expertise and time to gain this. But if you develop a tool that at least secures a 
minimum of knowledge to point the designer in the correct direction...” - Designer from /KL.7

Key findings
• Projects are in The Nudging Company distinguished between three themes: Strategy, compliance, 
and campaign model. Each of these themes determines the process and how behaviour is mapped 
explorative, however The Nudging Company are always a part of the entire behavioural design process 
regardless of the project theme.
• The Nudging Company relies on many behavioural theories throughout the behavioural design 
process, yet the most used is theory of Heuristics and Biases (Tversky & Kahnemann 1974), where all 
the employees at The Nudging Company have an understanding of the different categories of biases, 
but the behavioural economist is the expert in them. The behavioural designer takes the behavioural 
theories that the behavioural economist has identified with her in the design process if it fits into the 
design theory/frame.
• Projects in The Nudging Company differs between individual work (e.g. analysis) and group work (e.g. 
idea generation) and mainly there are 2-4 employees pr. project, where the competences differ from 
project to project. 
• The biggest challenge in The Nudging Company is to get enough information and then proper deliver 
this information to another employee by the phase shifts, so that no valuable insights and informations 
are lost in the process.
Answers
1.
No.
All projects are related to behavioural design and client companies contact The Nudging Company with a more or less defined 
issue.
 
Sometimes the problem can be easily solved (e.g. the disposal of coffee cups), but because of economic resources, The 
Nudging Company rejects the offer as it would cost to much versus the small problem it would solve. 
 
“Yes, roughly speaking [answer to that the projects always run from problem definition to prototype and testing]. It has been 
structured like this for the last period of time [..]. However, sometimes the clients have their own testing systems that they want 
to use.” - Designer from The Nudging Companyn
 
Longest projects are 3-4 months and multiple projects happen at the same time.
 
“When clients come to us, they have an expectation of something creative. Humans own thinking on creative is “noisy” and 
remarkable.” - Behavioural economist from The Nudging Company
 
“Humans expect that when they have a complex problem, they must have a complex solution. But this does not add up. The 
point is that you must have a simple solution.” - Behavioural economist from The Nudging Company

Cognitive psychology

Also look on other good cases that others have made to get inspiration. 
 
Default mindset.
 
Biases (some of them):
 
– Social proof (If you are unsure about something, then the easiest way to remove your doubt, is by looking at others).

– Salience (the remarkable).

“All [the employees at The Nudging Company] have an understanding of these categories of biases, but I am the expert in 
them."- Behavioural economist from The Nudging Company
 
“We have taken and benefited from 70 years of research on behaviour and stuffed many biases and heuristics types within 
several different categories, and then it's about going through them. So you have the problem and then you force yourself to 
only look at this category (eg. Friction). Boundaries create creativity. When the relevant categories then are selected, you can 
begin to delve into them and read up on theories.” - Behavioural economist from The Nudging Company
 
The designer takes the behavioural theories that the behavioural economist has identified with her in the design process if it fits 
into the design theory/frame.
 
“Practical challenges contributes to that the focus might change, but it is rare that there is a need for more theoretical insight it

When getting new employees there is no certain theories that the employee must learn. He/she just have to be a good at what 
he/she is specialised in.
“[..] therefore it is extra important to deliver the information as beneficial as possible in order to not loose the existing informa-

“Humans are miserable to talk about behaviour.” - Behavioural economist from The Nudging Company

Behavioural economist from The Nudging Company
 
“When getting stuck in the process it is rarely because of lack of knowledge, but practical challenges. We always stick to our 
premise.” - Designer from The Nudging Company

Appendix 8: Workshop 1 & 2
Throughout the first phase of the support tool development, two workshops were held to ensure proper and 
objective inclusion of the designers’ needs and to prevent any thesis bias to be reflected in the different activities. 
Although the aim of each of the workshops had been determined beforehand, different contributions were 
emphasised and important considerations were brought to attention:
• 
Workshop 1: In order to develop a valuable support tool, the aspects of for whom will the tool have 
effect, and when will the tool have effect need to be clarified, as these have great impact on the application 
and the lifetime of the support tool. Furthermore, these two aspects are essential to determine, as 
they have a big impact on the perceived value of the tool. If different features are aimed at the wrong 
users, the application of the support tool will be misinterpreted and possibly misunderstood.
• 
Workshop 2: Value Proposition Design is a valuable methodology to apply in a support tool 
development phase, as it highlights all the important aspects that need consideration if a product is to 
be sold (i.e. valuable for all product design). However, the method is based upon market observations 
unavailable in this thesis). Thus, combining the methodology with the user scenario method, showed to 
be fairly meaningful, as it allowed the users to put the definition of “what will bring value?” into more 
easy-understandable and translatable words. However, the combination of activities in the workshop 
made it possible to find out what the users need, not what they say they want, which was found to be 
an important distinction in this case.

Appendix 14: Requirements specification (1 of 2)
Topic
Requirements
Success Criteria
Unit
Comments
Purpose
The primary purpose of the 
tool is to be a step-by-step 
front-end analytical tool.
The aim of the tool is to help 
designers analyse all 
problem layers and make 
well-argued front-end 
decision-making processes. 
No. of 
applications 
in different 
behavioural 
design 
project 
phases
Must provide guidance for the 
task of completing a 
problem analysis and 
connecting problem aspects 
to the relevant behavioural 
theories and the further 
design work of a behavioural 
design project.
The tool should always refer 
to the behavioural design 
elements of problem 
behaviour, models of 
behaviour and theories of 
change, while at the same 
time consider the element of 
design approaches.
No. of 
elements
The BDS relates to both 
problem, models of 
behaviour, theories of change 
and design approaches, and 
therefore it is important that 
the tool provides guidance for 
all four aspects.
Must enable integrative 
thinking by applying 
behavioural theories from all 
categories.
Each problem analysis should 
refer to at least one theory 
from all three categories 
(individual, social and 
change theories).
No. of 
categories
Physical 
appearance
Must be a hands-on tool that 
is easy to use and understand.
Should enable a practical 
application in the behavioural 
design process.
No. of 
people 
interacting
In order for the tool to be 
successful, designers need to 
apply the tool and should 
want to apply the tool, thus 
the tool can neither be too 
rigid (i.e. boring) nor add 
further complexity to the 
behavioural design  process.
Must be presented in a 
simple way and should be 
effortless in its application.
The required time for 
understanding the tool (i.e. 
the time from the tool is 
presented to the application 
starts) should be maximum 
10 minutes.
No. of 
minutes
As the tool deals with a 
complex part of the 
behavioural design process, 
it should be accompanied 
with a simple guide or 
instructions for the use of the 
tool.
Application 
Time
Must be timesaving to use 
the tool, compared with 
current practice.
The duration of the tool 
should be around 60 minutes 
and maximum 90 minutes.
No. of 
minutes
It is important that the total 
time of the tool is maximum 
60-90 minutes, in order to 
obtain a certain level of 
thoroughness, while at the 
same time avoiding being too 
comprehensive or 
time-consuming.
Must provide the designer with 
a structured overview of 
available behavioural 
theories related to a range of 
problems, in order to check for 
relevant behavioural theories 
and obtain theoretical insights 
applicable for the further 
behavioural design process.
The tool must refer to a range 
of relevant behavioural 
design topics and a 
representative number of 
behavioural theories both 
models of behaviour and 
theories of change.
No. of 
behavioural 
design 
topics
No. of 
behavioural 
design
theories
Designers without a 
theoretical back-catalogue of 
behavioural theories need to 
acquire an initial under-
standing of behavioural 
theories, in order to increase 
the possibility of developing 
successful behavioural 
interventions.
In order to make a valid 
front-end decision in regards to 
behavioural design theory 
inclusion, the tool needs to 
ensure that the designer has 
considered or touched upon all 
elements of the BDS. Doing so 
will minimise the risk of tunnel 
vision and repeating mistakes, 
as well as minimise the risk of 
overlooking relevant 
behavioural design theories.

Appendix 14: Requirements specification (2 of 2)
Accessibility 
Must for all designers be 
easy to acquire.
Should be easy to acquire, 
either by printing it, 
creating it or buying it at an 
affordable price.
Amount of 
$
User
Must be applicable for both 
designers with limited 
experience of behavioural 
design and for designers 
with comprehensive expe-
rience of behavioural 
design.
No.of 
projects 
where 
behavioural 
design has 
been 
applied
Context
Must be possible to integrate 
in other front-end and 
post-project design 
activities, i.e. the tool 
cannot not be disruptive with 
the current behavioural 
design context.
As the purpose of the tool is 
aimed at different process 
phases, the tool should 
complement current 
behavioural design 
activities, through clear 
visualisation of process 
placement and progress.
Placement 
on project 
timeline, 
i.e. process 
stage
In order to make it easier for 
the designer to apply the tool 
in current behavioural design 
context, the tool must show the 
different process steps and the 
current progress in the tool, 
i.e. what have been done and 
what needs to be done.
Must provide the opportunity 
of applying the tool in an 
individual as well as a group 
context.
The tool should be applicable 
for minimum one person and 
maximum four persons.
No. of 
people
As there is no standard way of 
performing the analysis 
phase nor the evaluation 
phase of behavioural design 
(in regards to design 
staging), it is necessary that 
both individuals as well as 
groups can apply the tool.
Must provide clear and 
simple ways of ensuring 
process documentation.
The tool should enable 
structured ways of 
documenting the analysis 
phase of a behavioural design 
project, without being the 
main focus of the tool 
application. 
No. of 
documentations
Documentation of the 
decision-making processes of 
the analytical phase is 
necessary in order to validate 
the decisions, as well as 
being able to evaluate the 
chosen focus and theory 
path.
Maintenance
Must be continuously updated 
(in regards to available 
behavioural theories) in order 
to ensure constant relevance to 
the field of behavioural design.
No. of new 
behavioural 
theories 
included
Behavioural design is still an 
emerging field, and therefore 
it is necessary that the tool is 
continuously updated in order 
to ensure on-going relevance 
to the field.
Must be customisable in 
regards to the user (i.a. 
company) of the tool. 
No. of 
adjustment 
possibilities
If the tool can be customised 
for different designers and/or 
companies, it is more likely 
that they will take ownership 
of the tool.

Appendix 15: Workshop 3 & 4
Throughout the second phase of the support tool development, two workshops were held to involve the users 
in the ideation and conceptualisation process and to get inspiration from their initial ideas and needs regarding 
layout and interface. 
Although the aim of each of the workshops had been determined beforehand, different contributions were 
emphasised and important considerations were brought to attention:
• 
Workshop 3: Contributed with features and elements that the suport tool could include in order to 
be operationalised and add value for the designer. For instance, inclusion/presentation of behavioural 
theories in the support tool should be visualised and written to appear not too locked and neither too 
open for interpretation. Moreover, this workshop provided valuable insights in regards to the support 
tool’s possibilities and limitations.
• 
Workshop 4: Contributed directly with valuable ideas and suggestions for support tool layout and 
features, as this workshop was held as an open brainstorm session.

Appendix 17: Workshop 5 - 13
Throughout the final phase of the support tool development, nine workshops were performed, all contributing 
to five overall iterations in order to improve and develop the most valuable Super Cool Analysis Tool.
Although the aim of each of the workshops had been determined beforehand, different contributions were 
emphasised and important considerations were brought to attention. As the link from the workshops to the 
iterations is stated in the table above and the contributions for each workshop are illustrated in the tool iteration 
figures (figure 5.11-5.14 in the report) there is no subject to further elaboration here.

This Guide Book will help you through the game, visualise 
game elements and give instructions for the usage both before, 
during and after the game
1-4 players
60-90 min.

Appendix 18: Guide Book (2 of 7)
How to understand the game
What is the Super Cool Analysis Tool? The Super Cool 
Analysis Tool is a game aimed at helping you make better problem analysis and 
behaviour hypotheses applicable in your design projects concerning Behavioural 
Design (or Design for Behaviour Change). The aim of the game is supported 
by the belief that in order to develop the most successful solutions, you have to 
achieve a deep level of problem understanding first. This is a crucial aspect to keep 
in mind in regards to the aim of the game, as you otherwise may misunderstand 
the game and therefore not achieve the output you hoped for. 
Who should apply this game? The game can be played by any 
kind of designer, yet it is the perfect game for 
• 
Experienced behavioural designers who wish broaden their horizon 
with new behavioural theories and 
• 
Designers with no or limited experience with behavioural design who 
wish to explore the (many!) benefits behavioural analysis have to offer 
different kinds of design work or to obtain relevant insights into 
human behaviour.
Although the game’s imagined user is a designer, the tool’s language and tasks could 
complement users of other professions as well (such as behavioural economist, 
sociologists, psycohologist). No matter the profession, the game can be played by 
either individuals or groups, however we recommend you to be maximum four 
people playing the game (otherwise split the team into two groups). This is due to 
the fact that being more than four people in one group will require more time to 
reach the same level of output, and thus the duration will be more than 90 minutes.
When should the game be played? As the game helps 
you  make better problem analysis and behaviour hypotheses, the game should 
2
Why should I play this game? The Super Cool Analysis Tool 
will help you by providing an overview of different possibly relevant behavioural 
theories and the insights each of them could offer you, as well as providing better 
possibilities of translating these insights into your design work (by helping you 
formulate and rank different problem hypotheses that could apply to your project).  
be played in the early-phase of your design 
work – more specificly in between the 
discovery and the definition of the problem 
presented in your design project (as you 
can see in the Double Diamond figure (Design 
Council 2007)). 
Discover

A quick guide to start the game: 
Although we recommend you (or at least one from your group) to read this Guide Book 
before playing the game, the following quick guide explains how to start the game quickly: 
• 
Place the Theory Overview Wheel so that all players can see it, and give each 
player their own set of Theory Cards (at all times all players must discuss the same 
Theory Card)
• 
Based on the chosen game-mode (page 6), you (as a group) should work your way 
thorugh the Theory Cards, meaning: 
1. 
Read the brief description and the relating bias
2. 
Read and understand the applied problem case
3. 
Complete the “force yourself” task
4. 
Complete the three remember-tasks on the back of the Theory Card
5. 
Go through the decision-tree and determine further steps of the game
6. 
Finally, again depending on your chosen game-mode, reach the “End Card” and 
finish the game successfully! 
• 
In order to make sure that you progress successfully through the game, keep the 
following two time frames in mind: 
1. 
If you struggle for more than five minutes in trying to formulate a hypothesis 
in relation to the theory – move on to the ne[t Theory Card stated on the 
backside of the current Theory Card.
2. 
Flip the hour-glass everytime you pick a new Theory Card – try not to spend 
more than 10 minutes on each card (you can always come back to it!)

Appendix 18: Guide Book (3 of 7)
Game contents
• 1 x Theory Overview Wheel that visualises the 21 different 
Theory Cards in regards to eight different topics (see below) relevant to 
behavioural theories, as well as how the different theories are related 
within the three categories of individual behaviour (the inner circle), social 
behaviour (the middle circle) and behaviour change (the outer circle). 
• 4 x stacks of post-its (one for each player) and 4 pens (one 
for each player) use for documenting your hypotheses, which can be 
placed in the hypotheses-box on the Theory Overview Wheel
• 1 x bag of 49 small discs you should use for keeping track of both 
the Theory Cards you have considered so far and the topics you have covered 
(put them on the letter representing the Theory Card). Keep in mind that most 
of the letters are represented on the Theory Overview Wheel several times. 
ATTITUDE
       A settled way of thinking or    
      feeling
THINKING
       Conscious (reflective) or nonconscious 
       (automatic) processing of information
CHANGE STRATEGIES
       A plan of action to achieve a 
       behaviour change
MOTIVATION
       Commitment and interest in a 
       given situation
SOCIAL IMPACT
       Group impact and/or environmental 
      impact on the individual’s behaviour
SELF-EFFICACY
       Confidence and belief in oneself  
       to achieve a certain result
HABITS
       Nonconscious and routine based 
       behaviour
PRINCIPLES of 
BEHAVIOUR
      General rules of thumb that 
     describe behaviour
4
NB! Although a facilitator is not stated as a necessity, using one when playing would 
probably ensure better results and more structure of the game. Therefore, if you wish to use 
a facilitator, (s)he should consider the following things before playing: 
• 
Have I read and understood the game? (it will save you time while playing!) 
• 
How big is the team who should play the game? Do we need to split the team into smaller 
groups?
• 
Am I open-minded in regards to the output of the tool? 
• 
How many times should we play the game? 
• 
How do we ensure the needed documentation? Who is responsible for transferring the 
results from the game into the project we are working on? 
Game contents
What does the game contain? 
• 1 x Overall Guide Book (you are reading it)
• 1 x 10 minutes hour glass
• 1 x Begin Card to guide you into the game, by getting you 
to start assessing your problem and analysing it in regards to seven 
different problem layers:
• 1 x  Problem Documentation Card to guide the 
problem layer tasks on both the Begin Card and End Card.
• 1 x End Card to help you validate your problem analysis and 
to help you realise the potential and strenght of your formulated 
hypotheses.
• 4 x sets of Theory Cards, containing 
• 21 x Theory Cards in the three categories individual 
behaviour (8 pcs), social behaviour (5 pcs) and behaviour change 
(8 pcs)

How to enter the game
*ame-mode 1
If you deal with a 
Vaguely specified 
problem: 
The purpose of this game 
mode is to, within 90 minutes, 
create three specific, realistic, 
and feasible hypotheses 
based on a vaguely specified 
problem. 
An example of a vaguely 
specified problem could be 
“reduce the amount of cigarette 
butts thrown in Copenhagen”. 
This problem gives no 
indication of the target group 
nor the root causes of the 
problem.
To play this mode, you/your 
group should start with the 
Begin Card, yet when 
entering the game you should 
choose the path that leads 
you to choose between 
the two categories 
of Theory Cards, and 
thereafter go through the 
theory cards all the way to 
the End Card.  
*ame-mode 2
If you deal with a 
Well-specified 
problem: 
The purpose of this game 
mode is to, within 90 minutes, 
create three specific, realistic, 
and feasible hypotheses 
based on a well-specified 
problem. 
An example of a well-
specified problem could be 
“reduce the amount of cigarette 
butts left at train platforms in 
Copenhagen”. This problem 
gives no clear indication of the 
root causes of the problem, 
yet it indicates the main target 
group. 
To play this mode, you/your 
group should start with the 
Begin Card, yet when 
entering the game, you should 
choose the path that leads 
you straight to the Theory 
Overview Wheel, and 
thereafter go through the 
theory cards all the way to 
the End card.  
*ame-mode 
If your want to achieve 
Inspiration for 
behaviour
The purpose of this game 
mode is to, within 90 minutes, 
get inspired in regards to the 
21 behavioural theories and 
obtain an understanding of 
the theories similarities and 
differences.
This game mode is entirely 
determined by your own 
choices as the Theory 
Cards and the Theory 
Overview Wheel offer 
a lot of valuable insights 
themselves. 
This game mode can 
therefore be several things: 
Browsing through the Theory 
Cards for inspiration 
for a project (or just for 
fun!), assessing the Theory 
Overview Wheel to acquire 
an overview of the 
theories or something entirely 
different – it is up to you! 
Pick one of the three game-modes to determine how to enter the game:
6
1.
2.
3.
NB! When choosing game-mode 2 the Begin Card prompts you to determine whether 
your project concerns the behaviour of an individual or of a group. This can be a tricky question 
to answer, as there is the possibility that your problem relates to both kinds of behaviours. So, 
in order to answer this question, the Problem Documentation Card will help you, as it gives 
you an indication of what problems layers affects your problem the most. Use this reflection to 
get started the right place in the game! 
How to prepare and play the game
5
Preparation for the game: 
1. 
Fold out the Theory Overview Wheel and place it for all to see.
2. 
Fold out the disc-bag and together with the Begin Card, Problem 
Documentation Card and End Card put it next to the Theory Overview 
Wheel.
3. 
Give each player their own set of Theory Cards.
4. 
Look through the game-modes and determine how you wish to enter 
the game. 
Playing the game: While the game-modes determines how you 
enter the game, the following steps describes how the game is played out: 
1. 
Flip the (10 minutes) hour-glass everytime you pick a new Theory Card. 
2. 
Read the brief description and the relating bias on the chosen Theory 
Card.
3. 
Read and understand the applied problem case
4. 
Complete the “force yourself” task in relation to your project problem.
5. 
Complete the three °remember±-tasNs on the back of the Theory Card:
6. 
Write your hypothesis on a post-it.
7. 
Write the theory-letter on your post-it (to backtrack your decisions).
8. 
Cover the theory letters with the discs (to realise progress).
9. 
Go through the decision-tree on the back of the Theory Card and 
determine the further steps of the game.
10. You should be able to move through one Theory Card in ma[imum 10 
minutes. If it takes you longer, try to wrap it up and move on. You can 
always come back to it later! 
11. Once enough cards have been considered (the game will tell you when that 
is), reach the End Card and finish the game successfully!
NB! Each of the Theory Cards has a task, which “forces” you/your group to formulate a 
hypothesis that should describe how a given theory applies to your problem (if you prefer 
two hypotheses, you can of course write both of them). An example of a hypothesis could be 
“people throw cigarette butts at the train platforms, due to a convenience need and due to time 
pressure”. For the Theory Cards it is important that you attempt to formulate a hypothesis, 
yet if you struggle for more than five minutes in trying to make sense of a specific theory 
in relation to your problem – move on to the ne[t Theory Card (found on the back of 
the card)  – it is not a nessecity to formulate a hypothesis from all theories, it is merely the 
reflection over each theory’s relevance that is important.

Appendix 18: Guide Book (5 of 7)
How to update the game
Why would I want to update the game? Although the 
game has been developed to fit different companies, projects and problems, the 
possibility of updating the game has been included in the case that:
• 
You are super-experienced at behavioural design and would like to apply 
the game, yet some of the theories that you find to be very valuable in your 
projects are not included in the Theory Cards.
• 
You aren’t super-experienced at behavioural design, yet you would still like 
to learn and expand your behavioural theory back-catalogue – and to do so 
fully, you need the possibility of adding other theories as well.
Luckily, we have taken these scenarios into account and made it super simple 
for you to customise the Super Cool Analysis Tool to fit your context
How do I add new theory cards? Adding Theory Cards to the 
game is actually very simple and intuitive, all you have to do is: 
• 
)ind the empty template Theory Cards and fill in the information from 
the given theory you wish to add. To do so, you have to realise whether 
the theory is describing individual behaviour, social behaviour or 
behaviour change. You also have to reflect upon which of the eight 
topics the theory refers to. 
• 
Give the new theory a letter at the top of the Theory Card (a letter that 
has not been used for the other Theory Cards). If this is the first theory 
card you are adding, the letter would be V.
• 
All the Theory Cards are linked to each other, meaning that they are all a 
part of a big chain. When adding a new Theory Card you must therefore 
find the right place in the chain. To do so, there are only two simple 
rules to follow: 
1. 
The same card colour (individual, social or change) must not be next to 
each other in the chain – i.e. the Theory Cards before and after must be 
different than the one you are adding. 
2. 
The Theory Card can only have one topic in common which the Theory 
Cards next to it, i.e. the Theory Cards before and after should be 
somewhat describing another behaviour context. 
• 
Once you have found the place in the chain, be sure to remember to 
update the card-linNing bo[es on the Theory Card before and the 
Theory Card after - so the chain will not be broken when you play the 
game! 
• 
Finally, all you have to do is add the Theory Card to the Theory Overview 
Wheel. Make sure to place it in the correct circle – i.e. in the correct topics 
and in the correct category of behavioural theories. 
• 
Once this is done, you have customised the game and are good to 
go again! 
8
How to end the game
7
Ending the game: Based on the game you have now formulated 
several hypotheses relevant to the behavioural problem you are trying 
to adress. Based on these hypotheses, the End Card will guide you 
through the task of ending the game.
This task will help you/your group to reflect upon the results from the 
game and ultimately help you transfer the insights you have obtained from 
playing the game into your further design work.  
Once you agree that you have the best hypotheses you wish to apply 
in your further activities, remember to: 
• 
Take the Theory Cards (from which your chosen hypotheses 
have been formulated). 
• 
Consider the key takeaways each individual Theory Card has 
to offer in your furhter design process.
• 
To provide even more inspiration for the further design 
process, the table on the backpage of this Guide Book 
shows different design approaces that could possibly benefit 
you and your project!

How to understand the Theory Cards
1. Symbol and colour illustrating the Theory 
Card category. 
2. Letter illustrating the Theory Card “no.” and its 
placement on the Theory Overview Wheel. 
Name of the behavioural theory. 
Brief theoretical description of the theory.
A reference, if interest in the specific theory has 
been awoken and further information is wanted.
A behaviour bias, relevant to either the theory 
on the card or the problem case on the card. Biases 
are easy-to-understand phrases developed to describe 
a certain type of behaviour - they are therefore great 
to consider in regards to your problem as well.  
An example of a case, to show how the theory 
could be valuable in a specific context. Do not let 
this example limit the application of the theory – it 
is merely meant as a clearer way to understand the 
theory. Even though your problem in no way relates 
to the problem example on the card, the theory 
can still be valuable! 
Together with each problem case is an illustration 
relating to either the way the theory works or showing 
the figure originally formulated together with the theory. 
Social Identity Theory
%pplied pVSFleQ case
Why are people eating more healthy things at home 
than they are at work? 
For a problem like this, environmental context 
is valuable to consider, yet explaining it from the 
perspective of Social Identity Theory adds 
another layer of knowledge. The roles which the 
employees take part in influences the behaviours. 
Perhaps the wife is super active and will only eat 
salad, which leads to the husband eating healthier 
at home, and perhaps “relaxing” a bit on the 
health-front at work (or opposite). Or maybe, the 
majority of the employees are eating healthy, 
and in order to not be “outside” of the 
group, the husband eats healthy at work – but 
not at home, where his wife loves pizza and pasta.
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can your problem be explained by either ingroup behaviour 
or out-group behaviour?
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Social Identity Theory offers insights as to why individuals 
strive to achieve or maintain a positive social identity. It states that individuals act on behalf 
of (in-)group concerns (being part of the group) and not on behalf of personal concerns. 
Individuals, when interacting within a certain group context, acquire different social roles, or 
“levels of self”, which influences their behaviour. The theory distinguishes between interpersonal 
behaviour (two or more individuals acting solely on the grounds of individual concerns and 
characteristics, e.g. wife and husband) and intergroup behaviour (two or more individuals that 
behave solely on the grounds of social concerns and characteristics, e.g. soldiers in an army 
group).
Reference: 
Tajfel, H. & Turner, J., 1979. An Integrative Theory of Intergroup Conflicts. In The Social Psychology of Intergroup Relations. 
Brooks Cole Publishing, pp. 33–47.
J
        A bias relating to the theory on this card is the Ingroup bias: 
       The tendency for people to give preferential treatment to others they perceive to be 
members of their own groups

Appendix 19: Begin Card
Begin Card
front
*iVsX XLiRgs fiVsX Since you have chosen to play this game, we imagine that you 
are faced with a problem concerning some type of behaviour – or at least you think so!
The following task will get you started in the structuring and analysis of the problem behaviour 
that you have identified. This task will ease the usability of the game and is therefore 
valuable to the process, but if you are short of time or already have a rather thorough 
understanding of your problem, you can skip this task and go straight to the back of this card. 
If not, start the game by identifying how the following seven problem layers affect 
the problem individually (based on you initial perception of the problem). If there are any 
problem layers in which you cannot identify your problem, don’t worry – the game should 
guide you through them anyway. As a group fill out the problem layers you are able to identify 
on post-its and place them on the front of the “Problem Documentation card”. 
Once this is done, you can go to the back of the Begin Card and start the game!  
8Le seZeR pVSFleQ layeVs aVe
Personal problems relate to internal personal factors such 
as health and psyche.
Impersonal problems relate to external personal factors 
such as lack of money and time.
Interpersonal problems relate to internal social factors 
such as arguing with a friend or disagreeing with a study group, 
which can be seen as in-group behaviour.
Societal problems relate to external social factors such as 
crime rates, littering, and pollution, which can be seen as out-group 
behaviour.
Immediate story refers to the things prior to when the 
behaviour occurs, i.e. the bus was late, spilled coffee, or a broken 
phone.
Longterm background refers to the mood, place in life, 
environment, or childhood experiences of the individual.
Frequency of change refers to the span or frequency of 
the problem behaviour, i.e. is it an seldom behaviour or a frequent 
behaviour?

should answer the following questions (if in doubt about the questions, the Guide Book gives 
you detailed descriptions and examples):

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (1 of 21)
AIDS Risk Reduction Model
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How can I get more people to wear bike helmets?
1. Recognition and labelling: Make it clear 
that the behaviour is a high risk behaviour (can 
you show examples of traffic accidents, maybe 
have earlier implicated victims tell their story?)
2. Commitment: How can you make it more 
beneficial for the users/how can you make it 
seem as being more beneficial to use a helmet# 
3. Taking action: Is it easy to take action? 
Are there both cheap and expensive helmets? 
Coloured vs. black# Messes up your hair# What 
factors limit this action-taking at the moment? 
Are there enough information to make a 
qualified decision# 
&VieJ descVipXiSR The AIDS Risk Reduction Model aims to explain and 
predict behaviour change efforts of individuals through the three stages of recognition and 
labelling, commitment, and taking action. Although being developed for the focus of predicting 
behaviours regarding sexual transmission of AIDS or HIV, the theory is valuable in regards 
to understanding when to change behaviour, as each of the three stages represents different 
lenses through which behaviour change can be viewed. The theory is also valuable in regards 
to considering individual behaviour, as it among others considers emotional influences and 
interpersonal processes.
Reference: 
Catania, J.A., Kegeles, S.M. & Coates, T.J., 1990. Towards an Understanding of Risk Behavior: An AIDS Risk Reduction Model 
(ARRM). Health Education Quarterly, 17(1), pp.53–72.
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can the terms recognition and labelling, commitment, or taking 
action be used to change behaviour in your own problem case?

The refusal to plan for, or react to, a disaster which has never happened before

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (2 of 21)
Design Behaviour Intervention Model
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How can I get people to turn of the lights when 
leaving a room? 
1.  To guide a change, an electrical socket with 
a light bulb (that lights when the sock is turned 
on) could be used. The light in the socket would 
increase the user’s awareness of the electricity 
being used, without forcing any behaviours. 
2. To maintain a change, a lamp with a timer (that 
the user can adjusted when to turn on/off) could 
be used as a way of influencing the user’s habits. 
3. To force a behaviour change, sensors that 
automatically controls the light bulbs (depending 
on movement) could be used. The sensors would 
directly change the user’s behaviour by full product 
control, as the user can only influence the lights 
with his/hers presence. 
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can the terms guiding a change, maintaining a change, or forcing 
a change be used to change behaviour in your own problem case?
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Design Behaviour Intervention Model is valuable in 
describing different strategies for how a behaviour change can take place. These strategies 
are guiding a change (influencing the user’s intentions), maintaining a change (influencing the 
user’s habits with force), and forcing a change (influencing the user with full product control). 
The theory states that change can happen in three different stages: Declarative (awareness of 
one’s intentions), knowledge compilation (controlled by habits), and procedural (controlled by 
the product in use). While the theory works great in the context of sustainability, it can be 
applied for problems with other focuses as well.
Reference: 
Tang, T., 2010. Towards sustainable use: design behaviour intervention to reduce household environment impact. 
Loughborough University.
D
        A bias relating to the problem case on this card is the Hyperbolic Discounting bias: 
               Discounting is the tendency for people to have a stronger preference for more 
immediate payoffs relative to later payoffs

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (3 of 21)
Elaboration Likelihood Model
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How do I make sure that people do not smoke on 
planes?
The central route is efficient to use, once you 
have a person’s attention. Therefore it could be 
valuable to state that smoking is forbidden as a 
part of the security procedure in the beginning of 
the flight (i.e. when people are already reasoning 
with your logic). However, if the person is not 
paying attention to what you are saying, the 
peripheral route should be used. A way to do 
so, could be through small distractions leading to 
the same conclusions (e.g. through small pocket-
books stressing the dangers of smoking on planes 
or through strategically placed icons in the most 
obvious places).
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can the behaviour in your own problem case be changed by addressing 
either the central processing route or the peripheral processing route?
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Elaboration Likelihood Model presents two different 
strategies of influencing the user through different processing routes.  The theory states that 
persuasion can happen via two types of processing routes: The central route (much cognitive 
processing, i.e. rational thinking) and the peripheral route (little cognitive processing, i.e. 
automatic responses). The idea in this theory is that behaviour is guided by attitude, which 
can be influenced by persuasion (e.g. through motivation and ability or context and message). 
Therefore the theory is valuable when considering how to enable a certain behaviour change, 
either through communication of a message or through the placement of an intervention.
Reference: 
Petty, R.E. & Cacioppo, J.T., 1986. The Elaboration Likelihood Model of Persuasion. Advances in Experimental Social 
Psychology, 19, pp.123–205.
G
        A bias relating to the theory on this card is the Framing Effect bias:  
             Drawing different conclusions from the same information, depending on how that 
information is presented

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (4 of 21)
Fogg’s Behaviour Model
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How can I influence people to lose weight?
1. Increasing motivation could be through building 
more gyms close to apartment areas or showing 
clear benefits of losing weight. 
2. Increasing ability could be through making it 
easier to eat healthy (provide cheap but healthy 
meals) or by integrating easy activities during a 
work day (e.g. DHL relay). 
3. Placing different interventions in strategically 
through-out places, such as posters for the benefits 
of walking or visualising walking paths at bus stops 
or fitbit’s activity-tracker, constantly reminding (i.e. 
triggering) people to move.
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can the behaviour in your own problem case be changed by 
the terms motivation, ability, or triggers?
&VieJ descVipXiSR Fogg’s Behaviour Model is valuable in determining which 
factors of behaviour must be intervened or influenced, in order to create a behaviour change. 
Fogg states that behaviour is a product of three factors presented at the same time: Motivation 
(the individual has to be sufficiently motivated), Ability (the individual has to have the ability 
to perform the behaviour), and Triggers (the individual must be triggered to perform the 
behaviour). Although all three factors must be present at a time, the trigger is crucial in 
achieving a behaviour change, as the wrong trigger in the wrong place cannot guarantee the 
desired behaviour.
Reference: 
Fogg, B.J., 2009b. A Behavior Model for Persuasive Design. In 4th International Conference on Persuasive Technology. Clare-
mont, USA: ACM Press, p. Article No.: 40.
B
        A bias relating to the theory on this card is the Dunning-Kruger bias: 
           The tendency for unskilled individuals to overestimate their own ability, and the 
tendency for experts to underestimate their own ability
Fogg’s Behaviour Model

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (5 of 21)
Nudging
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How can I decrease trash being thrown in public 
areas? 
Automatic responses in public can be limited 
due to busyness, crowded areas or different 
purposes. Nudging could enable a behaviour 
change despite of these limitations, e.g. through 
clearly visible footprints on the streets leading to a 
trash bin. These would work in areas with smaller 
crowds (i.e. where the pavement is visible), yet in 
places with huge crowds or where there are only 
cyclists, trash bins with other nudges would be 
needed (e.g. colored or tilted ones). 
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can automatic responses (hint: nudges) be used to change 
behaviour in your own problem case?
&VieJ descVipXiSR Nudging is a theory that is valuable when wishing to create 
interventions that require little effort from people, yet at the same time has a big impact in 
the moment. Nudging theory believes that behaviour can be changed by implementing an 
intervention, which exploits the automatic behaviour of individuals. It is particularly well-suited 
to complex decisions and with a lot of different alternatives to choose from.  It has no clear 
principles of how to change behaviour, but is moreso a mindset of how behaviour change can 
be obtained. Nudging is efficient when needing temporary and superficial behaviour changes, 
which is very valuable in regards to public interventions and thus a theory that is highly 
applicable in this context.
Reference: 
Thaler, R.H. & Sunstein, C.R., 2009. Nudge: Improving Decisions about Health, Wealth, and Happiness 2nd ed., 
The Penguin Group.
H
        A bias relating to the theory on this card is the Choice-supportive bias: 
          In a self-justifying manner retroactively ascribing one’s choices to be more informed 
than they were when they were made

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (6 of 21)
Loughborough Model
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How can I decrease people talking loudly on their 
phones i public transport? 
1. By guiding a change, the influence could be 
communicated as informative through a folder on 
the table, prompting people to consider what they 
“share” with their co-travellers (by talking loudly 
about personal issues).
2. By steering people into a change, a “phone 
booth” (with sound-limiting design) at each end of 
the train wagon could give the illusion of private 
space and thus persuading people to move away 
from their co-travellers.
3. If the choice was to completely ensure the 
change, the solution could be coercive and perhaps 
sensors would remove the signal in the trains, thus 
making calls impossible. 
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Loughborough Model is a theory that considers different 
ways of creating influence. The theory distinguishes between three ways of staging a change: 
Guiding a change (enabling control of the change to the user), steering a change (controlling the 
user’s interaction through affordances and constraints), and ensuring change (forcing action). 
The theory further distinguished between three types of influence: Passive (informative), 
assertive (persuasive), and aggressive (coercive). The theory is created with the aim of solving 
sustainability problems, yet if the aim is to determine where the control of the intervention 
should lie, the model can be applied in other contexts as well.
Reference: 
Lilley, D., 2009. Design for sustainable behaviour: strategies and perceptions. Design Studies, 30(6), pp.704–720.
C
        A bias relating to the theory on this card is the Authority bias: 
         The tendency to attribute greater accuracy to the opinion of an authority figure (unrelated 
to its content) and be more influenced by that opinion
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis

 be used to change behaviour in your own problem case?

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (7 of 21)
Transtheoretical Model
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How can I tackle the challenge of alcoholism? 
As you have to appeal to the stage in which 
the user currently is located, different questions 
relating to these stages could be asked to realise 
the potential solutions. 
4VecSRXeQplaXiSR Is the user aware that 
(s)he is an alcoholic# Can you make them aware# 
'SRXeQplaXiSR Since the user has realised 
that (s)he has a problem, how do you make it 
seem beneficial to change behaviour# 
4VepaVaXiSR JSV acXiSR Is the user ready to 
stop drinking? How can you help him/her to do so?
%cXiSR Can you ease the task of forming new 
habits? Maybe through an app or an bracelet? 
1aiRXeRaRce How can you help the user stay 
away from alcohol in the future? 
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Transtheoretical Model is theory that is great at explaining 
different priorities (in regards to time) that can relate to making a change. The theory describes 
the process of intentional behaviour change through five stages of change: Precontemplation 
(no recognition of need for or interest in change), contemplation (thinking about changing), 
preparation for action (planning for changing), action (adopting new habits), maintenance (on-
going practice of new healthier behaviour). Each of these stages requires different kinds of 
interventions or information, as they must appeal to people with different perception and 
priorities. The theory is valuable as it deepens the understanding of how people perceive 
change and when to time persuasion in order to design successful interventions. 
Reference: 
Prochaska, J.O. & Velicer, W.F., 1997. The Transtheoretical Model of Health Behavior Change. American Journal of Health 
Promotion, 12(1), pp.38–48.
F
        A bias relating to the problem case on this card is the Hostile Attribution bias:
        The tendency to interpret others’ behaviours as having hostile intent, even when 
the behaviour is ambiguous

Theory of Influence
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How can I increase the number of citizens voting? 
6ecipVSciXy Maybe promises/handing out 
favors (cake, free candy, balloons).
'SQQiXQeRX aRd cSRsisXeRcy Enhance

*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can the terms reciprocity, commitment and consistency, social 
proof, authority, liking, or scarcity be used to change behaviour in 
your own problem case?
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Theory of Influence is a set of principles that can help 
understanding how persuasion can differ. The theory consists of six principles of persuasion: 
Reciprocity (people tend to return a favour); ommitment and consistency (when people commit, 
they do not back out); social proof (people trust in things trusted by people we trust); authority 
(people tend to obey authorities); liking (people are easily persuaded by person they like) and 
scarcity (relating to demand). The principles are applicable in targeting different solutions. They 
can be combined or they can stand alone, but most importantly they are open to some level 
of interpretation in regards to enabling a behaviour change.
Reference: 
Cialdini, R.B., 2007. Influence: The Psychology of Persuasion Revised ed., Harper Business.
A
        A bias relating to the problem case on this card is the Authority bias: 
         The tendency to attribute greater accuracy to the opinion of an authority figure (unrelated 
to its content) and be more influenced by that opinion

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (9 of 21)
Social Cognitive Theory
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How can I make people eat more vitamins? 
Since people observe what others do and 
the experiences they find, and relate this with their 
own personal experiences, you need to create 
these positive experiences. This can be done 
by acquiring several “vitamin-speakers”, advocating 
for vitamin use (and showing how and why they 
take vitamins) in appropriate places where people 
will see this. This approach is highly efficient and 
applied among the blogosphere at the moment.
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can your problem be explained by addressing either 
personal experiences or the surrounding group of people?
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Social Cognitive Theory is valuable in relating individuals 
behaviours with behaviours of groups. The theory’s core premise is that an individual’s 
behaviour does not only come from personal experiences, but can also come from observing 
the actions of others and their resulting behavioural consequences. Therefore this theory is 
valuable when dealing with a problem behaviour where the surrounding environment play a 
role in adopting behaviours. Although the theory was developed for health purposes, the core 
premise is applicable for other behaviours too.
Reference: 
Bandura, A., 1986. Social Foundations of Thought and Action: A Social Cognitive Theory 1st ed., Prentice Hall.

        A bias relating to the problem case on this card is the Illusion of External Agency bias:

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (10 of 21)
Social Cognitive 
Theory of Self-regulation
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How can I make people eat more vitamins? 
Since people observe what others do and 
the experiences they find, and relate this with their 
own personal experiences, you need to create 
these positive experiences. This can be done 
by acquiring several “vitamin-speakers”, advocating 
for vitamin use (and showing how and why they 
take vitamins) in appropriate places where people 
will see this. This approach is highly efficient and 
applied among the blogosphere at the moment. 
Yet, besides making sure that people can observe 
others, arguments relating to rationality (such 
as “they are healthy”, “they will make you feel 
better”) is needed too, as they addresses the 
forethought of people.
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can your problem be explained by addressing either the 
users self-influence or the signals the user gets from the surrounding 
environment?
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Social Cognitive Theory of Self-Regulation is valuable 
in relating individuals behaviours with behaviours of groups. It states that human behaviour 
is extensively motivated and regulated by on-going self-influence and that most human 
behaviour happens due to some level of forethought, which happens through different levels 
of psychological sub functions. However, what is important to notice is that, while these self-
regulating systems are set by individuals, they can be based on the observations of others. Thus, 
it is crucial to understand that the operations of these self-regulations are affected by social 
interaction and not solely individually determined.
Reference: 
Bandura, A., 1991. Social Cognitive Theory of Self-Regulation. Organizational Behavior and Human Decision Processes, 50(2), 
pp.248–287.
K
        A bias relating to the problem case on this card is the Ilusion of Asymmetric Insight bias:  
        People perceive their knowledge of their peers to surpass their peers’ knowledge of them

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (11 of 21)
Social Ecological Model 
%pplied pVSFleQ case
Through which channels can I influence people to 
change their minds? 
According to this model, influence can take place 
at: 1. The personal level  – does it appeal to my 
health, gender or age? 2. The interpersonal level 
– what do my family and peers think of this topic? 
3. The organisational level – do my co-workers 
agree or disagree? 4. The communal level – do 
my opinions stand out from what others think? 5. 
The policy level – what politicans agrees with me 
on this subject? Just as an individual can analyse 
the environmental context to change its mind, 
designers can do the same to change peoples’ 
behaviour.
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Social Ecological Model is valuable in overviewing different 
factors affecting behaviour. The model states that behaviour is affected by five different levels 
of influence, which interact across levels: Individual, interpersonal, organisational, community, and 
policy. It emphasises that behaviour both shape and are shaped by the social environment. 
This relationship between an individual and the individual’s social context, is important for the 
designer to acknowledge in behavioural design to understand social behavioural factors and 
their corresponding levels of relations.
Reference: 
Bronfenbrenner, U., 1986. Ecology of the Family as a Context for Human Development: Research Perspectives. 
Developmental Psychology, 22(6), pp.723–742.
I
        A bias relating to the theory on this card is the Courtesy bias: 
        The tendency to give an opinion that is more socially correct than one’s true 
opinion, so as to avoid offending anyone

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (12 of 21)
Social Identity Theory
%pplied pVSFleQ case
Why are people eating more healthy things at home 
than they are at work? 
For a problem like this, environmental context 
is valuable to consider, yet explaining it from the 
perspective of Social Identity Theory adds 
another layer of knowledge. The roles which the 
employees take part in influences the behaviours. 
Perhaps the wife is super active and will only eat 
salad, which leads to the husband eating healthier 
at home, and perhaps “relaxing” a bit on the 
health-front at work (or opposite). Or maybe, the 
majority of the employees are eating healthy, 
and in order to not be “outside” of the 
group, the husband eats healthy at work – but 
not at home, where his wife loves pizza and pasta.
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can your problem be explained by either ingroup behaviour 
or out-group behaviour?
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Social Identity Theory offers insights as to why individuals 
strive to achieve or maintain a positive social identity. It states that individuals act on behalf 
of (in-)group concerns (being part of the group) and not on behalf of personal concerns. 
Individuals, when interacting within a certain group context, acquire different social roles, or 
“levels of self”, which influences their behaviour. The theory distinguishes between interpersonal 
behaviour (two or more individuals acting solely on the grounds of individual concerns and 
characteristics, e.g. wife and husband) and intergroup behaviour (two or more individuals that 
behave solely on the grounds of social concerns and characteristics, e.g. soldiers in an army 
group).
Reference: 
Tajfel, H. & Turner, J., 1979. An Integrative Theory of Intergroup Conflicts. In The Social Psychology of Intergroup Relations. 
Brooks Cole Publishing, pp. 33–47.
J
        A bias relating to the theory on this card is the Ingroup bias: 
       The tendency for people to give preferential treatment to others they perceive to be 
members of their own groups

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (13 of 21)
Socially Responsible Design Theory
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How can I make more people commute by public 
transport than by car? 
This is a problem in many cities due to limited 
parking spaces, air pollution and heavily trafficked 
rush hours. It is therefore clearly a collective 
concern to solve this problem. Yet, why is it 
then so difficult to solve# Could it be due to less 
comfort in a bus than in a car# Could it be due 
to overcrowded trains? Due to delays in public 
transport? Due to few direct connections, making 
commuting in public transport more of a hassle? 
In this example the collective concern which is the 
aim, is stated clearly – yet the causing personal 
concern is what is to be found.
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can your problem be explained by the conflict of individual 
concerns versus collective concerns?
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Socially Responsible Design Theory is valuable as it offers 
insights as to why individuals would act in conflict with collective concerns (issues concerning 
the community). It explains why socially desirable behaviour does not always align with per-
sonally desirable behaviour. This is because individuals often act in accordance with personal 
concerns (“I find this important to me©”), and thus in conflict with the collective concerns (“As 
a community, this is important to us..”). Therefore it is important that the designer understands 
the impact of the collective concerns as well being able to make the personal concerns more 
beneficial in order to make the individuals act out desired social behaviours.
Reference: 
Tromp, N., Hekkert, P. & Verbeek, P.-P., 2011. Design for Socially Responsible Behavior: A Classification of Influence Based on 
Intended User Experience. Design Issues, 27(3), pp.3–19.
M
        A bias relating to the problem case on this card is the Reactance bias: 
      The urge to do the opposite of what someone wants you to do out of a need to resist 
a perceived attempt to constrain your freedom of choice

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (14 of 21)
Dual Process Theory
%pplied pVSFleQ case
Why do so much impulse shopping happen at 
supermarkets?  
A textbook example of system 1 and system 2 is 
the layout of a supermarket. Walking through the 
supermarket, the consumer makes rational decisions 
on what to buy based on a shopping list and logic 
(system 2). However, once the consumer reaches 
the register, all kinds of candies and sweets are 
placed right next to it. Being tired from finding all the 
groceries, and perhaps calculating the amount it will 
cost, the brain is now more open towards irrational 
decisions (system 1). The consumer knows that 
(s)he has shopped only healthy groceries, yet, since 
the chocolate is placed right next to her/him, (s)he 
might as well buy a small one. 
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can your problem be explained by addressing 
system 1 or system 2?
&VieJ descVipXiSR Dual Process Theory is a very valuable theory in regards to 
understanding in what ways individuals process information. The theory states that individuals 
have two ways of processing information: Nonconscious reasoning (fast and implicit, system 
1) and conscious reasoning (slow and explicit, system 2). This means that when designing for 
behaviour change, system 1 will take a long time to change as it relates to habits and routines, 
whereas system 2 may change with persuasion or education, as it relates to intentions and 
rationality. Several experts imply that around 90% of all decisions are based on system 1, 
i.e. automatic behaviour and thus this theory is important to have in mind when designing 
products or systems for behaviour change.
Reference: 
Kahneman, D., 2003. Maps of Bounded Rationality: Psychology for Behavioral Economics. American Economic 
Review, 93(5), pp.1449–1475.
S
        A bias relating to the theory on this card is the Restraint bias: 
     The tendency to overestimate one’s ability to show restraint in the face of temptation
intuition
reasoning
