Appendix 22: Theory Cards (15 of 21)
Health Belief Model
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How can I make more people use the seatbelt when 
driving? 
Relating to the different aspects of the theory, keep 
in mind: First, people must be able to see the point 
of using a seat belt, i.e. that is his/her own belief that 
it is important. This could be done by highlighting that 
the seatbelt will avoid him/her from being thrown out 
of the window. Second, people must have a positive 
expectation for the recommended action of wearing 
a seatbelt (perceived benefits of action). Third, 
you must ensure that people can easily use the seat 
belt with comfort and without the feeling of being 
uncool (barriers to action). The barrier in this case 
can both be physical (pain from wearing the seatbelt) 
or psychological (others haunting him/her for his/her 
choice).
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can the terms convincing the user, creating positive expectations, 
or removing barriers for action be used in your problem case?
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Health Belief Model is a theory applicable for explaining 
different factors that should be considered when predicting behaviours. The theory explains 
and predicts behaviour by focusing on the attitudes and beliefs of individuals. This means that 
an individual’s belief, perceived benefits of action and barriers to action, together with self-efficacy 
explain engagement behaviours. Although the theory was developed for predicting health-
promoting behaviours, it can be applied in other contexts as well.
Reference: 
Rosenstock, I., 1974. Historical Origins of the Health Belief Model. Health Education Monographs, 2(4), pp.328–335.
O
        A bias relating to the problem case on this card is the Risk Compensation bias: 
   The tendency to take greater risks when perceived safety increases

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (16 of 21)
Theory of Heuristics & Biases
%pplied pVSFleQ case
As mentioned above, the theory cannot be applied to 
describe generic behaviour, yet the individual biases 
are applicable to describe a given problem. 
The Anchoring bias: The tendency to rely 
too heavily, or “anchor”, on one trait or piece of 
information when making decisions. E.g. people 
who have just written a high number (completely 
irrelevant to the case) will give higher numeric 
guesses, than people who have written lower 
numbers. Or the Decoy bias: Preferences for 
either option A or B change in favor of option B 
when option C (which is similar to option B but 
in no way better) is presented. These are only two 
biases explaining different behaviour. Webpages 
such as Wikipedia has long lists of biases that can be 
perused to find relevant biases for your problem
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can different biases be used to explain your own problem 
case?
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Theory of Heuristics and Biases is valuable when trying 
to explain behaviour in different contexts which otherwise could be seen as inexplicable 
behaviours. This theory distinguishes itself from the other theories described in this card set, 
as it is not as directly applicable in analysing behaviour. Moreso, it is a set of principles which 
can be applied to reduce complex decisions of propability and value. These principles provide 
fast and easy ways of explaining behaviours, and the three most commonly applied heuristics 
are: representativeness, availability of instances and adjustment from an anchor. When using this 
theory it is important to understand that, the theory itself does not provide a clear model for 
describing behaviour, but rather a set of principles to describe common mistakes in behaviour.
References: 
Tversky, A. & Kahneman, D., 1974. Judgment under Uncertainty: Heuristics and Biases. Science, 185(4157), pp.1124–1131.
T
        A bias relating to the theory on this card is the Bias Blindspot bias: 
  The tendency to see oneself as less biased than other people, or to be able to identify 
more cognitive biases in others than in oneself

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (17 of 21)
How Habits Work
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How can I change a person’s habit about eating cake 
every day in the late afternoon?
What is the routine in question? 
What’s the cue for this routine? Is it hunger? 
Boredom? Low blood sugar? 
What’s the reward? The cookie itself? The 
temporary distraction? Socializing with colleagues? 
In this way you can break down the habit and start 
to investigate what causes the habit and maybe 
you can change it. So if the cue for the routine 
is temporary distraction, then maybe replace the 
need for cake with a walk around the block.
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can the terms cue, routine, or reward explain your current 
problem? What trigger is missing?
&VieJ descVipXiSR How Habits Work is a valuable theory when trying to explain 
how habits influence behaviours and decisions. The theory states that each habit refers to a 
loop, consisting of a cue, a routine, and a reward. By identifying these key elements in any habit, 
an understanding of the habit in question becomes available in terms of isolating the cue 
triggering a certain behaviour. As a result, the theory gives an understanding to the designer 
of how habits operate in order to gain the power over it and change a desired behaviour.
Reference: 
Duhigg, C., 2014. How Habits Work. In The Power of Habit. Why We Do What We Do in Life and Business. Random House 
Trade Paperbacks, pp. 275–286.
R
        A bias relating to the theory on this card is the Loss Aversion bias: 
     The disutility of giving up an object is greater than the utility associated with acquiring it

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (18 of 21)
Self-determination Theory
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How can I get people to recycle bottles?
Sustainability is a topic known to cause 
discussions and different opinions. People that 
are intrinsically motivated to recycle 
bottles, believe that it is for a good cause and see 
enjoyment in knowing that they do something 
good for mother earth. Maybe they have reached 
this intrinsic motivation by watching a movie or 
reading a book about plastic in the oceans. People 
that are not intrinsically motivated, i.e. see no 
enjoyment in taking the gross and sticky bottles 
to specific plastic sorting cans need extrinsic 
motivation instead. This could be in the form of 
a psysical bait, such as an economic prize.
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Self-determination Theory is valuable when dealing with 
problems relating to motivation. The theory offers insights into two kinds of motivation: 
Intrinsic motivation (an individual’s interest and enjoyment in the activity or task itself) and 
extrinsic motivation (activities that are not interesting and therefore an individual must be 
motivated externally). Motivation is a part of many other theories describing behaviour, yet 
what this theory states is that people need to witness their own competences (learn skills), 
connection (experience a sense of belonging), and autonomy (feel in control of own behaviours 
and goals) in order to change behaviour.
Reference: 
Deci, E.L. & Ryan, R.M., 2000. Self-Determination Theory and the Facilitation of Intrinsic Motivation, Social Development, and 
Well-Being. American Psychologist, 55(1), pp.68–78.
U
        A bias relating to the theory on this card is the Extrinsic Incentives bias: 
     When people view others as having (situational) extrinsic motivations, but (dispositional) 
intrinsic motivations for oneself
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can the terms intrinsic motivation or extrinsic motivation be 
used to explain your own problem?

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (19 of 21)
Theory of Interpersonal Behaviour
%pplied pVSFleQ case
How can I get fewer people to drive too fast? 
First, start by asking yourself: What root cause do 
you wish to address? Is it that people have good 
intentions of driving safely and obeying the 
speed limits, yet have bad habits of getting late 
out of the door, so they end up driving too fast 
on the way to work? Or is the root cause that 
people intend to drive within the speed limit, 
yet have a habit of getting irritated when cars 
ahead of them drives too slow, and therefore ends 
up speeding and overtaking the car? Analysing the 
problem in this way can help you determine the 
behaviour you want to target.
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can the terms intentions and current habits be used to 
explain your own problem?
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Theory of Interpersonal Behaviour is an all-around theory 
highly applicable for describing several factors influencing behaviour. However, the theory 
relates mainly to problems caused by habitual decision-making. It states that there are two 
routes which influences behaviour: Intentions and habits. Intentions are the deliberate part 
(conscious thinking) of decision-making processes, whereas habits are the non-deliberate part 
(nonconscious thinking). Intentions are created by attitude, social factors and affect whereas 
habits are created by frequency of past behaviour.
Reference: 
Triandis, H.C., 1977. Interpersonal Behavior, Monterey, USA: Brooks/Cole Pub. Co.
Q
        A bias relating to the problem case on this card is the Gambler’s Fallacy bias: 
     The tendency to think that future probabilities are altered by past events, 
when in reality they are unchanged.

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (20 of 21)
Theory of Planned Behaviour
%pplied pVSFleQ case
Why do so many people buy an iPhone?
Try to think like the user: Your attitude thinks it 
makes good sense for you to buy a new smartphone 
and due to the subjective norms, your 
friends will definitely expect you to buy an Apple 
iPhone. Further, your perceived behavioural 
control makes you trust that, you are capable of 
understanding all the new features and using them 
well, since you have had a smartphone before. 
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can your own problem be explained by the terms attitude, 
perceived behavioural control, or subjective norms?
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Theory of Planned Behaviour is a theory applicable when 
trying to explain why individuals behave as they do. The theory states that behaviour can 
be explained by an individuals intention, attitudes (the individual’s belief on how a certain 
behaviour makes a positive or negative contribution to the individual’s life), the subjective 
norms (social network, cultural norms etc.), and perceived behavioural control (how hard/easy 
is it to display certain behaviour). It is a highly valuable when trying to analyse the different 
aspects causing certain behaviours.
Reference: 
Ajzen, I., 1991. The Theory of Planned Behavior. Organizational Behavior and Human Decision Processes, 50(2), pp.179–211.
P
        A bias relating to the problem case on this card is the Choice Supportive bias: 
    The tendency to remember one’s choices as better than they actually were

Appendix 22: Theory Cards (21 of 21)
Theory of Self-efficacy
%pplied pVSFleQ case
Why do some people learn to ski faster than others? 
Imagine that you have two individuals whom 
both must learn how to ski. 
Imagine that they both have the same amount of 
sports talent, they are taught on the same slopes, in 
the same weather – i.e. all conditions are the same. 
Why do one person learn to ski faster than the 
other? 
If one person believes much more in his/her own 
ability to ski than the other, (s)he will probably: 1. 
put a lot of effort into learning how to ski, and 
2. have a more positive attitude towards the 
teaching and thus understand it faster. This is how 
self-efficacy can affect behaviours.
*SVce ySuVselJ XS JSVQulaXe a LypSXLesis 
How can your own problem be explained by increasing a persons 
self-efficacy (confidence in one’s own abilities)#
&VieJ descVipXiSR The Theory of Self-efficacy is valuable when trying to explain 
behaviours relating to personal issues. The theory explains why some people put a lot of 
effort into certain actions, whereas some don’t even try. It states that whether a behaviour 
will be initiated, how much effort will be expended for this and how long it will be sustained, 
behaviour is determined by an individual’s expectations of personal efficacy. Thus the higher 
the self-efficacy, the more an individual believes that they can perform a certain task. This 
theory is therefore great when trying to determine personal factors of behaviour.
Reference: 
Bandura, A., 1977. Self-efficacy: Toward a Unifying Theory of Behavioral Change. Psychological Review, 84(2), pp.191¯215.
N
        A bias relating to the problem case on this card is the Selective Perception bias: 
   The tendency for expectations to affect perception

Appendix 27: Final demonstration of the Super Cool Analysis Tool
Final 
Demonstration
Duration
Aim
Tasks
Results
Participants
2 D&I students
Initial
90 minutes
To view the Super Cool 
Analysis Tool in action 
and see if the require-
ments are fulfilled
Play the Super Cool 
Analysis Tool
Setting
May 22nd,
Copenhagen
Behavioural design 
knowledge
An indication of the 
issues, factors and 
links that need more 
detailed evaluation
As the final activity in the support tool development a final demonstration of the Super Cool Analysis Tool was 
conducted. 
In the final demonstration of the Super Cool Analysis Tool the game was played entirely indenpendently by the 
players, without any interruptions, explanations, or clarifications from the inventors. The results from the final 
demonstration were as follows: 
• The Guide Book was through all game steps open on the “How to prepare and play the game” page 
(page 5). 
• The total time of the game was 1 hour and 22 minutes. The individual Theory Cards took between 
8-12 minutes to go through (as allowed by the hour glass). It was observed that the 10 minute hour 
glass did in fact not last 10 minutes, but differed between lasting five minutes and 20 minutes. Thus, 
the hour glass was in fact not used, yet the players managed to spend approximately 10 minutes per 
Theory Card anyway. 
• The players browsed through a total of four Theory Cards. It was observed that the latter Theory Cards 
were processed faster than the first ones, due to an understanding of the flow and recognition of tasks. 
• The task of creating hypotheses appeared natural and intuitive for the players. Further, it appeared that 
the purpose of formulating hypotheses were well-understood by the players, i.e. they saw the benefit 
that formulating hypotheses brings. 
• One of the players participating in the final demonstration had previously in the support tool 
development been part of one of the workshops in which the basic concept of the Super Cool 
Analysis Tool was tested. An observation from this was that the player could remember one of the 
behavioural theories applied in the workshop that tested the basic concept, indicating that this designer 
had in fact started to build up a theoretical back-catalogue of behavioural theories.

Appendix 30: Survey design
Questions
Rationale
1.
When seeking to describe, analyse, and change 
behaviour, what are the 3-5 main behavioural theories 
that you use? (By use we mean theories actively referred 
to as a part of the behavioural design process)
To get insight in the most applied behavioural theories 
in regards to describing and analysing behaviour 
independently from this thesis categorisation of 
models of behaviour and theories of change.
Behavioural theories
No.
Behavioural Design Challenges
Project Staging
2.
How do you select the behavioural theories mentioned 
in the previous question? 
To get insight in the way the companies select 
theories in their behavioural design projects.
3.
What is the reasoning behind your way of selecting 
theories?
To obtain deeper insight into their reasoning of the 
selection of behavioural theories. 
4.
When a new employee is hired into the company you 
work at, are there any behavioural theories that he/she 
must learn? If yes, what theories?
To get insight into the companies’ expectations of new 
employees initial knowledge.
5.
Have you faced any of the following challenges in your 
behavioural design career? If yes, how often?
To get insight in day-to-day challenges that are 
common for all behavioural design project types.
6.
Based on the challenges mentioned above, which of the 
following features would you find meaningful and 
valuable to be operationalised in a behavioural design 
tool? 
To get insight into what the companies distinguish as 
valueable and meaningful, as well as what they find 
worthless.
7.
Can you please elaborate on your answers for which you 
answered "No, I would not find this meaningful nor 
valuable"?
To understand the rationale of the above asked 
question.
8.
How is your work within the different phases of a 
behavioural design project normally composed? I.e. 
how do you work within the different stages?
To get insight in the context that the tool must be a 
part of.
9.
What design tool/methods do you most often apply in 
the planning phase?
10.
What design tool/methods do you most often apply in 
the discover phase?
11.
What design tool/methods do you most often apply in 
the define phase?
12.
What parts of the behavioural design process do your 
projects most often consist of? 
13.
What is your most important criteria for project success?
To get insight in how project success is defined in 
order to take this into account when developing the 
tool.
14.
On the following scale from "Never" to "Always", what 
would you say is most typical for your behavioural 
design projects? 
To get insight in regards to being able to distinguish 
whether large theory inclusion has an effect on project 
success or not.
